Preface
This thesis is made as a completion of a master’s degree in civil engineering in the
department of civil engineering at the Technical University of Denmark. The work
has been conducted from the 3rd of August 2015 to the 14th of February 2016 and
has an extend of 30 ECTS credits.
The literature which forms the basis for this work, has mainly been obtained from
DTU’s library’s facilities, in terms of book, articles, standards, etc. In addition
has some literature been provided by Christian Ammitzbøll Madsen, in the form of
useful standards, books and not least his own master thesis. The work conducted
in his and his co-writer Daniel Sommerlund Pedersens master thesis, has provided
the basis for the present work and should be seen as a further development thereof.
During my work with the thesis, has I had the opportunity to work together and
gain knowledge from several experts within their own area of expertise. A special
thank should go to my supervisors:

ature and general problem solving.

==PAGE:6==
Abstract
Monopolies have become a widely used foundation for oﬀshore wind turbines due to
their relatively cheap production and installation costs.
Monopolies are fabricated by joining rolled can sections, by means of welding. The
welding process causes thermal loads, which will induce residual stresses in and
around welds - the so called welding induced residual stresses.
These residual stresses decrease the fatigue life of the structure considerably. The
extend of how much the fatigue life is decreased, is governed by the size and mag-
nitude of the residual stresses. The knowledge of the stress distribution in weldings
of thick plates is very limited.
In the present work are the welding induced residual stresses are investigated by
means of ﬁnite element method. A sequential coupled approach is used, where a
separate thermal analysis is coupled with a mechanical analysis. This approach ne-
glects some of the complex mechanisms inherent in the welding process, which has
minor impact on the development of residual stresses.
The used approach is validated against experimental data, in terms of temperature
and residual stress measurements of a welding experiment.
The vast majority of monopolies is produced in steel grade S355 with quality ML.
Mechanical testing of this steel has been conducted in order to obtain material prop-
erties for input in the numerical welding models. The testing includes temperature
elevated monotonic and cyclic test. On the basis of these tests an isotropic and a
kinematic material model has been developed.
Two welding processes have been investigated: submerged arc welding and laser
hybrid welding.
In both cases has there been conducted welding tests, on test
specimens of S355ML, with the outcome of temperature measurements and cross-
sectional data.
The welding tests has been simulated in 2D plain strain with both isotropic and
kinematic material models. The tests should be used to evaluate the generation
of residual stresses for these welding processes.
The performed analysis showed
==PAGE:7==
that residual stresses in the level of 500MPa was present in the weld for both the
submerged arc weld and the laser hybrid weld. This corresponds to stresses within
the plastic regime and above the onset of yielding at 20◦C also know as the yield
strength. Based on the analysis, are there no signs of diﬀerences in the level of the
generated residual stresses between the two welding processes and no immediately
proﬁt of using one welding process over another.
Furthermore, has there been conducted analysis of welds which are fabricated after
the procedure used on weld in monopiles today. Welds with a wall thickness of
30mm and 60mm produced using submerged arc welding has been analyzed with
the developed isotropic material model. The analysis predicted residual stresses at
the level of 400MPa which also is within the plastic regime.
In order to evaluate the validate the obtained result, should the simulations be
compared with measurement of residual stresses in similar welds.
Jessen

Chapter 1
Introduction
Supersized monopiles are used as foundations for wind turbines. The fatigue life
assessment is the governing design criteria of these structures. Due to the fabrication
method, welding induced residual stresses present in the structure, and are known
to have a major impact on the fatigue performance. The level of residual stresses in
welds in supersized monopiles are poorly understood. In this present work are the
level of residual stresses are investigated numerically with the use of ﬁnite element
method. Primarily for investigating the impact of the welding process and material
model.
The following section will give a description of monopiles, current design practice
and optimization possibilities. This will lead to the next section, where the objective
of the thesis is described. The introduction will end in a description of the scope of
work.
1.1
Monopiles - Background and Design Consid-
erations
The demand for clean and renewable energy has increased over the last decades.
Alongside with this trend has the eﬃciency of wind turbines also been increased
considerably. This in combination, has increased the investments in oﬀshore wind
farms, hitting 5 billions Euro’ s in 2014 and lead to the placement of wind farms on
greater water depths [65].
Several types of foundation types exist for wind turbines. The choice of foundation
type mainly relies on water depth. On Figure 1.1 is foundation types illustrated
depending on water depth. In addition exists ﬂoating foundations which presently
are rarely used.
1
==PAGE:13==
1.1. MONOPILES - BACKGROUND AND DESIGN CONSIDERATIONS
Figure 1.1: Typical wind turbine foundation types for deep, intermediate and shal-
low water depth, left to right: jacket, jacket, monopile, gravity, monopile, gravity
[22].
Monopiles is the most popular foundation type, with a market share of 91% of all
newly installed oﬀshore wind turbine foundations in 2014. It is preferred due to
cheap installation and production costs.
The monopile is basically a long hollow pile which is rammed into the seabed. The
pile is constructed of several can sections, usually 3m in length, which are welded
together. On top of the monopile is a transition piece installed. This acts as a
transition between the monopile and the wind turbine tower.
Traditionally, monopiles is used in shallow waters, of approximately 25 meters. How-
ever, the recent development has pushed the installation depth up to 60 meters.
These monopiles are referred to as Supersize Monopiles or XXL monopiles [60].
The increased water depth, in conjugation with increasing wind turbine size, induce
very high bending moments in the structure. This has set very high standards to
the load carrying capacity of the monopile, which is reﬂected in the can diameter
and wall thickness. The maximum outer diameter of monopiles has increased from
approximately 6m in 2007 to an estimated 9m in 2016 while the wall thickness has
increased from 95mm in 2008 to 130mm in 2016.
In excess of self weight, the monopile is subjected to cyclic environmental loads.
These loadings originates from wind, waves and current. Further cyclic contribution
comes from the rotating turbine.
Structures which are subjected to cyclic loadings must be assessed in relation to
fatigue performance. Fatigue is deﬁned as cyclic crack formation at vulnerable loca-
tions on the structure where high stress concentrations exist, typically in the form
of welded joints.
Weldings have an inherent tendency to initiate crack formation due to a number of
2
Jessen
==PAGE:14==
1.2. OBJECTIVE
reasons. One of them, which is of particular interest, is welding induced residual
stresses. Welding induced residual stresses is deﬁned as remaining internal stresses
which is induced by the welding process. The magnitude of the residual stresses
are generally assumed to be at the order of yield strength in tension. The pres-
ence of high tensile residual stresses in combination with cyclic loads, have a higher
tendency of experiencing cyclic loadings which exceed the yield strength and thus
initiate plastic deformations, leading to crack formation. A recent example of a
fatigue failure of a welded joint, is the collapse of a wind turbine tower near Samsø
in Denmark due to crack formation.
The assumption of residual stresses in the order of yield strength is adopted in fa-
tigue guidelines and recommendations for oﬀshore structures (e.g. Eurocode [21],
DNV-GL [9] and IIW [31]). However, they also provide the opportunity to lower
the design requirement, if it can be proven that the residual stresses is lower than
the yield strength, which opens the opportunity for material savings. Even a small
material saving in the order of mm’s can result in considerable saving in a wind
farm project. Whether, residual stresses are lower than yield strength?, that is the
question.
Development within the ﬁeld of numerical simulations, has over the last decades
resulted in the capability to model increasingly advanced processes. One of these
capabilities is the ability to model the welding process by use of ﬁnite element
modeling, also known as computational welding mechanics (CWM). By using CWM
it is possible to predict the highly nonlinear heat ﬂow distribution, which causes
plastic deformations and residual stresses.
However, the welding process is a very complex process which involving many vari-
ables. Theses variables are often speciﬁc to the particular weld procedure, in terms
of type of welding process and heat input and cooling rate.
This requires cali-
bration of the model against experimental data. Another issue in CWM is that it
requires material dependent properties. Especially, is the lack of temperature depen-
dent strength properties a major problem. This is further complicated when facing
multi-pass welding, where the cyclic thermal loading cause cyclic plastic behavior
and the chosen material model should reﬂect this.
1.2
Objective
This present work is q further development of the work done in a previous project.
During this project, they conducted analysis of welds with a thickness of 30mm and
60mm. However, due to lack of material data, was the analysis made with a lower
steel grade than used in the fabrication of monopiles.
The objective of this thesis reﬂects the need for acquiring relevant material prop-
erties, thus the main objective is not in terms of simulating residual stresses in
3
Jessen
==PAGE:15==
1.3. SCOPE OF WORK
monopiles, but more obtaining material properties and evaluating them for use in
CWM.
The overall objective is still to gain knowledge on residual stresses in supersized
monopiles by used of CWM and the secondary objectives of this thesis are as fol-
lows:
• To evaluate the feasibility of using CWM in obtaining residual stresses in
welds.
• Require temperature dependent material properties for steel used in monopiles
for use in CWM.
• Investigate the possibility for optimization with regard to lowering the gener-
ation of residual stresses during the manufacturing process of monopiles.
1.3
Scope of Work
The work done during this thesis consists of three main ﬁelds: a theoretical study
of the problem, experimental test trials and welding simulations. The structure of
the theses is represented in terms of theses three main ﬁelds.
Initially, will the theoretical background of the problem will be elaborated.
First, by giving a general description of the physical aspects, which includes: fatigue
mechanisms focusing on welds, welding processes and the thermal distortion focus-
ing on residual stresses. Secondly, by describing the background for the numerical
simulations, which includes: A general description of aspects in numerical modeling
of welds and a more speciﬁc description of modeling the thermo-mechanical problem
which includes governing equations, boundary conditions, material properties and
heat source modeling. The theoretical background will be ﬁnished with a chapter
which describes the general modeling approach adopted in the numerical analysis.
Next, will the experimental part follow. It consist of two kinds of mechanical testing
of steel S355ML and two welding tests.
The mechanical tests consist of heat elevated monotonic tensile tests and heat ele-
vated cyclic tests. The mechanical test will be followed by the post-processing of the
experimental results, in terms of development of isotropic and kinematic material
models.
The welding tests have been conducted with two welding processes: submerged arc
welding and laser hybrid welding. The data obtained in the tests will be used for
calibration of the thermal analysis.
Last, will the part containing the numerical simulation follow. The ﬁnite element
models will be presented together with the obtained results, in terms of temperature

ﬁeld and residual stresses. Four cases are simulated:
• Validation case
• Experimental submerged arc Weld
• Experimental laser hybrid weld

Chapter 2
Fatigue Mechanism
Fatigue is, with corrosion and wear, one of the main causes of damage in metallic
members. The ﬁrst problems with fatigue emerged under the western industrial-
ization as the use of steel became more common. Since, has the topic has been
researched extensively, which is reﬂected in the amount of literature available.
In this chapter the basic mechanisms behind fatigue are described. The main focus
is on topics related to fatigue of welded structures.
The concept of fatigue crack propagation will be explained, followed by the eﬀect of
residual stresses on fatigue and the design of welds with respect to fatigue.
2.1
Deﬁnition of Fatigue
Fatigue is progressive damage, which can occur in structures that are subjected to
cyclic loadings, e.g. cyclic wind and waves. The damage usually appears in the
form of cracks at vulnerable locations of the structure, such as construction details
from machining, welding or connections. Even if the structural detail is substantial
stronger than the surrounding structure, may it be the critical part of the struc-
ture with respect to fatigue. This leads to high design requirements when designing
structural details in risk of fatigue.
The crack modes of fatigue can be divided into three types as illustrated on Figure
2.1. The predominant cracking mode is mode I, where the crack evolves perpendic-
ular to the main principle stress, also denoted as the tensile mode. The shearing
modes, i.e. mode II and mode III can be present in small cracks, but will quickly
exhibit transition to crack growth in the tensile mode i.e. mode I [59].
6
==PAGE:18==
2.1. DEFINITION OF FATIGUE
Figure 2.1: Crack opening modes; from [59].
The sole reason for why fatigue damage is so harmful and often design decisive, is
that the ﬂuctuating stress necessary to cause failure, can be much less than the yield
strength of the structure.
The process of crack propagation is usually divided into two phases, the crack ini-
tiation phase and the crack growth phase, as illustrated on Figure 2.2. The gray
boxes refers to the factors inﬂuencing the diﬀerent phases.
Figure 2.2: Phases of the fatigue life and relevant factors, from [59].
The stress concentration factor Kt is the important parameter for predictions on
crack initiation and is deﬁned as:
Kt =
σpeak
σnominal
(2.1)
where the σpeak is the peak stress and σnominal is the nominal/average stress over
the cross section. In a butt-weld, is the highest stress concentration factor usually
located at the weld toe, as illustrated on Figure 2.6, thus the crack will initiate at
this location.
Fracture crack propagation is a consequence of cyclic slip in both the initiation and
crack growth phase. The slip implies plastic deformation or dislocation.
The initiation phase covers the crack propagation when the crack is limited to a
microstructure level. In this phase the plastic deformation is limited to a small
7
Jessen
==PAGE:19==
2.1. DEFINITION OF FATIGUE
number of grains of the material. The microplasticity preferably occurs in grains at
the material surface because of lower constraints to neighboring grains, compared
to subsurface grains, thus fatigue in the imitation phase is a surface phenomenon.
As a consequence, the plastic deformation can occur at lower stress levels.
The crack growth phase initiates when the crack no longer is limited to the surface
(i.e.
the surface grain layer is penetrated).
Now the crack growth is no longer
governed by the surface conditions, but by the strength of grain boundary of the
underlying grain layers [59].
On Figure 2.3 a cross section of a micro crack is
illustrated, showing the grain layers.
Figure 2.3: Cross section of micro crack; from [59].
When a structure is evaluated to be subjected to cyclic stresses which will lead to
crack propagation beyond the initiation phase, the term fatigue life is used as a
measure of life expectancy. Fatigue life is deﬁned as the amount of stress cycles, at
a certain stress level, the structure can withstand before failure, usually illustrated
by S-N curves. The fatigue life is governed by four main parameters [51]:
• The stress diﬀerence also known as the stress range
• The structural detail geometry
• The material characteristics
• The environment
The stress range is decisive in whether fatigue will initiate or not as well as on the
fatigue life. In addition, the location of the stress range, tensile or compressive, is
of particular interest. In this case residual stresses play a major role.
In the following section some of the main aspects related to the fatigue life of welds
will brieﬂy be discussed.
8
Jessen
==PAGE:20==
2.2. FATIGUE IN RELATION TO WELDS
2.2
Fatigue in relation to welds
The fatigue life of welded structures is a widely treated and discussed subject, il-
lustrated by the large amount of literature available on the subject. The fatigue
behavior of welded steels diﬀers from that of unwelded material in many aspects.
The most signiﬁcant diﬀerence is that welded structures have considerable worse fa-
tigue performance. The reason for this must be found among several aspects present
in welded structures, namely stress concentrations due to geometric considerations
of the weld (local and global), weld defects and the eﬀect of residual stresses on
stress range [43]. However, even though the stress concentrations and weld defects
play an important role of the fatigue life, it is the eﬀect of residual stress on the
stress range which is the subject of interest in the present work.
2.2.1
Stress Range in Welds
When dealing with fatigue resistance of welded structures, are the residual stresses
of particular interest. The eﬀect of residual stress can be explained by the placement
of the stress range. The stress range is deﬁned as:
Δσ = σmax −σmin
(2.2)
Where σmax is the maximum stress value (with sign) and σmin is the minimum stress
value (with sign).
In order to understand why the residual stresses have a negative eﬀect on fatigue
life, it is necessary to understand the mechanism of crack propagation. As stated
earlier, the conditions of crack propagation are present when the detail is subjected
to tensile stresses in a load cycle. One could think, based on the crack propagation
mechanism, that in the case of compressive load cycle the fatigue life would be in-
creased. This is, however, not the case for structures with residual tensile stresses
(which is the case for most welded structures). The stress ratio, R, deﬁned as the
minimum stress divided by the maximum stress is a convenient measure of how the
mean stress of the load cycle acts on the structure.
9
Jessen
==PAGE:21==
2.2. FATIGUE IN RELATION TO WELDS
Figure 2.4: Eﬀect of mean stress on fatigue strength: (a) zero mean stress (alter-
nating loading), R = -1, (b) zero minimum stress (pulsating loading), R = 0, (c)
tensile minimum stress (wholly tensile loading), R > 1; from [43].
On Figure 2.4 is diﬀerent stress ratios illustrated.
In case (c) where R > 1, it
is observed that during the whole load cycle, the structure is subjected to tensile
stresses. Even if the load cycle is compressive, the load cycle the structure will
experience, is tensile. This implies that the structure experience the sum of the
applied stress, i.e. the residual stresses will ”shift” the stress range.
The stresses a weld will experience during a load cycle, in case of tensile residual
stress in the order of yield strength, is illustrated on Figure 2.5. It is illustrated, that
the weld will experienced stress above the yield strength up to the maximum stress
of the load cycle is reached. This will generate irreversible plastic deformations,
which is the basis for crack propagation and ultimately fatigue failure.
Figure 2.5: Stress at the weld toe (a) before fatigue cycling; (b) at the maximum
stress in the fatigue cycle; and (c) at the minimum stress in the fatigue cycle, from
[43].
2.2.2
Fatigue design of welded joints
The fatigue design of welded joints is traditionally based on experimental trials.
Since the 50s extensive and well documented trials programs has been carried out.
The fatigue life, also known as the service life, is often represented in terms of S-N
10
Jessen
==PAGE:22==
2.2. FATIGUE IN RELATION TO WELDS
curves (stress - number of repetitions). Several approaches are used to form these
S-N curves, among these are the nominal stress and fatigue joint classiﬁcation and
hot spot stress approach [43].
The nominal stress and fatigue joint classiﬁcation are the approach used in many
codes, including Eurocode. It is based on extensive fatigue testing on diﬀerent joint
classiﬁcations i.e. joint geometries.
The ”nominal stress” approach is not appropriate to use in all cases, for instance
on a tubular ”T” nodal joint on a jacket structure. In this type of joint, very local
high stress concentration, or so called hot spots are present. The S-N-curves for
these joint are based, on hot spot stress ranges instead of the nominal stress. This
approach is of particular interest, when evaluating the fatigue life with respect to
known residual stresses. According to [49] may a bonus factor be applied to the
fatigue strength, in the case of post-weld heat-treated or other components known
to contain less severe residual stresses (i.e. lower than yield stress). A typical stress
distribution of a weld, showing the hot spots, is illustrated on Figure 2.6. Where
σln is the nonlinear stress, σm is the membrane stress, σb is the bending stress and
σnlp is the non-linear peak stress.
Figure 2.6: A typical non-linear stress distribution across the plate thickness at a
Type ”a” hot-spot, from [49].
In many cases, including the case of structures subjected to cyclic wave loading, are
the cyclic stress history variable in magnitude. The design procedure for dealing
with these kind of loadings, is the use the concept of equivalent constant amplitude
stress history. This approach breaks down the stress history into a series of constant
amplitude stress cycles with varying stress ranges. The allowable cycles for each
stress range is determined by use of S-N-curves, as shown on Figure 2.7. The fatigue
life is then determined by use of Miner’s rule, expressed as:
n1
N1
+ n2
N2
+ n3
N3
+ . . . =


ni
Ni
≤1
(2.3)
where n is the expected load cycles and N is the allowable N load cycles.

Chapter 3
The Welding Process
The concept of welding basically consists of joining metal together by heating it to
the point of melting. Many complex processes are involved in the creation of the
weld and the physical outcome.
This chapter presents some of the basics involved in the welding process, includ-
ing: diﬀerent welding processes, welding procedure speciﬁcation, weld geometry,
microstructure, welding ﬁller material and weldability.
3.1
Diﬀerent Welding Processes
Generally, the fusion welding process can be divided into three main categories, gas
welding, arc welding and high-energy beam welding. Within these categories many
diﬀerent kinds of welding processes exist, each with diﬀerent properties and intent
of use.
In the present work arc welding and laser beam welding are treated. The arc weld-
ing process is represented in terms of submerged arc welding (SAW) and gas metal
arc welding (MAG) and the laser welding process is performed with the use of a so
called disc laser.
Figure 3.1 shows the main characteristic of the power densities for diﬀerent welding
processes. The arc welding process is a so called surface welding process where the
power density, and thus the penetration depth, is limited to the near surface region.
In contrast is the laser beam welding a deep penetrating welding process with a very
narrow and high power density.
The characteristic of the power density and thus the penetration depth, is visible in
the use and characteristics of the weld. The heat source characteristics are described
13
==PAGE:25==
3.1. DIFFERENT WELDING PROCESSES
in chapter 8.
Another main feature separating SAW and MAG welding from laser beam welding,
is that SAW and MAG welding have the capability of adding ﬁller metal to the base
material. This missing feature can however be overcome for laser beam welding, by
combining the laser beam with an arc welding process such as MAG. The combined
welding process is called Laser-hybrid (LH) welding.
The following sections describe the features of SAW and LH welding.
(a)
(b)
Figure 3.1: Power densities for typical welding heat sources (a), from [57] and ge-
ometry of weld beads obtained at respective densities (b), from [34].
3.1.1
Submerged Arc Welding
The SAW process is an arc welding process, meaning that heat is produced by an
electrical arc which is generated by an electrical current. The arc current [Amp]
is carried by a plasma, which is deﬁned as hot ionized gas. The electrical circuit
consists of a anode and a cathode, where most of the heat conduction ﬂow out of
the negative cathode towards the positive anode [52].
Three diﬀerent polarities can be applied to the welding process, illustrated on Figure
3.2: direct-current electrode negative (a), direct-current electrode positive (b) and
alternating current (c). The penetration depth and weld pool geometry depends on
the applied polarity as shown on the ﬁgure [35].
14
Jessen
==PAGE:26==
3.1. DIFFERENT WELDING PROCESSES
Figure 3.2: Diﬀerent polarities, from [35]
The SAW process and setup are schematically illustrated on Figure 3.3. The SAW
process uses a consumable wire electrode to establish the electrical arc and is usually
used with direct-current electrode positive polarity. The molten metal is shielded by
a molten slag and granular ﬂux powder. Since the weld is protected by the slag
and ﬂux powder, more clean welds are produced. The eﬀect of the weld pool being
submerged, does also cause less heat loss to the surroundings. The granular ﬂux
powder can also be added alloying elements and metal powder, which increases the
deposition rate. SAW is often combined with two or more electrodes in tandem,
which further increase the deposition rate. Because of the capabilities of high de-
position rate, is the SAW process applicable for thick workpieces with relative ﬂat
positions1. However, this high deposition rate also creates a lot of heat, which can
reduce the weld quality and increase thermal distortion [35].
Figure 3.3: Schematic illustration of SAW, from [35]
3.1.2
Laser Hybrid Welding
LH welding is a combination of, in this case, MAG welding and Laser beam welding.
MAG is an arc welding process like SAW. However, instead of being shielded from
1Due to the integrity of the granular ﬂux powder and the relatively low penetration depth.
15
Jessen
==PAGE:27==
3.2. WELDING PROCEDURE SPECIFICATION
hydrogen intrusion by slag, the MAG is shielded by gas, in terms of carbon dioxide
(CO2) and argon (Ar). The LH setup is illustrated on Figure 3.4. The objective
of using laser beam and MAG together, is to achieve the deep penetration from
the laser beam and the adding of ﬁller material from the MAG. This results in the
capability of increasing the welding speed signiﬁcantly and the thermal distortion
is also reduced signiﬁcantly, compared to other conventional welding processes (e.g.
SAW), which in turn reduces production time and costs.
In laser beam welding, the heat is generated from a laser beam, which is focused and
directed to achieve very high power densities. The laser dealt with in the present
work, is a disc laser. The disc laser is a solid state laser that generates the laser
beam by amplifying a laser diode, with the use of an active mirror [34].
The laser beam penetrates the surface of the steel, in a small area, referred to as
the keyhole. The beam is focused slightly below the surface for optimal keyhole
generation. Shielding gas is also used, however not only for shielding but also for
blowing plasma away, which can absorb and scatter the laser beam.
The penetration depth of the laser is governed by the power density, yielding that
stronger laser beam with a narrow focus diameter has deeper penetration.
Figure 3.4: Schematic illustration of laser hybrid welding, from [34]
3.2
Welding Procedure Speciﬁcation
The welding procedure speciﬁcation (WPS) is a document describing the welding
procedure, which provides guidance to the welder for making sure that the welds is
performed in accordance with welding regulations (such as the European standard:
DS/EN ISO 15607 [12]) and with a certain level of quality.
The document contains detailed information on the welding process, base material,
ﬁller material, granular ﬂux powder and welding parameters, such as welding speed,
voltage, current, wire feeding speed etc.
The WPS is accompanied by a welding procedure qualiﬁcation record (WPQR),
which contains information on test welds performed, which has been undergoing
16
Jessen
==PAGE:28==
3.3. WELD GEOMETRY
inspection and testing. This document serves as evidence that the procedure will
produce a quality welding.
The WPS is developed for each material alloy and for each welding type used. The
WPS is developed on the basis of test welds, where the quality of the test weld is
ensured. The qualiﬁcation process involves a preliminary welding procedure speciﬁ-
cation (pWPS), which is made on the basis of experience and welding calculations.
The amount of inspections and tests performed in order to qualify the welding proce-
dure depends on the particular welding process, among these are: visual inspection,
ultrasonic examination, surface crack detection, tensile and bend test, Charpy-V
and hardness test [12].
Researchers within the numerical welding simulation ﬁeld have made successful use
of numerical simulations for WPS optimization. It is forecasted that in the future
will be common that WPS’s will be made in conjugation with numerical simula-
tions. This enables the capability of optimizing the weld with respect to cracking,
controlling deformations and residual stresses [26].
3.3
Weld Geometry
Figure 3.5 deﬁnes the weld zones and weld geometry for a single weld bead. The
weld can be divided into three major zones, the fusion zone (Composite region
and unmixed zone), partially melted zone and the (true) heat aﬀected zone (HAZ),
each with diﬀerent mechanical properties. The mechanical properties depend on
the chemical composition and the microstructure (and to some extend the cyclic
thermal loading).
Figure 3.5: Dentition sketch of weld zones, from [39].
3.3.1
Weld Microstructure
The weld microstructure in the fusion zone and in the partially melted zone is af-
fected by the weld metal composition, the cooling time from 800◦C to 500◦C, the
17
Jessen
==PAGE:29==
3.3. WELD GEOMETRY
weld metal oxygen content and the austenite grain size [39]. Whereas the microstruc-
ture of the HAZ is mainly governed by the peak temperature and cooling rate. The
microstructure, in terms of crystalline structure and grain size, aﬀects the mechan-
ical properties, namely the toughness.
The solidiﬁcation process of steel is shown on a contentious-cooling transformation
diagram in Figure 3.6. Acicular ferrite is desirable, because it increases the tough-
ness and fatigue properties due to ﬁne grained crystalline formation [35].
Figure 3.6: Continuous-cooling transformation diagram for weld metal of low carbon
steel, from [35].
Figure 3.7: Microstructural zones in the heat aﬀected zone after welding of low-alloy
(medium strength) quenched and tempered steel, including peak temperature proﬁle
and iron-carbon diagram, from [39].
In the HAZ, temperatures are too low to cause melting, but high enough to cause

3.3. WELD GEOMETRY
recrystallization. The HAZ microstructure as a function of temperature and iron-
carbon diagram is illustrated on Figure 3.7. The microstructure of the HAZ inﬂuence
the hardness as illustrated on Figure 3.8 (see medium strength steels), where it
also is seen that the heat input plays a major role. In case of severe hardening,
hydrogen cracking can become a problem and are often there special requirements
to the hardness of oﬀshore steel structures. The problem can be treated by applying

3.3.2
Weld Sequence
Depending on the thickness of the weld, can one weld pass be insuﬃcient for obtain-
ing an appropriate weld. The amount of weld passes is prescribed in the WPS. One
single pass is deﬁned as a continuous welding pass along the joint. The material
deposited is referred to as a welding bead. If more passes are needed the weld is
referred to as a multi-pass weld, an example is illustrated on Figure 3.9.
Figure 3.9: Single pass (left) and multi pass butt weld (right), from [52].
Diﬀerent strategies can be applied for building up multi-pass welds, such as backstep
sequence, block sequence, built-up sequence, and cascade sequence. Furthermore, the
19
Jessen
==PAGE:31==
3.4. WELDING FILLER MATERIAL
welding strategy can be chosen in order to minimize thermal distortion [52].
In case of multi-pass welding, the temperature of the weld between each pass/thermal
cycle is important. It is referred to as the interpass temperature in the WPS and is
aﬀecting the microstructure formation as discussed in the previous section.
3.4
Welding Filler Material
There exist three types of fusion zones [39]: autogeneous, homogeneous and hetero-
geneous.
Autogeneous welds refers to welds where no ﬁller material is added. The fusion zone
is formed by melting and resolidiﬁcation of the base metal.
Homogeneous welds refers to weld where the base and the ﬁller material is closely
matched. Homogeneous welds can be applied when special requirements are present,
e.g. heat treatment and corrosion properties.
Heterogeneous welds referee to welds where the base and weld metal composition is
diﬀerent. This is often the case for welds in structural steel, where the base has in-
herent poor weldability. The reason for use of dissimilar composition of ﬁller metal
include strength, weld defect formation (e.g.
porosity), weldability/solidiﬁcation
cracking resistance, heat treatment response, corrosion resistance, ﬁller metal cost
and operating characteristic of the consumable. Dilution eﬀect, deﬁned as change in
composition of ﬁller due to mixing, require some attention in heterogeneous welds.
The properties of the diluted metal can have signiﬁcantly diﬀerent properties from
the ﬁller and base metal.
3.5
Weldability - S355ML
The weldability refers to the ability of a metal to meet technical quantitative re-
quirements when it is used in welded structures, Covering both production defect
and service life performances.
The most famous example of using metal with poor weldability, is the Liberty ship’s
failure under World War II, where a huge amounts of ships did split in half and sank
due to brittle fractures.
Steel S355 with Quality ML (S355ML) is the most frequently used construction
steels within the oﬀshore sector and therefore also one of the treated steels in the
present work. It is estimated that 90% of the structures are constructed in this
material2.
S355ML is a thermomechanical (TM) rolled low-carbon ﬁne-grain steel after Euro-
pean standard DS/EN 10025-4.
2According to information received at Nordic Steel Conference 2015 by Micheal Joachim An-
dreassen
20
Jessen
==PAGE:32==
3.5. WELDABILITY - S355ML
According to steel producer DILLINGER H¨UTTE, possesses S355ML excellent
weldability [37].
S355 has a low carbon content compared to S355NL (Normal-
ized rolled) which lowers the cold cracking tendency and increases the toughness
in the HAZ. The increased performance against cold cracking makes it possible to
reduce the preheating, which leads to cost reduction in terms of reduced fabrication
time and energy savings.

Chapter 4
Thermal Distortion of Welds -
Residual stresses
As a consequence of the considerable input of heat into the weld and the work
piece during welding, volume changes will occur, due to the expansion properties of
steel. These volume changes will inevitably cause distortion and internal stresses
(i.e. residual stresses), as the steel contracts due to cooling.
The following chapter will elaborate on mechanical mechanisms on a macroscopic
level due to the heating process of welding, e.i.
thermal distortion and residual
stresses.
4.1
Temperature Field
The welding process causes a highly nonuniform heating of the parts being joined.
The areas close to the weld are heated up several thousands ◦C, followed by con-
duction of the heat to the bulk of the material and eventually cooled down.
Two examples of such nonuniform heating are illustrated on Figure 4.1. The ﬁgure
illustrates the temperature ﬁeld for two diﬀerent welding speeds and heat input. As
seen in the ﬁgure, there is a very high temperature gradient within a length of a
few cm’s. This temperature gradient is not only caused volume changes between
the weld bead and the work piece, but also causes plastic deformations [35].
The characteristics of the temperature ﬁeld, strongly rely on the heat source, in
terms of welding process and welding properties. Chapter 8 describes the basis for
developing heat source models, based on the welding process, welding properties
and desired temperature ﬁeld.
22
==PAGE:34==
4.2. THERMAL DISTORTION
Figure 4.1: Temperature ﬁeld of moving heat source with welding 2.4mm/s and heat
indput 3200W (a) and welding speed 6.2 mm/s and heat input 5000W (b), from [35].
4.2
Thermal Distortion
As stated, is distortion in welds caused by the expansion and contraction of the weld
metal and adjacent base metal during the heating and cooling cycle of the welding
process.
The weld fusion and works piece metal is joined together at solidiﬁcation, where the
HAZ is close to its maximum expanded form. Upon cooling, the metal contracts
causing distortion and plastic deformations. While the volume changes increase with
rising temperatures, the strength of the metal (e.g. yield strength) and elasticity
drops, which facilitate the plastic deformations.
On ﬁgure 4.2, some of the common deformation modes on a butt-weld are illustrated.
In some industries cause these deformations major production diﬃculties and costs
(e.g. shipbuilding).
Actions can be made in order to minimize the distortion, so called shrinkage control.
Shrinkage control is incorporated into the conﬁguration of the WPS, in terms of
welding sequence, heat input and geometry of weld [35, 45]. Examples of shrinkage
control are illustrated on Figure 4.3, in terms of alternating welding sequence and
groove geometry.
23
Jessen
==PAGE:35==
4.3. RESIDUAL STRESSES
Figure 4.2: Basic types of welding deformation, from [45].
Figure 4.3: Shrinkage control: alternating welding sequence (a), grove geometry (b),
from [35].
4.3
Residual Stresses
As outlined in Chapter 2, the residual stresses caused by welds, have a major eﬀect
on the fatigue life of the welded structure.
Residual stresses are, as the name implies, remaining stresses when the external
load and self load is subtracted, which is why, residual stresses sometimes are called
internal stresses. Residual stress that exists in a body, which has been subjected to
a nonuniform temperature ﬁeld, can also be referred to as thermal stresses.
4.3.1
Order classiﬁcation
The residual stresses can be divided into three classiﬁcations with respect to the
length, which they act over: ﬁrst (σI), second (σII) and third (σIII) order. The
classiﬁcations are illustrated on Figure 4.4.
24
Jessen
==PAGE:36==
4.3. RESIDUAL STRESSES
The residual stresses, which have been referred to so far, is of the ﬁrst order. These
residual stresses act over an macroscopic level and is caused by thermo-mechanical
distortion. The second and third order are acting on a microscopic level, where the
second order is the residual stresses that act between the grains and the third order
is related to the interatomic mechanisms (e.g. dislocations and imperfections within
the single crystals) [30].
Figure 4.4:
Classiﬁcation of ﬁrst, second and third order residual stresses
(σI, σII, σIII), in terms of diﬀerent mechanisms based on length scale, according to
[57], from [30].
From an engineering point of view, the residual stresses of interest are of ﬁrst order,
because only the macroscopic residual stresses are included in the fatigue design
[30]. From this point and on, when referring to residual stresses, only the ﬁrst order
is considered.
4.3.2
Residual stresses in welds
The residual stresses in welds are quite substantial and are in general considered
to be in the magnitude of yield strength of recommendations and standards (e.g.
Eurocode [21], DNV-GL [9] and IIW [31]).
The development of longitudinal residual stress in a weld, can on a simpliﬁed manner
be explained by considering heating and cooling under constraint. Figure 4.5 shows
the principle. The hot weld is applied to the less hot plate, which yields a diﬀerence
in length between the weld and plate (in cold condition) due to thermal expansion
(a) (length measures from cold conditions), The weld and plate are joined, thus are
the length diﬀerence constrained (b), (c) shows the internal forces due to contraction
[43].
25
Jessen
==PAGE:37==
4.3. RESIDUAL STRESSES
Figure 4.5: Schematic diagram of residual stresses resulting from diﬀerential thermal
expansion and contraction between the weld metal and surrounding parent metal,
from [42].
However, this example only explains residual stresses in the case where the whole
weld bead is added at once with uniform temperature over the weld. This is impos-
sible in practice due to the welding process. In reality, the welding process causes a
highly non-uniform temperature ﬁeld over the plate, as described in section 4.1. This
highly nonlinear temperature ﬁeld creates cyclic displacements which are illustrated
on Figure 4.6 for the region near the heat source. The parabola-like dashed curve in-
dicates the local temperature maximum, where local temperatures rise ahead of the
curve and drops behind it. In front of the heat source (1,2) compressional stresses
exist due to the expansion from the rising temperature. When the heat source has
passed, tensional stresses start to emerge as the temperature drops (3,4), (7) shows
the newly deposited ﬁller material. (5,6) illustrates the compressional stress, which
balances the tensional plastic zone. At ﬁrst glance, should point (6) have the same
progress as (5), however the point is illustrated with the annealing eﬀect of high
temperature on steel [57].
Figure 4.6: Plastic zones and local (thermal) stress-strain cycles in quasi-stationary
temperature ﬁeld of moving heat source, from [57].
Figure 4.7 shows the stationary far-ﬁeld temperature and longitudinal residual stress
distribution during welding. Here is (B) and (C) equivalent to (1) and (6) on Figure
26
Jessen
==PAGE:38==
4.3. RESIDUAL STRESSES
4.6, respectively. (A) shows the initial condition of the plate, where no residual
stress is present and (D) shows the ﬁnal stage after cooling.
Figure 4.7: Temperature and longitudinal residual stresses during the welding pro-
cedure of single butt weld, from [42].
The volumetric changes during heating and cooling obliviously work in all three
dimensions. However the residual stresses in the other two principal directions, i.e.
transverse and through-thickness, are known to be of considerably lower magnitude
than the longitudinal, thus longitudinal residual stress is of primary interest (in
a long butt-weld).
The reason for this is mainly due to the strong longitudinal
constraint against thermal expansion from the parent metal, while the transverse
constraint is so low as to be presumed free [45].
In Table 4.1 the maximum value of longitudinal residual stress is compared to yield
limit for diﬀerent metals listed. The table conﬁrms the general assumption, stated
in the beginning of the section, that residual stresses in welds are in the order of the
yield limit.

will not increase signiﬁcantly for welds longer than approximately 500mm, even
though only a piece of the weld of this size needs to be modeled.

In the chapters so far, the physical mechanisms of welds have been elaborated,
including the welding process, thermal distortion and the following residual stresses.
The following chapter presents the basics for performing a numerical simulation of
a welding with respect to obtaining residual stress prediction.

Chapter 5
Numerical Analysis of Welding
Chapter 5 describes some of the most relevant aspects of numerical analysis of weld-
ing. Including the basic concept of describing a physical problem with a numerical
model.
Furthermore, the complexity of the welding simulation and the current
capabilities will be outlined together with methods of simplifying the problem by
neglecting certain aspects of welding mechanics and decoupling.
5.1
Concept of Numerical Modeling
Numerical modeling or computational welding mechanics (CWM), is the discipline
of solving a physical problem numerically by use of a computer model. The com-
puter model consists of algorithms and equations capable of describing the problem.
Numerical modeling is applied in many ﬁelds of study’s, e.g. astrophysics, clima-
tology, chemistry , biology and engineering, etc. It is often used to solve problems
which are too complex for analytical solutions. Numerical solution is, in contrast to
analytical solutions, solved on the basis of a discrete representation of the problem.
This means that the problem is discretized into certain points in time and space
which can be represented by discrete diﬀerence equations, which is suitable for nu-
merical computing. This implies, that in numerical modeling some discretization
error always is present.
The discipline of representing a physical problem by a numerical model involves
some processes which are necessary to perform in order to obtain a representative
model. The processes are illustrated on Figure 5.1. As mentioned, a numerical anal-
ysis consists of a mathematical model which is capable of providing a mathematical
solution to the physical problem. The numerical model needs to go through vali-
dation, qualiﬁcation and veriﬁcation. Validation is the process where the accuracy
of the model is evaluated, in relation to experimental result or analytical solutions.
Veriﬁcation is the process where the ﬁnite element model is evaluated with respect
30
==PAGE:42==
5.2. HISTORY OF WELDING MODELING
to the conceptual model and qualiﬁcation is the same, but between the conceptual
model and the reality [26].
Figure 5.1: Process involved in obtaining a numerical solution (ﬁnite element solu-
tion in this case), from [26].
When the model has gone through this validation, qualiﬁcation and veriﬁcation -
process, it is valid for use of predictions. Predictions is the simulation of a speciﬁc
case, diﬀerent from the validated case.
Usually the model, in terms of parameters needs to be calibrated in order to match
with some measurements. The same measurements cannot be used for both cali-
bration and veriﬁcation due to requirements of no dependency. This leads to two of
the main diﬃculties in CWM - the material model and the heat input model both
needs calibration and veriﬁcation which often is quite comprehensive [26].
5.2
History of Welding Modeling
The development of arc welding began in the late 1800s, shortly after the electrical
power became available.
Due to failure of welded bridges in Europe in the 1930s and the American liberty
ships during world war II, attention were drawn towards the subject and research
was initiated.
The steady state heat transfer analysis of Rosenthal was developed in the 1930s, but
the ﬁrst attempts to analyze welds by numerical methods (ﬁnite diﬀerence and ﬁnite
element) began in the 1960s as a consequence of the growing computer capacity [3].
In the recent decades the use of ﬁnite element analysis (FEA) has been a widely
used approach.
Lindgren [25] illustrates the development in CWM by Figure 5.2, which shows the
increase in the size of the computational models in welding simulation during the
last decades.
31
Jessen
==PAGE:43==
5.3. THE COUPLED PROBLEM
Figure 5.2: Size of computational models of welding measured by degree of freedom
multiplied by the number of time steps versus year of publication of work, from [25].
In the 2000s 3D model started to appear. Most of the development had been driven
by industries with high safety hazards, such as the nuclear industry and aerospace.
Resent development within commercial software codes, in terms of more support for
CWM and further increase in computational power has made CWM more feasible,
namely in the production industry.
This has lead to the need for the development of code of practices for CWM, in
terms of methodology and validation procedures [26]. In 2013 released AWS a guide
which has the purpose of fulﬁlling this need: AWS A9.5:2013 - Guide for Veriﬁcation
and Validation in Computation Weld Mechanics. However, this guide has not been
available in the present work.
The most recent development within the ﬁeld, has been the coupling of computa-
tional ﬂuid dynamic (CFD) analysis with FE-analysis, such as performed in [64].
This allows a more precise simulation of the weld pool dynamics and as well of the
temperature distribution.
5.3
The Coupled Problem
The process of welding involves many physical factors, which all play a part in
forming the outcome of the weld.
These factors make it diﬃcult to perform a
complete numerical simulation of welding, where all aspects are accounted for. On
a Macroscopic level a weld can be considered as a thermo-mechanical problem,
consisting of calculating transient temperature, displacement, stress and strain. At
the microscopic level, the problem consists of phase transformation, including grain
growth, dissolution and precipitation [3].
The mechanical behavior of the weld, rely on the interaction of the physical factors
32
Jessen
==PAGE:44==
5.3. THE COUPLED PROBLEM
on both levels. The interaction is described by couplings which are illustrated on
Figure1 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Couplings in thermo-mechanical analysis of welding, from [3].
1. Transformation Rates (microstructure evolution depends on temperature).
2. Latent heats (each phase transformation can have an associated latent heat).
They act as a heat sink on heating and as a heat source on cooling.
3. Phase Transformations (volume changes due to phase changes, plastic and
elastic material behavior depend on the microstructure of the material).
4. Transformation Rates (microstructure evolution can particularly martensitic
and bainitic transformations, depend on mechanical deformation).
5. Thermal Expansion (mechanical deformations depend on temperature).
6. Plastic Work (mechanical deformation generates heat in the material and
changes the thermal boundary conditions). In most welding processes this
eﬀect is very small.
The coupling does not have equal importance on the behavior of the weld. The
primary couplings are marked with bold and the secondary couplings are marked
with dashed lines. Coupling #4 and #6 are usually considered negligible [26].
The objective of the simulation is decisive in the way the problem should be modeled
and thereby also the coupling approach. If the objective is to determine the mechan-
ical eﬀects of welding, namely the residual stresses and distortion, it is usually only
to consider the thermal and mechanical coupling [3]. Due to the negligible eﬀects
of plastic work on thermal heat generation, compared to the heat generated by the
welding arc, this coupling is usually only considered on-way (coupling #5). The
one-way approach allows for decoupling of the problem, thus the problem can be
1It must be noted that the ﬂuid ﬂow is not included in the ﬁgure. It is obvious that when the
weld material becomes liquid some ﬂow will exist. However, this ﬂow is considered negligible to
the deformation and stress ﬁeld in the ﬁnal weld. If changes of the weld geometry are of primary
interest, the ﬂuid ﬂow should be included.
33
Jessen
==PAGE:45==
5.4. FINITE ELEMENT METHOD
solved independently. This kind of analysis is usually termed as a sequential coupled
thermo-mechanical analysis, which is the approach used in the present work.
5.4
Finite Element Method
Finite element method (FEM) is a widely used and well documented numerical
method used within engineering problems, such as thermal static and dynamic.
The name, ”ﬁnite elements method” refers to, a subdivision of a structure into a
ﬁnite number of elements, which combined forms a mesh. Each element is repre-
sented by some simple element equations. The element equations are based on the
particular element geometry, and is very decisive for the element properties, see
section 9.2.
The whole structure is represented by a system of these element equations. The
problem is solved by solving the system of equations with respect to the boundary
and initial conditions.
Several software producers oﬀer commercial FEM software. In the present work the
commercial software Abaqus FEA has been used together with the semi-commercial
FEAT-WMT, which has been used for heat source parameters estimation (see section
F).
5.4.1
Abaqus FEA and Abaqus welding interface
Abaqus FAE is a software suite for ﬁnite element analysis and computer-aided en-
gineering. The Abaqus product suite consists of ﬁve core products, whereof the
Abaqus CAE is the product used in the present work.
Abaqus CAE, is a Complete Abaqus Environment which is capable of pre-processing
(modeling), processing and post-processing the results.
The processing procedure is performed by submission of the job input ﬁle (.inp) to
the Abaqus solver, creating a visual output ﬁle (.odb) which contains the obtained
results.
One of the most basic features of Abaqus CAE is the concepts of dividing the
problem into steps. Several types of steps are available depending on the analysis
procedure needed, e.g. static, heat transfer, mass diﬀusion, dynamic or geostatic.
The problem can conveniently be subdivided into diﬀerent steps, when the state of
the model needs to be changed, e.g. change of boundary conditions, loading etc.
In non-linear problems, where the problem cannot be solved by solving a single sys-
tem of linear equation, the solution is found by specifying the loading as a function
of time. The steps are then divided into several increments, which is chosen appro-
priately with respect to the non-linearity of the problem.
34
Jessen
==PAGE:46==
5.5. ACCURACY CLASSIFICATION
As standard, Abaqus is using Newton’s method to iteratively solve the non-linear
problem, which usually require several iterations to ﬁnd a solution to the time in-
crement.
Abaqus welding interface
Abaqus welding interface (AWI) is a plug-in tool for Abaqus CAE. AWI provides
an interface which is capable of constructing a welding problem using a prescribed
temperature approach. AWI constructs the welding problem by supplying a step-
by-step procedure that creates the steps, interactions, thermal boundary conditions
and predeﬁned ﬁelds that are necessary for the analysis. Without the use of AWI
this process is quite comprehensive and monotone. AWI also uses the mentioned se-
quential coupled thermo-mechanical analysis method to solve the thermo-mechanical
problem. After constructing the thermal and mechanical model in AWI, the con-
structed model is available in Abaqus CAE for job submission or further modeling.
AWI supports both 2D and 3D welding simulations.
5.5
Accuracy Classiﬁcation
The accuracy with which the numerical model should be modeled with, does strongly
rely on the scope and aﬀects all the factors of the model, from choice of overall
thermo-mechanical model to accuracy of material model.
The motivation for deﬁning accuracy classiﬁcations is to quantify the needed accu-
racy of the model so the modeling approach is neither over complicated nor under
complicated.
The accuracy classiﬁcation is by [25], divided into ﬁve diﬀerent category’s: reduced,
basic, standard, accurate and very accurate. The scope and ﬂexibility related to
each category are outlined in Table 5.1. The ﬂexibility refers to the level each bead
inﬂuence the deformations of the structure, where one-pass thin plate welds have a
high level of ﬂexibility and multi-pass welds of thick plates are more rigid.

On the basis of Table 5.1 and the fact that the scope is to predict macroscopic
residual stresses, it is concluded that the suﬃcient accuracy class is 1/2 i.e. basic
simulation accuracy /standard simulation accuracy. The chosen accuracy level is
decisive in the modeling approach with respect to heat input model, material model,
geometry and discretization which will be described in the following sections and
taken into use in the following chapters.
5.5.1
Heat Input Model
The overall deformation is not particularly sensitive to the detailed heat distribu-
tion, more the overall heat input in the HAZ, whereas the microstructure has greater
sensitivity.
The proposed heat source model based on accuracy category is given in Table 5.2.
The constant ﬂux model is often referred to as prescribed temperature[25].

5.5.2
Material Model
The accuracy level of the material model is based on cut-oﬀtemperature Tcut. Above
this temperature, no further changes in material properties are accounted for in the
mechanical analysis. The cutoﬀtemperature dependency on the accuracy category
is given in Table 5.3.
Table 5.3: Accuracy categories and corresponding material models, based on [25].
1
Basic simulation
Tcut ≥0.5Tmelt2
2-3
Standard simulation and accurate
Tcut ≥0.7Tmelt2
4
Very accurate simulation
Tcut ≥Tmelt
The basic simulation ignores the volume changes due to phase changes, but includes
their eﬀect on ﬁnal obtained yield limit at cooled conditions. Temperature depen-
dent properties and latent heat due to solidiﬁcation are used.
The standard simulations include the same dependencies, however the eﬀect of phase
changes in volume and material properties are included. The very accurate simula-
tions include rate dependency or even ﬂuid ﬂow [25].
From the above explanation, the material accuracy level can be related to the dif-
ferent strain contribution to the total stress level:

The geometry and discretization of the FE model depend very much on the scope.

In the present work, both 2D-X and 3D-solid models are used.

Chapter 6
Thermo-Mechanical Analysis of
Welding
As stated in the previous chapter, the approach named sequential coupled thermo-
mechanical analysis will be applied in the present work. This approach consists of
two separate analysis, a thermal analysis, which ”feeds” a mechanical analysis with
nodal temperatures - thereof the term ”sequential coupled” arises.
As the thermal analysis provides the time-temperature at the nodal points to the
mechanical analysis, this analysis is carried out ﬁrst. Two main approaches have
been applied in this present work in order to perform the nonlinear heat transfer
analysis, the prescribed weld temperature and the moving heat source, which will be
elaborated in chapter 8.
Subsequently to the thermal analysis the non-linear mechanical analysis is performed
based only on the thermal loads.
In this chapter the theoretical background for the thermo-mechanical analysis will
be presented, including:
• The governing equations of the FE formulation of the thermal and mechanical
analysis
• The boundary conditions (BC) in terms of thermal- and mechanical BC’s
• The material modeling
• Element properties
The chapter should provide the reader with basic knowledge of the technological
background, material properties and assumptions made, which makes it possible to
perform CWM.
The mathematical background, in terms of governing equations will simply be pre-
sented and brieﬂy explained, as the validity is assumed due to reliable sources of
39
==PAGE:51==
6.1. GOVERNING EQUATIONS
literature [3, 57, 63, 38].
6.1
Governing Equations
The governing equations are the basis for the mathematical model which describes
the physical problem.
In the coming section, will the governing equations for the thermal and the mechan-
ical analysis be brieﬂy described, follow by a brief description of the ﬁnite element
formulation of the same.
6.1.1
Thermal Analysis
The thermal analysis consists of a nonlinear heat transfer analysis. The general
assumption is that heat is conducted in isotropic homogeneous material by Fourier’s
Law [3]:
q = −λ∇T
(6.1)
where q is the local heat ﬂux density [W/m2], λ is the materials thermal conductiv-
ity [W/m/◦C] and ∇T is the temperature gradient.
Together with the principle of conservation of energy also know as the ﬁrst low of
thermodynamics, the general heat conduction equation takes the form:
ρ ˙H = ˙Qv −∇q
(6.2)
where ρ [kg/m3] is the density of the material, ˙H [J/kg] is the volumetric enthalpy,
which describes the thermodynamic potential, ˙Qv [W/m3] is the volumetric heat
input, and ∇q is the diﬀusive heat ﬂow.
By rewriting the general heat conduction equation, and introducing the underlying
fundamentals of the enthalpy, the governing equation of heat conduction, on tensor
notation becomes [63]:
ρcp ˙T = ˙Qv + ∇(λ∇T)
(6.3)
where cp [J/kg/◦C] is the speciﬁc heat capacity. In Cartesian coordinates is the heat
transfer equation (equation (6.3)) given by:

(6.4)

conditions. The initial condition is the temperature distribution at the time = 0
and the boundary conditions are the heat dissipation on the boundary surface (i.e.

6.1.2
Mechanical Analysis
The mechanical model is based on the three governing partial diﬀerential equation
of force equilibrium, which is in tensor notation written as:
σij,i + pj = 0
(6.6)
where pj is the body force at any point within the volume and σij,i is the Cauchy
stress tensor.
Finite element formulations uses the principle of virtual work. The force equilibrium
equation can then be expressed in the weak form:
	
V
σijδεdV
	
V
piδuidV +
	
S
TiδuidS
(6.7)
where δε is the virtual strain (or deformation), δu is a virtual displacement ﬁeld and
T is the surface traction at any point on the surface S. The virtual work is deﬁned
as, the work done by an external force on a virtual displacement ﬁeld, is equal to
the work done by equilibrium stresses from the same displacement ﬁeld. Within
applications where small deformations can be assumed, such as the case for welding
deformations, the small deformation theory can be applied.
In case of small deformation theory the total Cauchy stress tensor, which is de-
rived from the displacement ﬁeld, an additive decomposition of strain part. In case
of welding mechanics, the total stress tensor can be divided into decompositions
cased by elasticity (e), plasticity (p), temperature (th), creep (cr), transformation
plasticity (tp) and viscoelasticity (vp) [38]:
εtotal

(6.8)

evaluated in close relation to the purpose of the simulation. As stated previously,

ij
(6.9)
The elastic strain is governed by Hook’s law, which is subdivided into a deviatoric
and dilatoric (volumetric) portion [57]:
εd,ij = 1
2Gσd,ij
(6.10)
εv,ij =
1
3K σv,ij
(6.11)
where the shear modulus G and the compression modulus K is a function of the
modulus of elasticity E and Poisson’s ratio ν.
The plastic strains are governed by a yield condition, yield law and hardening law.
The yield condition designates the initiation of yield. The yield law correlates the
plastic strain increments with the momentary stress state and with the stress incre-
ment. The hardening law dictates the yield limit is changed with plastic deforma-
tions.
The yield condition is usually given by Von Mises yield criterion, which can be
expressed in terms of a yield function:
f = σ2
e −σ2
Y = 0
(6.12)
Where σ2
Y is the yield stress of the material.
The yield law (or ﬂow rule) states that the plastic strain increment occurs normal
to the yield surface, hence the strain increment and the deviatoric stress tensor have
the same principal direction.
dεp,ij = dλσd,ij
(6.13)
where dλ is the plastic multiplier. The plastic multiplier represents the resistance of
the inelastic state to plastic ﬂow, e.g. via work-hardening or damage [27].
It is clear from the yield function that as the material yield the yield stress must
increase. The increase is also known as the work-hardening, which is dictated by
the hardening law, here represented by the hardening modulus H:
H = dσY
dε∗
p
(6.14)
where dε∗
p is the eﬀective plastic strain increment.
The work-hardening can be described by diﬀerent constitutive models, e.g. isotropic
and kinematic, dependent on the plasticity phenomenon the material is assumed to
experience. This subject, i.e the modeling of plasticity, is treated in Chapter 7.
42
Jessen
==PAGE:54==
6.2. FINITE ELEMENT INTERPRETATION
The thermal strains εT is given by the dilatoric or the volumetric strain compo-
nent:
dεTii = 3dεT = 3αdT
(6.15)
where α is the thermal expansion coeﬃcient, which is a function of temperature.
The material properties, which has been referred to in this section and forms the
basis of the thermal and mechanical analysis, e.g. speciﬁc heat capacity cp (function
of density ρ), thermal conductivity λ, Young’s modulus E, Yield stress, Poisson’s
ratio ν, thermal expansion coeﬃcient α and plasticity will be elaborated in section
6.4.
6.2
Finite Element Interpretation
The ﬁnite element principle builds on the idea of discretization of the problem.
This is obtained by the principle of virtual work, where the governing equations
of the physical problem can be expressed in a weak form for a speciﬁc domain in
therms of ﬁnite element contributions. The problem is solved by obtaining nodal
values (temperatures/displacements) which are point wise interpolated, by use of
the shape functions of the elements.
6.2.1
Thermal Formulation
The thermal ﬁnite element formulation is based on the principles of virtual temper-
atures, this enables the capability of discretization of the heat ﬂux in terms of nodal
temperatures [7]. The overall balance of heat ﬂuxes is obtained by assembling the
element integrals of the temperature distribution by summation. The temperature
gradient in the element is given by:
{T ′} ≡[Bth]{T}
(6.16)
where [Bth] is the temperature gradient interpolation matrix, obtained from dif-
ferentiation of the shape functions. As stated previously, the transfer of heat is
determined by Fourier’s law. Fourier’s law, is represented in the form of the consti-
tutive conductivity matrix [KT] which relates the ﬂux to the temperatures.
By obtaining the weak form of the heat transfer equation (equation (6.5)), where
the energy balance is obtained by integration over the volume and surface, and
using the information from the temperature gradient interpolation matrix and the
constitutive conductivity matrix, the general ﬁnite element system equation can
be obtained. The element contribution is summarized in the system equilibrium
equations [7].
The general thermal ﬁnite element system equation has the form:
[Chc]{ ˙T} + [KT]{T} = {Rq} + {Rr}
(6.17)
43
Jessen
==PAGE:55==
6.3. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS
where [Chc] is the speciﬁc heat convection matrix, {Rq} is the element surface ﬂux
and {Rr} is the heat ﬂux over the volumes. The equilibrium is obtained by inter-
polation and appropriate integration over the elements volumes and surfaces.
6.2.2
Mechanical Formulation
The mechanical ﬁnite element formulation is based on the principal of virtual dis-
placements, which enable the capability of discretization. The nodal displacements
are found from point wise interpolation of the displacement, by use of the shape func-
tions of the element. On matrix form, the relation between strains and displacement
is given by:
{εtotal} ≡[B]u
(6.18)
where [B] is the strain-displacement matrix.
It is obtained by diﬀerentiation of
the shape functions of the element. The stress is related to the strain through the
constitutive matrix, which contains the constitutive law, describing the material
behavior:
{σ} = [C]{εtotal}
(6.19)
where [C] is the constitutive matrix.
The principle of virtual displacement is used therefore, the weak form of the equilib-
rium equation can be applied (equation (6.7)). As in the thermal formulation, is the
equilibrium obtained by integration over volumes and surfaces of the element. The
element contributions to the system, is expressed as nodal forces, which is obtained
for all the nodal point displacements [7].
The general ﬁnite element equation of the system can then be expressed by equilib-
rium between displacement and nodal forces.
[K]{u} = {Rb} + {Rs} + {Rc}
,
[K] =
	
V
[B]T[C][B]dv
(6.20)
where [K] is the stiﬀness matrix, {Rb} is the body force over the volumes, {Rs} is
the surface forces and {Rc} is the concentrated nodal force.
6.3
Boundary Conditions
In this section, the thermal and mechanical Boundary conditions (BC’s), used in
the analysis will be described.
6.3.1
Thermal Boundary Conditions
The thermal BC’s describe the heat exchange at the boundary of the body. Two
kinds of heat exchange are present at the boundary: convection and radiation.
44
Jessen
==PAGE:56==
6.3. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS
Convection
Convective heat transfer q [W] is the heat transfer between a surface and a moving
ﬂuid for a certain temperature diﬀerence.
q = hcAΔT
(6.21)
It is governed by the Film coeﬃcient or also known as the convective heat transfer
coeﬃcient hc [W/(m2◦C)] which is the rate of convective heat transferred.
The convective heat transfer is divided into two forms of convection: forced con-
vection and natural convection.
Forced convection occurs when the ﬂuid ﬂow is
induced by an external force, such as a fan or a pump. Natural convection is caused
by buoyancy forces due to density diﬀerences caused by temperature variations in
the ﬂuid ﬁeld. In this particular case where the welds are performed indoor, with
low ﬂuid velocities and high temperature diﬀerences in the near ﬁeld of the welds,
the convection is governed by natural convection.
According to [15, page. 815] the ﬁlm coeﬃcient for natural convection can be ex-
pressed as a function of temperature by the following simpliﬁed expressions, when
assuming laminar ﬂow condition:
hc,top = 1.32
ΔT
L
0.25
(6.22)
hc,bottom = 0.59
ΔT
L
0.25
(6.23)
Where hc,top and hc,bottom is a hot surface facing up and a hot surface facing down,
respectively. ΔT [◦C] is the diﬀerence between the temperature of the solid and the
temperature of the liquid and L [m] is the length. The applied ﬁlm coeﬃcient in
the thermal simulations is shown on Figure 6.1.

6.3. BOUNDARY CONDITIONS
Radiation
The surface loss due to radiation refers to the transfer of heat energy due to electro-
magnetic wave propagation. According to Stefan Boltzmann’s law
is the radiative
heat loss given by:
q = εσ((T 4 −Tz) −(T 4
0 −Tz))
(6.24)
Where ε is the emissivity constant for a certain surface, σ is the Stefan Boltzmann’s
constant equal to 5.67 × 10−8 [W/m2/oK4] which is the proportionality factor de-
ﬁned relative to a perfect black body and Tz is the absolute zero for the actual
temperature scale used, in this case −273.15◦C.
The emissivity constant depends of the surface ability to emit energy as thermal
radiation. This ability is governed by the glossiness of the surface or the lack of it.
It varies from 0 to 1 where glossy surfaces have low emissivity, hence low radiation
and visa versa for darker and rougher surfaces.
A study of the emissivity constant of carbon steel as a function of temperature,
found the relation illustrated on Figure 6.2. From the ﬁgure is it apparent that
the emissivity increases in the interval 300◦C −500◦C, from 0.2 to approximately
0.7, thus is it obvious that the emissivity of carbon steel is temperature-dependent.
However, implementing this into an heat transfer analysis, as cavity radiation, is
very computational expensive and preferable avoided.
Studies have shown that
implementation of temperature dependent emissivity instead of an reasonable chosen
single value, have only little eﬀect on the results in this kind of thermal analysis [30].
Therefore, single value emissivity is applied. According to DS/EN 1993-1-2 [19], the
emissivity can be selected to 0.7 for carbon steel.
Figure 6.2: Emissivity of carbon steel as a function of temperature [53].

6.4. MATERIAL MODELING
6.3.2
Mechanical Boundary Conditions
The mechanical BC’s serve as the mechanical constraint of the FE model on the
surface boundary.
Mechanical BC’s exists generally in two forms: prescribed displacement (includes
rotation and torsion) or prescribed traction, in terms of loads.
In this present work, only prescribed displacement will by applied and in the form
of a prescribed displacement of zero.
6.4
Material Modeling
The following sections presents the material properties which are used in the thermo-
mechanical analysis.
Most of these characteristics are not appreciably diﬀerent, independently of steel
quality (carbon steel).
However, when it comes to strength properties, such as
modulus of elasticity, yield stress and plasticity, is a dependency of the steel grade
present.
The mechanical properties dependency on steel grade and quality makes it relatively
hard to obtain data on these properties, simply because experimental testing have
not been conducted on the considered steel.
As a consequence of the lack of material data on S355ML, experimental testing
has been conducted in this work, with respect to obtaining Young’s modulus, yield
strength and plasticity properties for elevated temperatures.
6.4.1
Thermal Properties
The thermal properties are the properties which are used in the thermal analysis,
in addition to the thermal boundary conditions, namely the parameters involved in
the heat conduction formulation (equation (6.3)):
• Speciﬁc heat capacity, cp
• Density, ρ
• Thermal conductivity, λ
These parameters are, in addition to being material speciﬁc, also temperature de-
pendent. In the following, the thermal input parameters which are applied in the
thermal analysis, will be presented.

The speciﬁc heat capacity is a measure for how much energy need to be applied or
removed, in order to result in a temperature change for a speciﬁc mass. The speciﬁc

When the steel liquidates, the ﬂow conditions within the weld pool, aﬀect the ther-

rates. This can not be modelled in this type of thermal analysis. However, this
eﬀect can be accounted for by multiplying the thermal conductivity by a factor of
three [30]. This is illustrated by the rapid increase in conductivity in the interval
1400◦C −2000◦C.
6.4.2
Mechanical Properties
The essential factors for prediction residual stresses is the temperature history and
the mechanical properties. The mechanical properties is not only temperature de-
pendent as the thermal properties, but also dependent on the thermal-history, in
terms of annealing and cycling thermal loading.

describing the mechanical behavior of steel at elevated temperatures. It is deﬁned
as the temperature where the strength properties are very low and the deforma-
tions are dominated by plastic deformations. For steel, is the mechanical melting
point located at 600◦C−700◦C [45], which will be illustrated in the following section.

Poisson’s Ratio
Poisson’s ratio describes the relationship between the axial strain and the negative
transverse axial strain, when the material is tensioned or compressed in one direction.
The value of Poisson’s ratio is the fraction of the expansion or compression of the
loaded side, in the two other principal directions. The Poisson’s ratio of steel is given
in Figure 6.9. It is seen that Poisson’s ratio increases from 0.3 at room temperature
to 0.5 at 1500◦C. This increase is explained by the fact that when the steel melts, it
acts as a liquid and therefore, obtains the value of 0.5 due to the incompressibility
of ﬂuid. However, there is no reason to believe that Poisson’s ratio should be 0.5
just below the melting point, but in general is it considered that the inﬂuence of
Poisson’s ratio have small eﬀect [25].

Plasticity

6.4.3
Melting point/Annealing Temperature
The melting point of steel or also known as the liquefaction point is the point where
the metal becomes liquid. When this point is reached the steel possesses no ability
to obtain stresses (except some neglective small viscous forces). This temperature is
used in the mechanical analysis to deﬁne an annealing temperature where all stress
in the material is canceled when this temperature is reached.
Cold-working eﬀects, such as work-hardening, the Bauschinger eﬀect, and induced
anisotropy, can largely be removed by performing annealing [41].

calculated to 1500.1◦C and 1507.3◦C, respectively [40]. The chemical composition
of S355ML can be found in Appendix A.
