Chapter 7
Modeling Plasticity
When modeling FE problems which involves plasticity the analysis change charac-
ter of being a linear problem to a nonlinear problem and the process of modeling
complicates rapidly.
When modeling the stress-strain behavior of metals involving plastic behavior, it is
essential to choose a model that represents the behavior of the material for the ana-
lyzed problem, this is also known as phenomenological modeling [28]. When dealing
with plasticity it is usual to distinguish between rate-dependent and rate-independent
material behavior. In this work only rate-independent behavior is treated. rate-
dependent inelastic behavior is covered by the viscoplastic theory.
Within this ﬁeld of rate-independent plasticity, a distinction is made between mono-
tonic plasticity and cyclic plasticity. Monotonic plasticity is fairly simple compared
to cyclic plasticity. Monotonic plasticity models can be applied where loading is
relatively monotonic and creep eﬀects are unimportant [1, ch. 12.8.2].
In case of cyclic loading of a material, which is the case of multi-pass welding, a
number of phenomena comes into action, which also is reﬂected in the number of
developed material models for cyclic plasticity. Such phenomena as cyclic harden-
ing/softening and cyclic creep (also called ratcheting) will be brieﬂy described in
the following sections. Followed, by some outlining of the basic theory behind the
constitutive models, developed in order to predict plastic deformations and in par-
ticular the cyclic behavior.
Later in Chapter 10, material properties will be presented, in terms of both elastic
and plastic. They are developed from experimental testing of steel S355ML, con-
ducted in connection with the present work. Both monotonic and cyclic testing has
been conducted, which respectively will form the basis of a isotropic and a kinematic
material model for use in CWM.
55
==PAGE:67==
7.1. MATERIAL RESPONSE OF CYCLIC LOADING
7.1
Material Response of Cyclic Loading
The real material response of steel under cyclic loading is well documented through-
out decades of experimental research. The most relevant phenomenon is outlined in
the following sections.
7.1.1
Bauschinger’s Eﬀect
The Bauschinger’s eﬀect is one of the most basic phenomena within cyclic loading
of steel. Bauschinger’s eﬀect cause early yielding of the material on strain reversal.
The eﬀect is illustrated on Figure 7.1. The ﬁgure shows a stress strain response of
a uniaxial cyclic loading with full stress reversal (R=1) against a normal monotonic
stress strain response. As illustrated in the ﬁgure, the material under cyclic load-
ing exhibit early yielding due to Bauschinger’s eﬀect [28]. This is caused by the
dislocation structure in the cold worked metal, generally polycrystalline metals. As
deformation occurs, accumulation of dislocation at barriers will pile up and form
stable dislocation cells, upon reversal the back-stresses at the barriers are weaker
than upon the initial state, hence resulting in a lower yielding point.
Figure 7.1: Bauschinger’s eﬀect, from [58]
7.1.2
Cyclic Hardening/Softening
The cyclic hardening/softening of a material relates to softening/hardening response
corresponding to decreasing/increasing resistance against deformation under cyclic
loading. The intensity of this eﬀect is often decreasing with the number of cycles
applied, which means that the material can reach a saturated state. The soften-
ing/hardening eﬀects can be characterized by closed hysteresis loop, as illustrated
on Figure 7.2. The ﬁgure illustrates that for cyclic loading, within a closed strain
range, the material response (stress) decreases or increases for cyclic softening or
56
Jessen
==PAGE:68==
7.1. MATERIAL RESPONSE OF CYCLIC LOADING
cyclic hardening material, respectively.
This eﬀect is most signiﬁcantly seen in stainless steel and copper, whereas structural
steels have less tendency to show this response. The modeling of this type of behav-
ior does, not only relate to the loading amplitude but also the loading history. Such
transient nature makes these problems very diﬃcult to model accurately.
Figure 7.2: Cyclic softening (a) and cyclic hardening (b), from [28].
7.1.3
Ratcheting
Under uniaxial low cycle load controlled test with non-zero mean stress, the hys-
teresis loop can have an open path. This phenomenon is called ratcheting or cyclic
creep. The accumulation of axial plastic strain occurs cycle by cycle and the result
is a nonlinear behavior of the material in compression and tension [28]. The phe-
nomenon is illustrated on Figure 7.3, where σm is the mean stress. The ratcheting
behavior is correlated to the dislocation substructure of the material that forms
during cyclic loading. As a consequence of the asymmetric nature of the loading,
only some of the dislocations gets annihilated during load reversal and thus the ac-
cumulation of plastic strain in one of the principal directions will occur. The rate
of ratcheting/accumulation of plastic strain will decrease with the number of cycles
reached, this behavior is called saturation of ratcheting strain [14].
Figure 7.3: Uniaxial ratcheting behavior and inﬂuence of hardening/softening be-
havior, from [28].
57
Jessen
==PAGE:69==
7.2. CONSTITUTIVE MODELS OF CYCLIC PLASTICITY
7.2
Constitutive Models of Cyclic Plasticity
The cyclic plasticity models can basically be divided into the following groups [28,
ch. 15]:
• Overlay models (Besseling, 1958)
• Single surface models (Armstrong & Frederick, 1966)
• Multisurface models (Mroz, 1967)
• Two-surface models (Dafalias & Popov, 1976)
• Endochronicmodels (Valanis, 1971)
• Models with yield surface distortion (Kurtyka, 1988)
The most robust and widely used models is the single surface models. Which also
is the standard models available in Abaqus, and the ones applied in the present work.
7.2.1
Incremental Theory of Plasticity
The rate independent strain is based on the additive rule, which is expressed by:
ε = εe + εp
(7.1)
which we also know as the total strain tensor (some strain contributions has been
omitted), where εe is the elastic strain tensor component and εp is the plastic strain
tensor component.
The elastic strains are subjected to Hook’s law:
σ = Deεe = De(ε −εp)
(7.2)
where De is the elastic stiﬀness matrix. The stresses are assembled by using the
Einstein summation notation (dij = Bijklckl).
The irreversible deformation i.e. plastic strain is developed due to exceedance of the
yield surface. In multiaxial stress state an appropriate yield condition must be used.
For metallic material, such as steel, it is usual to use the Von Mises yield conditions,
here represented in terms of the yield function f:
f =

3
2(s −a)ij(s −a)ij −σY
,
σY = σ|0 + R
(7.3)
Where σY is the size of the yield surface, σ|0 is the initial size and R is the isotropic
internal variable. s is the deviatoric part of the stress tensor σ and a is the deviatoric
part of the back-stress α, which states the center position of the yield surface.
Plastic strains occur when the current stress state has reached the yield limit, i.e.
58
Jessen
==PAGE:70==
7.2. CONSTITUTIVE MODELS OF CYCLIC PLASTICITY

Generally, these three constitutive models/rules of plasticity has been employed
in CWM when the goal is to simulate the residual stresses [47, 46, 44]. The three
hardening models represented in plane stress state are illustrated on Figure 7.4

==PAGE:71==
7.2. CONSTITUTIVE MODELS OF CYCLIC PLASTICITY
Figure 7.4: (a) Isotropic hardening model showing the expansion of the yield sur-
face with plastic strain; (b) kinematic hardening model showing the translation of
the yield surface with plastic strain; (c) mixed isotropic-kinematic hardening model
showing the expansion and translation of the yield surface with plastic strain; and
(d) resulting stress-strain curves showing diﬀerent yield stress in compression as pre-
dicted by diﬀerent plasticity models: C – kinematic hardening, D – mixed hardening,
and E – isotropic hardening, from [46].
7.2.2
Isotropic Hardening
The isotropic hardening model assumes that the yield surface maintains the same
shape, center and orientation. As the yield criterion is reached and plastic defor-
mation occurs the size of the yield surface subsequently expands, as illustrated on
Figure 7.4 (a). This model neglect most phenomenons associated with cyclic load-
ing response such as path dependent Bauschinger’s eﬀect, which involves lowered
yield point on stress reversal. This hardening model tends to over-predict the size
of the elastic region for cyclic loading problems. Nevertheless, this model is often
used CWM due to the easy implementation and calibration of input parameters
[46].
7.2.3
Kinematic Hardening
The kinematic hardening model assumes that the yield surface maintains the same
shape, size and orientation. As plastic deformation initiates, the center of the yield
surface is translated, as illustrated on Figure 7.4 (b).
This model response cor-
responds to material response which takes place for low amplitude cyclic loading
problems [46].
60
Jessen
==PAGE:72==
7.3. THE ROLE OF CONSTITUTIVE MODEL ON THE PREDICTED
RESIDUAL STRESS FIELD OF WELD STRUCTURES
7.2.4
Mixed Hardening
The mixed hardening model, is a combination of the isotropic and kinematic hard-
ening. The model allows simultaneous translation and expansion/contraction of the
yield surface, as illustrated on Figure 7.4 (c). This model is considered to be one
of the most accurate models for predicting residual stresses in CWM, especially for
welds involving hardening materials such as stainless steel. However, the model is
rarely used in CWM due to few accessible material data.
7.3
The Role of Constitutive model on the Pre-
dicted Residual Stress Field of Weld Struc-
tures
A number of studies have been performed on investigating the role of constitutive
model on the predicted residual stresses which arise in relation to the welding process
[47, 46, 44, 61].
All of the studies are performed in relation to research of the performance of nuclear
facilities. Theses welds are mainly performed in stainless steel, namely AISI 316LN.
Similarly, they all investigate the isotopic, kinematic and mixed hardening model
predictions with neutron diﬀraction measurement of residual stresses (one study of
the two in [44] is using deep hole drilling measurements).
[47, 46] investigates a 6mm deep slot weld, consisting of three passes. [61] also in-
vestigates a similar weld, while the exact geometry is not described. They all come
to the same conclusion for the particular multi-pass welding: the mixed hardening
model gives the most accurate predictions, the isotropic models overestimates the
residual stresses and the kinematic model underestimates the residual stresses. How-
ever, [46] uses a special two step annealing approach which is diﬃcult to implement,
therefore they recommend as an alternative, to use the isotropic model with a single
annealing approach which gives moderately conservative predictions. The isotropic
material data were derived from monotonic tests and the kinematic/mixed material
data was derived from cyclic tests.
[44] investigates two cases, an intermediate-walled pipe with Rin/t = 10.5 and a
thick walled pipe with Rin/t = 2.8. Same material (AISI 316LN) was used. The
investigation concluded that the most accurate predictions was achieved by using
the isotropic hardening model. However, for the thick walled pipe, the isotropic
model gave a substantial overestimation of the residual stresses in the hoop direc-
tion, which is the direction of interest for residual stresses in circumferential weld
joined pipes (corresponding to the longitudinal direction in a straight weld). For
this direction the mixed hardening model gave better result.
Although the same mechanical properties do not apply to normal carbon steel such
61
Jessen
==PAGE:73==
7.4. NON-LINEAR KINEMATIC AND MIXED HARDENING MODELS
as S355ML, theses investigations give an idea of how the hardening model aﬀect the
predictions of the residual stresses and how CWM is used today to give a greater
insight of the residual stresses in welded structures.
7.4
Non-linear Kinematic and Mixed Hardening
Models
The preferable nonlinear kinematic and mixed hardening models used in Abaqus is
the Lemaitre-Chaboche formulation. The formulation is primarily used to predict
material response under inelastic loading. The formulation is a further development
of the Armstrong-Frederik formulation (1966) [46].
Chaboche Kinematic Model
The kinematic hardening model is deﬁned as a combination of a kinematic term and
a relaxation term. It is implemented through the shift of the evolution of the back-
stress tensor 1. When temperature and ﬁeld variables are omitted the kinematic
hardening law takes the form:
˙α =

number of back-stresses terms, determines the accuracy of the models representation
of the experimental data. Usually, two pairs of back-stress terms are suﬃcient (M=2)
[33]. The Chaboche model is illustrated schematically, showing the inﬂuence of the
diﬀerent back-stresses on Figure 7.5
In the Chaboche model, the last term of back-stress, γM inﬂuence the ratcheting and
mean stress relaxation if chosen small (up to γM = 10, γM = 0 yields no occurrence
of ratcheting )2. A suitable choice of γM can be provided by the relation [28, ch.

Which provides good predictions of uniaxial ratcheting for steady state. In case of

[28].

Mixed Model
The isotropic component, or the expansion/contraction of the yield surface, analo-
gous to hardening/softening, is conveniently described by superposition of isotropic
hardening to kinematic hardening. If the hardening is non-linear, it is advantageous
to express the isotropic and kinematic hardening of the same form:
dR = b(Q −R)dεp
(7.10)
which also can expressed as:
σY = σ|0 + Q(1 −e−bεp)
(7.11)
Where σ|0 is the yield stress at zero plastic strain and εp is the equivalent plastic
strain. The terms Qinf and b are material parameters, which deﬁnes the maximum
change in the radius of the yield surface and the rate of which the yield surface
changes, respectively [46].
The information given in this chapter should be seen as the theoretical background
for the experimental testing described in Chapter 10. In this chapter there will be
developed a isotropic and a kinematic material model based these tests.

Chapter 8
Heat Source Modeling
In a simpliﬁed manner it can be said that, the amount of heat entering the model is
directly correlated to the size of residual stresses, the more heat the more distortion.
Therefore, heat source modeling is essential to the precession of the numerical model.
In this chapter the diﬀerent types of heat sources used in CWM will be presented.
In addition, the procedure for estimating heat source parameters (i.e. calibrating)
will be shown.
Several heat source models exist. The choice of which heat source model there should
be used in a simulation, is governed by the level of precision needed to simulate a
certain phenomena, as described in section 5.5. If microstructural processes e.g.,
hot cracking is simulated, great precision near the weld pool (fusion zone) is needed.
Whereas if distortion and residual stresses are sought, lower temperatures below
about 600 ◦C-800◦C are of most importance [3], thus the importance of a precise
geometry of the weld pool is lowered. The proﬁt of not having the needs to model the
weld pool geometry and dynamics exactly, is very visible in savings in the modeling
and computational time. According to [3] the heat source models can be divided
into ﬁve generations:
1. generation Point, line and plane heat source models. These heat sources are
the most common heat sources and are included as standard in most common
FEM software. These heat sources can be used when analyzing welding eﬀects
suﬃciently far away from the weld.
2. generation The heat source are described by distribution functions. Distributed
ﬂux functions, or distributed power functions for weld process with deep pene-
tration (electro beam, laser and higher power arc welding). These heat source
models are solely based on the heat equation and the only required input is
the power density distribution (or prescribed ﬂux or prescribed temperature).
Most of the weld pool physics is ignored, nevertheless the weld pool shape
and position data can be realistic because these models can compute accu-
rate temperature distributions outside the weld pool. On the other hand, the
64
==PAGE:76==
8.1. HEAT SOURCE MODELS
predictions inside the weld pool is often very crude.
3. generation Capable of predicting the liquid weld pool shape and shape of bead
created by the weld pool. The models solve the Stefan problem for the weld
pool. These models include the hydrostatic stress in the liquid pool, a pressure
distribution on the weld pool surface from the arc, surface tension forces, and
a volume constraint to enforce conservation of mass liquid-solid free bound-
ary. This generation is not much more expensive in computational costs, but
requires more input data.
4. generation Adds equations of ﬂuid dynamics to the modeling of the heat source.
Capable of including buoyancy and Lorentz forces acting on the interior of the
liquid phase by using typical Navier-Stokes equations.
The heat source is
modeled in software with computational ﬂuid dynamic (CFD) capabilities.
5. generation Includes a model of the arc in the heat source model, which adds
magneto hydrodynamics to the equations in the previous generations. These
models face serious challenges still, and often uniqueness cannot be proven.
The heat source model used in this thesis needs to fulﬁll the requirements for esti-
mating accurate temperature ﬁeld for temperature levels aﬀecting the residual stress.
The heat sources of 2. generation fulﬁlls this and covers both prescribed tempera-
ture models and arbitrary distributed heat source models, which is corresponding to
heat source accuracy of basic, standard, accurate and very accurate as described in
section 5.5.
8.1
Heat Source Models
Some of the most used heat source models of 2. generation is the ellipsoid, Goldak
double ellipsoid. Theses are, so called, surface heat sources which reproduces the
heat distribution of low penetrating welding processes (e.g. arc welding). Theses
models are all based on a Gaussian distribution of power density distributed over a
shape (i.e. ellipsoid or double ellipsoid).
Other types of heat source models must be used when a deep penetrating welding
process is simulated, which are capable of reproducing the penetration depth and
very concentrated power input.
As a simpliﬁed approach, the prescribed temperature model can be applied. This
model simply prescribes initial temperature to the elements within the FE model.
The standard Abaqus/CAE interface does not provide facilities to model a moving
heat source of arbitrary shape. Therefore the user subroutine Dﬂux must be imple-
mented. Deluxe is only being supported in Fortran language, therefore Abaqus must
be set up to run a Fortran compiler (such as Intel Parallel Studio XE 2016) from the
command prompt together with a writing environment (such as Microsoft Visual
Studio). The use of Dﬂux subroutine is described in Appendix H. Dﬂux distributes
65
Jessen
==PAGE:77==
8.1. HEAT SOURCE MODELS
the power to the nodes which is within the heat source, so the sum reproduces the
total power input.
The power input [Watt] generated by the welding process, which is implemented
into the heat source model is deﬁned as:
q = IV η
(8.1)
Where I is the current [amp], V is the potential [volt] and η is the arc eﬃciency.

8.1. HEAT SOURCE MODELS
Laser heat source
When laser welding is modeled, there should be used a heat source which is capable
of simulating the very high intensity of heat transport below the surface, also known
as keyhole generation. These heat sources are characterized by having a very high
intensity over a very small area. Several heat sources are available for this purpose
[54].
The simplest heat sources with this capability is heat sources which have a uniform
destitution within a shape, e.g. cone or cylinder. Another option, could be to use
the Gaussian heat source with a very narrow distribution combined with a decay
function that decreases the heat input with penetration depth.
The heat source implemented in the present work is the cylindrical-involution-normal
(CIN) heat source model. It is characterized by having great penetration properties,
which makes it a good heat source model for workpieces of large thickness [54].
The CIN model is represented by the following equation:
Q(r, z) =
kKzq
π(1 −exp(Kzs) exp(−kr2 + Kzz)(1 −u(z −s))
(8.5)
where r =

x2 + y2 is the instantaneous laser beam location, Kz = 3/s is the heat
power exponent, k = 3/r2
0 is the beam focus coeﬃcient, r0 is the beam radius and s
is the penetration depth and u(z −s) is the Heaviside function. x, y and z are the
coordinates of the laser heat source at time t. A representation of the CIN model is
illustrated on Figure 8.4.

(b)

laser beam (b), from [35].

==PAGE:80==
8.1. HEAT SOURCE MODELS
Prescribed Temperature
Prescribed temperature is the approach used in AWI. The approach basically con-
sists of, dumping blocks of elements which has an initial temperature into the re-
maining mesh. The boundary between the dumped block and the remaining mesh
is also given a prescribed temperature, which often is higher than the temperature
of the dumped block. The process is illustrated on Figure 8.5.
This approach is fairly simple to implement in Abaqus (by use of AWI), and involves
no additional coding. The downside of this approach is, that it is relatively hard
to obtain the temperature ﬁeld which is experienced in real welding, secondly it is
hard to know which temperature the blocks should be given, due to the fact, that
heat input in a welding process is supplied in power [W].
The amount of energy supplied into the model, for a given amount of time, depends
on the prescribed temperatures and the volume of the block.
Two temperatures are prescribed, one for the bulk of the block and one for the
surfaces adjacent the block, see Figure 8.5.
As the goal of this procedure, is to model the amount of heat going into the HAZ,
and not the exact heat distribution, the prescribed temperatures correlation with
real conditions is of minor importance.
Usually, the bulk of the material is prescribed the melting temperature. The sur-
face temperature also denoted as the torch temperature Ttorch is based on practical
experience, e.g. for SAW process the temperature is estimated at 2500◦C [57].
The holding time for the torch temperature is determined based on the chunk length
Lchunk and the welding speed v, deﬁned as:
ttorch = Lchunk
v
(8.6)
The holding time is represented by the length of the discrete time step, for which
each chunk is applied. The torch temperature is ramped over the holding time. This
implies that the net heat input also rely on the chunk length, the longer the chunk,
the less energy is lost due to ramping, compared to smaller chunks.
The temperature ﬁeld can be calibrated by partly varying the prescribed temper-
atures (and boundary temp) and partly by adjusting the chunk size. Usually the
prescribed temperatures are determined based on a qualiﬁed guess, where as the
chunk length is varied for calibration. This is a very time consuming process, which
both involve remodeling and computation of computational demanding models.
In case of multi-pass welds, it is very diﬃcult to obtain precise calibrations, because
each welding bead cannot be adjusted separately with respect to chunk length. The
chunk length must be the same for all beads in the same cross-section.
69
Jessen
==PAGE:81==
8.2. HEAT SOURCE IN 3D VS. 2D
Figure 8.5: Concept of prescribed temperature: prescribed boundary temperature
(left) and prescribed block temperature (right).
8.2
Heat Source in 3D vs. 2D
3D heat source parameters cannot be implemented directly into 2D since the 2D
geometric space ignores the diﬀusive heat ﬂux in the direction of torch travel. A
measure which governs the comparability of 3D heat sources in 2D is the weld Paclet
number. It is deﬁned as Pew = ρcpSwLw/kT where Sw is the linear torch speed, Lw is
a characteristic length, taken to be the diameter of the heat source representing the
torch in the welding direction, and ρ, cp and λ is the density, speciﬁc heat capacity
and the thermal conductivity. For high Paclet number, in the order of 15, the 3D
and 2D almost identical. Whereas lower Paclet numbers, yield lower speeds, the
heat sources are not that similar. Therefore, the same heat sources used in 3D and
2D, must be calibrated for each geometric space. Usually, the eﬃciency of the 2D
heat source is considerably lower than the 3D eﬃciency [62].
8.3
Estimating/Calibrating Heat Source Param-
eters
Estimating heat source parameters can be a very time consuming and trivial pro-
cess, especially for 3D analysis. It is based on manual iteration of parameters and
comparative assessment of the FE results and experimental measurements. This
procedure is the same, independent of the chosen heat source model.
However the approach is quite diﬀerent whether a prescribed temperature heat
source is used or a distributed heat source is used.
One way of speeding this process up, for distributed heat source, is by making the
thermal analysis by use of a 3D steady-state formulation, which is the formulation
used in FEAT-WMT FEM software.
70
Jessen
==PAGE:82==

A description of FEAT-WMT and the capabilities, together with a calibration ex-
ample located in Appendix F.

Chapter 9
Modeling Approach
Until now, the basis for performing a sequentially coupled thermo-mechanical anal-
ysis have been outlined.
In this chapter the modeling approach for performing a sequentially coupled thermo-
mechanical analysis in Abaqus/CAE will be presented, together with the motivation
behind the chosen approach. However, the general motivation for the applied ap-
proach should correspond to the accuracy level of at least basic.
As both 2D and 3D analysis has been performed in the present work, the modeling
approach will cover both practices.
9.1
General Model Choice
Basically three diﬀerent modeling practices are being applied in this work:
• 3D modeling using AWI (prescribed temperatures)
• 3D modeling using Dﬂux (distributed heat source)
• 2D plain strain using Dﬂux (distributed heat source)
Each of these modeling approached has some basics in common, but on many levels
they diﬀer. In Table 9.1 the main characteristics for each model is given:

==PAGE:84==
9.2. SELECTION OF ELEMENTS AND MESH DENSITY
Some of the adapted modeling approaches, has been based on modeling experience
acquired by the predecessors to the present thesis, namely Residual Stresses in Welds
of Supersize Monopiles for Oﬀshore Wind Substructures [42]. The modeling experi-
ence adapted does mostly relate to the validation case and includes: the validation
case geometry, 3D mesh density, 3D element choice, chunk length and prescribed
temperatures.
9.2
Selection of Elements and Mesh Density
In this present work, is it chosen to work in both 2D and 3D geometric space. The
2D analysis is performed as a 2D plain strain simulation, where the plain strain
thickness is selected to correspond to the depth of the weld. This type of simulation
(denoted as 2D-X in [25]) is only suitable for predicting longitudinal residual stress,
because the nonlinear temperature variation is not captured by this geometric space
[25]. The 3D simulation is called the general case. It is capable of predicting residual
stresses in all principal directions. The approach where 3D solid elements are used
is applied.
In the following, only topics related to 2D plain strain and 3D solid element will be
treated.
9.2.1
Element Selection
The selection of elements has been done on the basis of the performance under con-
ditions given in the welding models, namely relatively large levels of plasticity.
Abaqus gives the general choice of using either linear elements (includes bilinear) or
quadratic elements. Linear element or simple elements have a nodal point at each
corner, whereas quadratic elements have an additional node between the corners.
Quadratic elements are often referred to as element of higher order. The diﬀerence
between linear and quadratic element lies in the shape functions and are expressed
in terms of the element’s ability to simulate the stress/temperature variation. Lin-
ear elements are only capable of having a linear variation over the element, whereas
quadratic have a variation, over the element, of a higher degree (polynomial). In
Figure 9.1 diﬀerent elements in 2D and 3D which is available in Abaqus are shown.
73
Jessen
==PAGE:85==
9.2. SELECTION OF ELEMENTS AND MESH DENSITY
Figure 9.1: Diﬀerent FEM elements in 2D and 3D space, with indicated nodal degree
of freedom. N.B. integration points are omitted, from [66].
In general, it is assumed that the linear elements perform better compared to higher
order elements for large deformation problems (i.e. plasticity). The reason for this
is, that the linear element has a more simple formulation1, which gives a more robust
numerical model and analysis [10]. On this basis, and observations experienced in a
convergence analysis (see Appendix L), it is chosen to use linear elements through-
out the welding simulations.
Diﬀerent integration rules can be applied for quadrilaterals or hexahedrons, namely
full integration or reduced integration. Reduced integration reduces the number of
integration points, also known as Gauss points. This aﬀects, that some accuracy is
lost, but less computational time is used. It is also the assumption, that the stiﬀness
matrix is overestimated in displacement-based FE formulations. Therefor can it be
advisable to use reduced integration in order to reduce the stiﬀness, especially for
nonlinear problems, such as welding simulation [67]. However, when using reduced
integration, care should be taken with respect to instability of the stiﬀness matrix.
This eﬀect is called hourglassing and occurs due to the stiﬀness matrix being zero.
Thus, when using reduced integration hourglass control should be enabled, which
means that the hourglass energy should be less than 5% of the internal energy [10].
The element choices are summarized in Table 9.2. The choice of not using reduced
integration in 2D plain strain is that the computational time is of minor concern,
therefore full integration is used here. In the 2D thermal analysis, elements of higher
order are used because the instability problem is not present (no plasticity) and very
high temperature gradients are present in the weld region (approx 3000◦C to 24◦C
within few millimeters).
1The linear element is only capable of being either elastic and plastic, whereas the higher order
elements can be both elastic and plastic at the same time.
74
Jessen
==PAGE:86==
9.3. DEFINITION OF COORDINATE SYSTEM
Table 9.2: Element choice. The 3D solid elements are of type hexahedrons.
3D solid
Thermal
DC3D8: An 8-node linear heat transfer brick
Mechnical
C3D8R: An 8-node linear brick, reduced int., hourglass control
2D plain strain
Thermal
DC2D8: An 8-node quadratic heat transfer quadrilateral
DC2D6: A 6-node quadratic heat transfer triangle
Mechnical
CPE4: A 4-node bilinear plane strain quadrilateral
CPE3: A 3-node linear plane strain triangle
9.2.2
Mesh Density
The applied mesh density is governed by the choice or elements and stress variation.
The linear element can only simulate linear variations, whereas the quadratic ele-
ment can simulate polynomial variation. This yield that fewer quadratic elements
are needed to simulate a given variation, compared to linear. As linear elements are
chosen, more elements are needed compared to quadratic elements.
The eﬀect of concentration (stress/temperature) variation on the choice of mesh
density does also relate to the ability to simulate concentration variations. In areas
of the model where the detailed stress variation is of interest and great concentra-
tion variation is expected, the mesh density should be increased in order to predict
a realistic variation within the area. This means that the mesh density should be
increased in the weld region, whereas the mesh density further away from the weld
can be decreased.
The mesh density of the 3D models is based on convergence analysis made by [42].
It was found that a mesh density, corresponding to an average element length of
2mm within the weld region was suﬃcient.
The mesh density of the 2D models is based on a convergence study of the validation
case, which is available in Appendix L. Mesh densities related to average element
lengths of 2mm, 1mm, 0.5mm and 0.25mm was investigated. It was found that mesh
densities related to an average element length of 1mm were suﬃcient for obtaining
convergence in the simulations.
9.3
Deﬁnition of Coordinate System
The coordinate system used is corresponding to the coordinate system illustrated
on Figure 9.2 and Figure 9.3 for 3D and 2D, respectively. The terminology used in
Abaqus denotes the, x direction in subscript 11, y-direction in subscript 22 and the
z-direction in subscript 33. In the present models, the x-direction (11) corresponds
to the longitudinal direction of the weld, y-direction (22) to the through-thickness di-
rection of the weld and z-direction (33) to the transverse direction of the weld.
75
Jessen
==PAGE:87==
9.4. THERMAL MODELING
Figure 9.2: 2D coordinate system.
Figure 9.3: 2D coordinate system.
9.4
Thermal Modeling
As stated, the thermal modeling approach is conducted after two diﬀerent ap-
proaches: Prescribed temperatures and distributed heat source. The basics of these
approaches have been outlined in Chapter 8.
The accuracy class of these heat source models is basis/standard and accurate/very
accurate for prescribed temperatures and distributed heat source, respectively, as
stated in Section 5.5.1 after [25].
9.4.1
Thermal Properties and BC’s
The thermal properties, in terms of thermal conductivity, density, speciﬁc heat, are
modeled as described in Section 6.4.1.
The thermal BC’s, is similarly modeled as described in Section 6.3.1. The radia-
tion is implemented as an embedded coeﬃcient, which means that the emissivity
coeﬃcient has no temperature dependency. The conduction, in terms of the ﬁlm
coeﬃcient, is on the other hand, modeled with temperature dependency as an in-
teraction property.
76
Jessen
==PAGE:88==
9.5. MECHANICAL MODELING
9.4.2
Calibration of Heat Source
The two models are calibrated on the basis of the same data, namely temperature
history measurement and the fusion boundary observed on cross-sectional cutout
from welding tests. The calibration process is described in section 8.3.
9.4.3
Addition of Filler Material
The addition of ﬁller material is performed with the use of element inactivity for all
three modeling approaches.
Initially all the beads are removed from the model and subsequently inserted into
the model. However, diﬀerences exist. For the AWI approach, chunks of the bead
are added subsequently corresponding to the welding speed.
In the case of dis-
tributed heat source, the whole bead is placed at once with ambient temperature,
subsequently the heat source moves over the bead. The amount of erroneous heat
conducted to the bead in front of the heat source, is considered neglective, due to
the welding speed [3]. The two approaches are illustrated on Figure 9.4.
(a)
(b)
Figure 9.4: Weld pool geometry: Prescribed temperature (a), distributed heat source
(b).
9.5
Mechanical Modeling
The material modeling are generally chosen in relation to the accuracy level basic/-
standard (established in Section 5.5)
The cases which are referred to in the following, are numerical simulations of diﬀer-
ent welding experiments.
77
Jessen
==PAGE:89==
9.5. MECHANICAL MODELING
9.5.1
Material Model
The thermal expansion coeﬃcient and Poisson’s ratio are implemented as described
in section 6.4.2.
For the validation case (case 1) where steel S235JR is used, the E-modulus, yield
strength and the plasticity is modeled as described in Section 6.4.2 and the consti-
tutive model isotropic plasticity. The annealing temperature is implemented with a
value of 1500◦C.
For cases using steel S355ML, two sets of material models are available. A isotropic
model, which is based on monotonic testing, which includes values of E-modulus,
yield-strength and plasticity in the temperature interval 20◦C −1000◦C. The mate-
rial parameters are described in Section 10.2.
The other model is a kinematic model, which is based on cyclic testing. The model
includes sets of E-modulus, yield-strength and plasticity in the temperature interval
20◦C −850◦C. The material parameters are found in Section 10.4.
The annealing temperature for S355ML is implemented with a value of 1507◦C.
The welding ﬁller material is modeled, with the assumption, that it has the same
material propertied as the parent base material. This means that diluting eﬀect and
the eﬀect of diﬀerent strength properties is neglected. This assumption is made due
to lack of material properties of the ﬁller material.
9.5.2
Addition of Filler Material
The procedure for adding ﬁller material becomes increasingly important when mod-
eling multi-pass welds. The procedure for doing this, can be done in several ways.
AWI either uses bead removal on a pass level or on a model level. Both these pro-
cedures are denoted inactive by [25]. The inactive procedure, uses a method where
the elements are removed from the model and reactivated, stress free, at deﬁned
steps. The pass level procedure, removes and inserts the active elements (inserted
chunk) in the torch pass momentarily. The renaming chunks, which are not heated,
is still within the model and therefore ”ride” along with the deformed mesh. The
advantage of using this approach, is that the elements are updated with their new
location for each pass. This decreases the risk of inserting severely distorted ele-
ments. The element not activated in the analysis is assumed not to aﬀect because
the temperature is deﬁned with a predeﬁned ﬁeld above the annealing temperature,
thus the element has very low strength properties.
The model level approaches removes all the elements in all bead at the initial stage
and inserts them as the torch passes. This approach can result in deployment of
severely distorted elements. But also have the advantage of, the inactive elements
78
Jessen
==PAGE:90==
9.5. MECHANICAL MODELING
not aﬀecting the mechanical analysis.
Another approach, is the passive [25]. In this approach the elements are present in
the model at all time, but the element which should not inﬂuence the mechanical
analysis are given very low strength properties. As the passive elements should be
activated, they are given their actual strength properties. This approach can be
applied within Abaqus by associating the strength properties with ﬁeld variables.
The ﬁeld variables are associated with an amplitude function, which controls the
strength properties as a function of time. This approach is, however, quite time
consuming to implement.
In AWI the pass level approach is adopted.
This approach cannot be implemented easily with the use of Dﬂux. Because the
elements are modeled with room temperature, whereas the AWI model, models
the elements with a uniform predeﬁned temperature within the mechanical analysis
(melting point). Therefor it is diﬃcult to asses at which times the elements should
be inserted.
Instead the inactive approach is used on a bead level.
The bead is activated as
they are deployed. Between each pass (of a whole bead) the overlying beads are
updated with their new position, which prevent severe distortion of the elements
under multi-pass welding. This approach can be applied because it is used together
with an annealing function, which has the eﬀect of canceling all stress in nodes with
temperatures above melting point.
The two diﬀerent approaches are illustrated on Figure 9.5. Where the top pictures
illustrates the AWI approach and the bottom picture illustrates the bead level ap-
proach.
Figure 9.5: Bead removal approach: pass level (top), bead level (bottom).
79
Jessen
==PAGE:91==

proach described in this chapter will be adopted in practice.

Chapter 10
Experimental Work
In this chapter the experimental work done within the present work will be presented.
Two main objectives have been the motivation for performing the experiments: ob-
taining material properties of steel S355 with quality ML (S355ML) for use in CWM
and welding data for calibration of heat sources in CWM. Furthermore, the weld
specimens should form basis for neutron diﬀraction measurement in future studies.
The following experimental work has been conducted:
• Heat Elevated Monotonic Tensile Test of S355ML (Appendix B)
• Heat Elevated Cyclic Test of S355ML (Appendix C)
• Submerged Arc Welding (Appendix E)
• Laser Hybrid Welding (Appendix D)
In general, only a brief presentation of the experiment will be presented together
with the results of the experiments. For a complete description of the experimental
work, reference is made to their relative appendices.
In addition, some processing of the results is performed, with respect to obtaining
material parameters for numerical investigation of S355ML based on experimental
results, namely:
• Monotonic material parameters (isotropic material model)
• Cyclic material parameters (kinematic material model)

10.1. HEAT ELEVATED MONOTONIC TENSILE TEST OF S355ML
10.1
Heat Elevated Monotonic Tensile Test of S355ML
Heat elevated monotonic tensile test of S355ML has been performed consisting of 25
tests in the temperature range 20◦C−1000◦C. The complete experiment description
and in depth discussion of the results is available in Appendix B.
The test is performed in compliance with DS-EN ISO 6892-2-2013 - Metallic material
- Tensile testing Part2: Method of test at elevated temperature [13] and DS-EN ISO
6892-1-2013 - Metallic material - Tensile testing Part1: Method of test at room
temperature [11].
The test has been performed at the DTU’s civil engineering department’s laboratory.
The experimental setup can be seen in Figure 10.1 and Figure 10.2.
Figure 10.1: Test setup: test machine

furnace,

Figure 10.2: View from inside of heat-

In order to capture speciﬁc material properties which is speciﬁc for diﬀerent ranges
of the stress-strain curve (E-modulus, yield stress Rp0.2, tensile strength Rm), the
test was performed with displacement control, with two displacement rate ranges.
Namely, ˙d = 0.0059mm/s and ˙d = 0.02125mm/s for the elastic and plastic, range,
respectively.
82
Jessen
==PAGE:94==
10.1. HEAT ELEVATED MONOTONIC TENSILE TEST OF S355ML
The general test procedure consisted of:
• Heating to speciﬁed temperature
• Holding at speciﬁed temperature for 10min (soak time)
• Displacing test specimen in elastic range
• Displacing test specimen in plastic range until fracture (or piston max)
It can be discussed if the test specimen should have been heated to a higher tem-
perature (e.g. max= 1000◦C) before establishing the speciﬁed test temperature, in
order to reproduce the conditions under the welding process. However this is not
performed.
The resulting monotonic stress-strain curves are illustrated in Figure 10.3. It is
observed that a general decreasing trend of yield stress for increasing temperature
is present. However, for tensile strength the trend is more divergent. It is observed
that for test at 200◦C and 300◦C the tensile strength actually increase, with tensile
strength of 300◦C at the same level as 20◦C.
The measured values of E-modulus, yield stress and tensile strength can be observed
in Figure 10.4, Figure 10.5 and 10.6, respectively.

10.2. DETERMINATION OF MONOTONIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
10.2
Determination of Monotonic Material Pa-
rameters for S355ML
In this section the results obtained in the monotonic tensile test is processed in order
to obtain monotonic material properties to be used for numerical investigations.
The nature of the nonlinear calculation process allows no decreases in stress with
increasing values of plastic strain. This means that the experimental stress-strain
curves cannot be applied directly without no further manipulation.
A well known and widely used representation of the stress-strain relation of steel,
which fulﬁlls the above requirement is the Ramberg–Osgood relationship [64, 36].
The Ramberg–Osgood relationship divides the total strain into an elastic and plastic
part, where the elastic part is described by a linear function and the plastic part is
described by a power function:
εt = εe + εp = σ
E +
 σ
K
1/β
(10.1)
Where K and β is the Ramberg–Osgood constants which respectively describes the
monotonic strength behavior and monotonic hardening behavior of the considered
material.
Equation (10.1) implies that the elastic and plastic part can be divided into inde-
pendent strain ranges. This is not fully true, however the error is negligible and
the assumption of independence is made. By extracting the elastic part of equation
(10.1) a log-transformation can be applied and the plastic strains can be expressed
conveniently as revealed in equation (10.3).
Δεp = Δεt −Δσ
E =
Δσ
K
1/β
(10.2)
Δσ = K(Δεp)β
(10.3)
By using the formulation in equation (10.3) a normal power regression analysis can
be applied in order to derive values of K and β. The Ramberg–Osgood relation has
been applied to the obtained test results.
The procedure for obtaining K and β for each considered temperature involved
subtraction of the elastic strain from the total stress-strain curves as revealed in
equation (10.2) giving the plastic stress-strain relation.
The plastic stress-strain curves measured at the same temperature are combined
into one data set and a regression analysis is performed2.
The resulting ﬁt of the regression analysis for each investigated temperature is illus-
trated in Figure 10.8 together with the R-square value of the regression. Weighted

10.2.1
Establishing Point of Zero Plastic Strain
From the derived values of K and β plastic stress-strain curves for each temperature
can be produced. However the Ramberg-Osgood formulation, when only consider-
ing the plastic part, implies zero stress at zero plastic strain. This is not valid and
cannot be used as plasticity input data. The yield stress needs to be established,
88
Jessen
==PAGE:100==
10.2. DETERMINATION OF MONOTONIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
which is stress at zero plastic strains. The usual procedure for establishing this value
is to use the 0.2% oﬀset as described in Appendix B, section B.5.2. Unfortunately,
this procedure supplies rather conservative values, which cannot be used together
with ﬁtted Ramberg-Osgood curves, especially not for curves with yielding plateau
as observed in the test range 20◦C −300◦C. Instead the approach used in [33] is
adapted.
They use 0.55 · Rp0.2 which is derived by AREVA GmbH Erlangen in
connecting with calibration of Chaboche material models which has a similar slope
as Ramberg-Osgood. The diﬀerence of using Rp0.2 and 0.55 · Rp0.2 is illustrated for
20◦C in Figure 10.10.
Rp0.2 is calculated to 403.45 MPa (see section B.5.2), hence Rp0.2 · 0.55 yields
221.9 MPa which corresponds to a strain oﬀset equal to 0.079%. If an even smaller
value is needed to be established, the 0.01% oﬀset (Rp0.01) could be applied and the
yield stress would be established to 153.5, MPa.
Figure 10.10: Yield stress/point determination, showed for 20◦C.
For each temperature, values of plastic strain εp and corresponding values of stress
σ has been produced. The curves have been translated sideways to satisfy that the
derived values of 0.55 · Rp0.2 intersects with zero plastic strain. The values of εp
is log-distributed in order to capture the power curve close to zero strain. Theses
curves are intended for use in Abaqus with isotropic hardening models. The curves
are illustrated in Figure 10.11. On Figure 10.12 is a 3D interpretation of Figure
10.11 illustrated. The ﬁgure illustrates the interpolation ”surface” Abaqus uses for
plastic calculations. The derived curves undergoes minor changes due to calibration
which is treated in section 10.2.2. The ﬁnal curves expressed in table format can be
found in Appendix K.1.

10.2.2
Parameter Calibration/Validation
The derived monotonic plastic stress-strain relation has been implemented into a
FE-model based on the monotonic test specimen. The geometry and mesh can be
seen in Figure 10.13 (top). The mesh consists of 536 elements of type C3D20, which
is a 20-node quadratic brick.
The specimen is constrained in one end by an encastre boundary condition and in
the opposite end a displacement of 20mm is prescribed, as illustrated in Figure 10.13
(bottom).
Predeﬁned temperature ﬁelds have been deﬁned in order to investigate the diﬀerent
90
Jessen
==PAGE:102==
10.2. DETERMINATION OF MONOTONIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
curves.
Figure 10.13: Geometry and mesh of FE-model of monotonic test specimen (top) ,
Boundary conditions (bottom).
The plasticity is implemented with isotropic hardening rule. Preliminary only ver-
iﬁcation of stress-strain curves at three temperatures (20◦C, 500◦C, 1000◦C) is
performed due to the triviality of the task.
The result of the veriﬁcation analysis can be observed in Figure 10.14. The results
are obtained at the center of the test specimen. It is seen that perfect correlation
between the input material plasticity and the calculated plastic stress-strain relation
of the model, which implies that the material response is as intended for this setup
(Linear geometry).
91
Jessen
==PAGE:103==
10.2. DETERMINATION OF MONOTONIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
Figure 10.14: Comparison of input material plasticity stress-strain relation vs. plas-
ticity response from numerical simulation.
Figure 10.15: Comparison of input material plasticity stress-strain relation vs. plas-
ticity response from numerical simulation with geometric nonlinearity.
However, when the model accounts for geometric nonlinearity3 the situation changes.
By accounting for geometric nonlinearity, the nonlinear geometric response due to
large deformations is taken into account. Diﬀerent from linear geometry calcula-
tions, the element geometry is updated at each load increment, which provides the
ability to recognize that the element size/shape/position is diﬀerent from the initial
state [1].
In relation to the present FE analysis, the geometric nonlinearity should account for
the reduction of the cross-sectional area in case of large deformations, also known as
3By applying ”Nlgeom” to the considered step.
92
Jessen
==PAGE:104==
10.2. DETERMINATION OF MONOTONIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
necking. The results of the numerical simulation with geometric nonlinearity com-
pared with the input material plasticity response can be observed in Figure 10.15.
It is observed, that perfect correlation is present for minor deformations O(≤0.1),
when larger deformations initiates, nonlinear geometric response is observed in terms
of deviation from the input material stress-strain relation.
In order to evaluate whether the observed response is realistic or not, a comparison
should be made with the unﬁtted experimental results. This comparison is made
for all temperatures in Figure 10.16.

tained, inverse analyses could be applied. This involves a quite substantial procedure

and therefor it is decided that this kind of procedure lies beyond the scope of this
work. Instead, some manual changes are performed in order to make the nonlinear
geometric response workable within the deformation range needed.
From analysis made within Chapter 11, it was experienced that the largest plastic
strains were in the order of 0.12. This material data is intended to be used in similar
simulations, thus plastic strains in the same order can be assumed. With this in
mind, it is clear that curve 600◦C, 700◦C, 800◦C needs to be corrected in order to
obtain a reasonable response within this deformation range.

wanted modiﬁcations.

10.3
Heat Elevated Cyclic Test of S355ML
Heat elevated cyclic test of S355ML has been performed, consisting of seven tests
in the temperature range 20◦C −850◦C. The complete experiment description and
in depth discussion of the results is available in Appendix C.
The general test conditions are determined using ”A Code of Practice for the deter-
mination of cyclic stress-strain data” [29].
Some practices recommended in DS-EN ISO 6892-2-2013 - Metallic material - Ten-
sile testing Part2: Method of test at elevated temperature [13] have been applied
e.g. heating procedure and establishment of gauge length, because no equivalent
recommendation is available for heat elevated cyclic tests.
The test has been performed at DTU’s civil engineering department’s laboratory.
95
Jessen
==PAGE:107==
10.3. HEAT ELEVATED CYCLIC TEST OF S355ML
The experimental setup can be seen in Figure 10.18 and Figure 10.19.
Figure 10.18:
Test setup:
test ma-
chine MTS312.31, heating furnace,
displacement
measurement
mecha-
nism, temperature monitors, cooling
fans.
Figure 10.19:
View from inside of
heating furnace:
displacement mea-
surement mechanism, grips, place-
ment of test specimen.
The aim of the experiment is to determine material characteristics for use in low
cycle fatigue, namely multi-pass welding simulations which involves reversed strain
range (R = −1) and relatively large deformations O(εmax = 0.12).
The applied test parameters is chosen based partly on values used in similar ex-
periments [29, 6] and estimates on which capabilities the experimental setup was
capable of.
The total strain range Δεt was chosen to 1.4% yielding a stress range of ±0.7%,
allows the capturing of a relatively large portion of the plastic strain range. The
strain rate is chosen to 0.0125%/s which gives a total time of 14s for each load cycle
or a frequency of 0.07143s−1. This stress range and rate is in compliance with (LCF)
testing regime (failure within 102 −104 cycles) [50].
Strain controlled test is recommended, in order to obtain stabilized hysteresis loops
which the material parameters should be based on [29]. However, this option is not
possible due to limitations of the preciseness of measurement equipment, which is
outlined in Appendix B, section B.2. Instead, displacement control is used. The
displacement range, which is used for the setup, is derived from preliminary strain
96
Jessen
==PAGE:108==
10.3. HEAT ELEVATED CYCLIC TEST OF S355ML
controlled tests. It is found that a displacement of ±0.62273mm equals strain of
±0.7%. The testing time is chosen to 20min which corresponds to 85 load cycles.
The resulting experimental stress-strain hysteresis loop is illustrated in Figure 10.20
and Figure 10.21.

By observing the results in Figure 10.20 and Figure 10.21, it is clear that the re-
sult of test 600◦C and 800◦C is relatively diﬀerent from the remaining tests. It was
also experienced that after ended test, the displacement measurement devise had
detached itself from the specimen5. This means that the strain measurements of
these test is questionable, and it can be discussed if the results is usable for further
investigation. However, these results are used, due to lack of better alternatives.
It is also observed that the smoothest curves is observed for the test at 20◦C, which
where the test performed with the extensometer attached directly on the test speci-
men. However, the tests at 100 −400◦C the hysteresis loop has a comparable shape
and the obtained maximum strains is in the same order of magnitude.
On Figure 10.22, Figure 10.23, Figure 10.24, Figure 10.25 and Figure 10.26 the
strain and stress is plotted as a function of time, for test 20◦C, 100◦C, 200◦C, 300◦C
and 400◦C, respectively. Test 600◦C and 800◦C are omitted due to the uncertainties
present in these tests. By observing the stress as a function of time (upper and
5The measurement device was retightened just before the onset of each test (at elevated tem-

lower peaks) it is seen that a small amount of cyclic softening is present (with the

indicates that the test specimen has ratcheting nature. Normally this phenomenon
is only said to occur at force-controlled test, referring to that there exists strain-
controlled and force-controlled test. However, in displacement controlled test the
strains is not restricted to a speciﬁed interval, thus ratcheting are possible 7.

10.3. HEAT ELEVATED CYCLIC TEST OF S355ML
10.3.1
Stabilized Hysteresis Cycle
The stabilized hysteresis cycle is established when the stable conditions are observed,
i.e. no signiﬁcant change in shape of the loop. The determination of the stabilized
hysteresis cycle is marked with red on the results in Figure 10.20 and 10.21. How-
ever, for the test at 600◦C and 850◦C no stabilized cycle was observed, probably
due to the issues regarding the displacement measurement device, as described in
the previous section. Consequently, a cycle located early in the test is chosen, as it
is assumed that this is the most representative for the real behavior. The stabilized
cycles are illustrated in Figure 10.27 (left). By observing the ﬁgure, it is seen that
the strains for test at 20◦C −400◦C lies within the same range, however test at
100◦C and ◦C has slightly higher strain range Δεt = 1.6% relative to the target
range Δεt = 1.4%. For test at 600◦C and 850◦C it is seen that the strain range is
considerable higher, this could be an indication of erroneous strain measurements.
However, when the strength of the material is decreased (as a consequence of ele-
vated temperature), it is assumed that the strain will increase to some extend.
The determination of the module of elasticity is based on the established stable cy-
cles. The E-modulus is calculated from both the compressional and tensional elastic
range, as recommended in [29]. The process of locating the linear elastic range is
based on visual considerations. The data points which are used for the linear re-
gression is illustrated in Figure 10.27 (right) and the derived values of E-modulus is
illustrated in Figure 10.28 and listed in Table 10.4. By observing Figure 10.28 it is
observed that the derived E-modulus do not perfectly correlate with the recommen-
dations from Eurocode [19] and the results observed in a monotonic tension test of
the same material (see Section 10.1), however, the decreasing trend with increasing
temperature is observed.
The yield strength, established after the 0.2% oﬀset procedure is listed in Table
10.4. The procedure for this is described in Appendix B, section B.5.2. The less
conservative yield stress 0.55 · Rp0.2 which is derived by AREVA GmbH Erlangen in
connecting with calibration of Chaboche material models [33] is listed there as well
together with the corresponding yield strain.

10.4
Determination of Cyclic Material Parame-
ters for S355ML
The cyclic experiments outlined in the previous section are used to derive cyclic
material parameters for numerical investigation.
The approach used for determining the cyclic material parameters is the approach
recommended in the Abaqus analysis user manual [1, sec. 23.2.2] for nonlinear cyclic
kinematic hardening model. The kinematic constitutive material model is described
in Section 7.2.
The kinematic components is deﬁned as additive combinations of a purely kinematic
term (linear Ziegler hardening law) and a relaxation term (the recall term) which
introduces the nonlinearity. The evolution of the kinematic hardening is described
in terms of on or several back-stresses. The eﬀect of using several back-stresses is
illustrated in Figure 10.29. When temperature and ﬁeld variable dependencies are
omitted the hardening law for each back stress is expressed by:
˙αk = Ck

(10.5)
where N is the number of back-stresses and Ck and γk are material parameters also
know as the Chaboche kinematic parameters. The Chaboche model is discussed in
Section 7.4.

The kinematic hardening component, in terms of Chaboche kinematic parameters
is determined from stabilized cyclic hysteresis stress-strain curves.
A schematic
drawing of a stabilized cyclic hysteresis stress-strain curve is illustrated in Figure
10.30. In order to obtain the hardening parameters the shifted plastic stress-strain
relation need to be generated.
The procedure for this is described by equation
(10.6).

The back-stress for each data pair is given by:
αi = σi −σs
(10.7)
where αi is the overall back-stress obtained by summarizing all the back-stress at
this point and σs is the stabilized size of the yield surface, deﬁned as σs = (σ1+σn)/2.
By integrating the back-stress evolution laws over the strain cycle, the following
expression for the ﬁrst data pair (σ1,0) yields:
αk = Ck

(10.8)
where αk,1 denotes the kth back-stress at the ﬁrst data point. The above relations
enables calibration of the parameters Ck and γk.

ferent ranges, it can be necessary to deﬁne several back-stresses in order to obtain

A suitable value of the yield stress needs to be established corresponding to the
ﬁrst data pair (σ1,0). The usual procedure for establishing the yield stress is to
use the 0.2% oﬀset, however, in this case, it is not suitable to use because of its

10.4.1
Parameter Calibration/Validation
In this section the cyclic material parameters, derived in the previous section are
being numerically veriﬁed.
The geometry of the numerical model is corresponding to the geometry of the cyclic
test specimen. Symmetry conditions has been applied, so only one quarter of the
model is modeled. Symmetry boundary conditions has been applied in x- and y-
direction and a simple support has been applied in the z-direction. A prescribed
displacement has been applied to the top of the test specimen. The test specimen,
boundary conditions and the applied mesh are illustrated in Figure 10.34.
The element used is C3D20, which is a 20-node quadratic brick.
One time step, of type static, general including nonlinear geometric eﬀects is applied
with a duration of 10,000s with a maximum increment of 0.4s.
107
Jessen
==PAGE:119==
10.4. DETERMINATION OF CYCLIC MATERIAL PARAMETERS FOR
S355ML
The prescribed displacement is deﬁned as a periodic function, where the amplitude
a is given by use of Fourier series:
a = A0 +
N

n=1
[An cos nω(t −t0) + Bn cos nω(t −t0)]
for
t ≥t0
(10.11)
a = A0
for
t < t0
(10.12)
Where t0, N, A0, An and Bn, n = 1, 2 . . . N are user deﬁned constants.
In order to simulate the ramping oscillation of the displacement, the parameters are
chosen as follows: t0 = 0, A0 = 0, A1 = 0, B1 = 1 and the angular frequency is set
to ω = 0.4488rad corresponding to a period of 14s.
Figure 10.34: Mesh (bottom) and boundary conditions (top) of veriﬁcation model.
The kinematic material parameters, derived in the previous section, should be veri-
ﬁed with respect to the cyclic test results. In order to accomplish this the real strain
range needs to be established.
This is done by centering the stabilized hysteresis curves with respect to the strain
range. The centered curves are illustrated in Figure 10.35. From the ﬁgure, the
real stress range is red. Although the strain range was supposed to be 0.7% ac-
cording to the preliminary investigations (see Appendix C) it diﬀers from this value.
For each temperature, manual interpolation of prescribed osculating displacement is
performed within Abaqus in order to obtain strains corresponding to the measured.
The real strain range, observed in Figure 10.35 and the corresponding prescribed
oscillating displacement are listed in Table 10.6.

Jessen
==PAGE:122==
10.5. SUBMERGED ARC WELDING EXPERIMENT
In general, a quite good correlation is observed between the experimental result and
the veriﬁcation simulation. The hysteresis loop has a similar shape, especially the
plastic plateau is simulated relatively precise. The ratcheting behavior is also cov-
ered quite well. However, for 200◦C the ratcheting behavior is overpredicted with
a factor in the order of 3. However, this is not considered to be a major prob-
lem, due to the relative few thermo mechanical cycles present in multi pass welding
compared to LFC. For test at 600◦C and 800◦C noting can be concluded on the
ratcheting behavior due to the quality of the test results, but it covers the chosen
stabilized hysteresis loop quite well. Therefore it is concluded that the veriﬁcation
is representative.
Hence it can be concluded that the derived material properties are veriﬁed and
therefor representative of cyclic deformations within comparable conditions.
It must be noted, that the eﬀect observed, can arise from other phenomena than
ratcheting. Strictly speaking, it can only be isolated as ratcheting when the cyclic
tests are performed under force controlled conditions. As the test is displacement
controlled neither the strain range, nor the force range are held constant. On the
contrary, the Chaboche kinematic model has been proved to provide good predictions
of ratcheting eﬀects [28].

Chapter 11
Case 1: Veriﬁcation Case - Two
Pass One Sided SAW Butt Weld
In an earlier PhD thesis ”Numerical Modeling of Welding Induced Stresses” by Jan
Langkjær Hansen [30] an investigation of the residual stresses in a two pass one
sided butt weld was performed.
The investigation consisted of a numerical prediction along side with an experimental
welding application. The experimental part consisted of temperature measurements
of the welding process, cross-sectional information on the fusion boundary and neu-
tron diﬀraction measurements of the residual stresses.
In this chapter, the same case investigated by Jan Langkjær Hansen will be treated.
Due to the presence of rare neutron diﬀraction measurements of welding induced
residual stresses, the case will act as a veriﬁcation of various modeling approaches
thereof the name ”Veriﬁcation Case”.
The case as also been used in ”Residual
Stresses in Welds of Supersize Monopiles for Oﬀshore Wind Substructures” [42] by
Christian Ammitzbøll Madsen and Daniel Sommerlund Pedersen which is a prede-
cessor master thesis project to the present. Some modeling approaches and experi-
ences made by [42], in particular within use of AWI, will be adapted in the present
investigation.
The following modeling approaches will be investigated:
• 3D Abaqus model using AWI approach
• 3D Abaqus model using Dﬂux subroutine
• 2D Abaqus model using Dﬂux subroutine
11.1
Application Description
The geometry of the welding application and the weld is illustrated in Figure 11.1
and Figure 11.2, respectively. The exact geometry of the weld is not given in [30],
119
==PAGE:131==
11.2. WELDING EXPERIMENT
thus the weld geometry is a qualiﬁed guess based on illustrative material. The weld
is performed in two passes, indicated with 1 and 2 in Figure 11.2.
The steel plates which are joined is of grade S235 with quality JR.
Figure 11.1: Geometry of welding application, from [42].
Figure 11.2: Geometry of veriﬁcation case weld, from [42].

for this experiment, however the welding parameters was. The welding parameters

estimate based on general eﬃciency on SAW procedure given in [27].
Table 11.1: SAW parameters for case: two pass one sided butt weld, from [30].

11.2.1
Neutron Diﬀraction
Neutron diﬀraction is a non-destructive method of measuring residual stresses within
structural components. In the following an quick introduction is given.
An typical setup of an neutron diﬀraction process is illustrated in Figure 11.5. The
incident beam is pointed at an gauge volume, which can have various size, depending
on the material investigated. The incident beam is then scattered by the material
within the gage volume. By measuring the scattered beam the mean lattice plane
spacing d of the steel crystal can be determined. The lattice spacing, d, is determined
for all three principal directions, equivalent to the strain element in x, y and z.
The strain is determined by knowing the relative change in lattice spacing, expressed
as:
ε = d −d0
d0
(11.1)
Where d0 is the initial lattice spacing at zero strain.
The determination of the
reference point typically determined at a location on the specimen where no residual
121
Jessen
==PAGE:133==
11.2. WELDING EXPERIMENT
is expected, thus residual stresses from rolling and other machining prior to the
welding, will not be accounted in the residual stress determination.
Figure 11.5: Schematic illustration of a typical continuous source based diﬀractome-
ter for strain measurement, from [32].
The stress can then be determined by following expression:
σx =
E
(1 + ν)

εx +
ν
1 −2ν (εx + εy + εz)

(11.2)
Where E is the modulus of elasticity, ν is Poisson’s ratio. Equivalent expressions
exists for σy and σz.
The neutron diﬀraction measurements for the present case was taken according to
the illustration in Figure 11.6. The gauge volume was chosen to be 5 mm x 5 mm x
1 mm, with the 1 mm dimension being in the direction of each individual orthogonal
measurement direction. The 5 mm dimension in the two other directions means that
some average is inherent.
Figure 11.6: Location of neutron diﬀraction measurements, from [30]
122
Jessen

11.3
General Modeling Choices
In this section will the general modeling choice which are general for the veriﬁcation
case, namely the material modeling and thermal boundary conditions.
It must be noted that all the models is modeled with symmetry conditions in the
center of the weld. This is made with having computational time in mind. However,
this should not aﬀect the results.
11.3.1
Material Modeling
The material modeling in terms of thermal and mechanical properties, covering the
factors listed in Table 11.2, is modeled as described in section 6.4.
124
Jessen

11.4
3D Model Using AWI Approach
The analysis is this section has been made using the AWI approach. The analysis is
performed as a sequential coupled thermo-mechanical analysis. Where the thermal
and mechanical analysis is performed separately.
In this section will the FE-model be presented together with results of the analy-
sis.
11.4.1
Boundary Conditions
The thermal boundary properties is modeled as stated in the Section 11.3.2. The
surfaces where the thermal boundary conditions are applied can be seen in Appendix
M.
The mechanical boundary conditions are illustrated in Figure 11.12. It consists of
a symmetry condition, constraint in y-direction and constraint in z-direction. The
coordinate system is indicated on the ﬁgure.
The symmetry-condition, located in the center of the weld, indicated with blue
arrows on the ﬁgure. The constraints in the y-direction is located at two nodes, one
at each end-plate. The constraint in the y-direction is located at one node at one of
the sides.
126
Jessen
==PAGE:138==
11.4. 3D MODEL USING AWI APPROACH
Figure 11.12: Boundary conditions
11.4.2
Meshing and Elements
The applied mesh can be seen in Figure 11.13. It consist of 49866 linear hexahedral
elements of type DC3D8 for the thermal analysis and DC3D8R for the mechanical
analysis. The mesh density is based on a convergence study conducted by [42]. Th
mesh density within the HAZ is made with an average element length of 2mm, while
the mesh density further away from the HAZ increase with a average element length
of 10mm. The distribution can be seen in Figure 11.14.
Figure 11.13: Mesh geometry
Figure 11.14: Mesh geometry close to weld

11.4. 3D MODEL USING AWI APPROACH
11.4.3
Heat Source - Pass Control
The heat source of this model is of the prescribed temperature type.
The heat
source and the calibration process are described in Section 8.1. The heat source
characteristic depend on the prescribed temperatures, chunk length and torch time.
The calibration of the prescribed temperature heat source has been conducted in [42]
on the basis of the welding experiment. These characteristics are adapted in the
present work and listed in Table.
Table 11.5: Prescribed temperature heat source characteristic.

Torch

1500

The torch time is calculated from the welding speed (taken as 5.95 [mm/s] for both
passes) and the chunk length. The chunk length is based on heat source calibration
and assessments of the weld pool geometry1. The interpass time is assessed from
the temperature-history of the welding experiment and the afterpass time is chosen
suﬃciently large to ensure cooling to ambient temperature.
Each chunk laid is discretized in steps, with a time length corresponding to the torch
time. The increment control of the step is chosen to be automatic, meaning, that
Abaqus calculates the necessary increment to perform the nonlinear analysis.
The bead/chunk removal in the mechanical analysis is performed on pass level (de-
scribed in Section 9.5.2). Meaning, that beads is initially included in the mechanical
analysis and then removed and reinserted stress free, when the torch has passed the
respective chunk.
11.4.4
Results
In the following will the result of the present thermo-mechanical weld analysis be
presented. It consist of a thermal part and a mechanical part. The thermal analysis
will be compared with the welding test data, and the mechanical will be compared
with the neutron diﬀraction measurement obtained on the welded specimen.
Thermal Analysis
The transient temperature history of the simulation has been plotted together with
the measurement from the welding experiment. The temperatures are extracted
1The chunk length should not be greater than the expected weld pool length[25].

==PAGE:140==
11.4. 3D MODEL USING AWI APPROACH
at nodal positions, corresponding to position of the thermocouples in the welding
experiment (see Figure 11.4). The plot is illustrated in Figure 11.15. From the
ﬁgure it is seen that there is relatively good agreement between the predictions
and the experiment, for both the peaks and the cooling slope. Generally is the
peak temperatures a bit higher (in the order of 5%). However, is the correlation
of the peaks a bit more precise on the second pass. The reason for this, is that
the calibration has been focused on matching the ﬁrst pass, thus the second pass
becomes a result thereof. A more imprecise prediction could probably have been
obtained, by changing the geometry of the beads, by making the cross sectional area
of the ﬁrst pass a bit bigger and reducing the area of the second.

The residual stresses have also been obtained on the path deﬁned in Figure 11.18.
Contour plots of the stress distribution are shown together with the plot of the
residual stress distribution along the path for the three principal directions. Figure
11.22 and Figure 11.23 shows the longitudinal residual stresses. Figure 11.24 and
Figure 11.25 shows the transverse residual stresses. Whereas the through-thickness
residual stresses, only are shown from the path in Figure 11.26, due to fact that no
interesting information is visible on the contour plot.
By observing the contour plots of the longitudinal and transverse residual stresses,
is it seen that a direct correlation can be drawn to the expected distribution of
residual stresses in a butt-weld, shown in Figure 4.8 in Section 4.3.2.
Furthermore, is it seen that the predicted distribution of longitudinal residual stress
corresponds to the measured residual stresses, with great precision. However, within
the proximity of the weld (±20mm), no conclusion can be drawn due to the fact that
132
Jessen
==PAGE:144==
11.4. 3D MODEL USING AWI APPROACH
few measurements are present in this region, and the few present are inconsistent.
The maximum Von Mises stresses observed has a value of 428.2 MPa, this corre-
lates with the maximum longitudinal stresses observed which is 411.0 MPa. This
observation conﬁrms, that the longitudinal residual stress, is the stress of interest
and that the residual stresses is of the order of yield strength.
For the transverse residual stress distribution in Figure 11.25 is a minor correlation
observed, but the general stress trend is captured. The predicted residual stress
in through-thickness is of very low magnitude and correlates not with the residual
stresses calculated from the neutron diﬀraction measurement.
The reason for the relatively low match for the residual stresses in the transverse
and through-thickness can be boiled down to two options: either are the residual
stress predictions enormous or either are the residual stresses calculated from the
neutron diﬀraction measurement enormous.
For the prediction of elastic strains, a quite good correlation was found in all di-
rections.
The equation for calculating residual stresses from neutron diﬀraction
measurements (equation (11.2)) combines all the principal directions, for calculat-
ing each direction. This calculation method may favor one direction, if one principal
direction has elastic strains which are considerably higher than the other two.
Another reason could be, that the material properties in terms of modulus of elas-
ticity and Poisson’s ratio are diﬀerent from the expected. This is, however, unlikely
that the inaccuracy in the predictions, should originate only from this, due to the
size of the error.

11.5
3D Model Using Dﬂux Subroutine
The same application has been modeled using distributed heat source instead af the
AWI approach.
All the model parameters, in terms of thermal and mechanical boundary conditions,
mesh, material properties is the same as described in previous section.
The heat source has been modeled with Goldak double ellipsoid heat source through
Dﬂux subroutine, as described in Section 8. The heat source parameters has been
derived by use of FEAT-WMT. The calibration process is described in Appendix G.
However, during analysis was it realized that the heat source parameters should be
slightly adjusted in order to give better predictions. The eﬃciency was changed from
70% to 65% and 76%2, for pass 1 and pass 2, respectively. The shape parameter b
was changed from 3mm to 4mm for pass 2. The implemented heat source parameters
is listed in Table 11.6. The vertical position vpos indicate the vertical displacement
of the heat source center, relative to center of mass of the applied bead.

seen in 3D models. The stresses, in the near weld, are considerably under predicted.

stresses. This conﬁrms, the claims by [25], that 2D models cannot model in-plane
welding residual stresses, because of the ignored conduction of heat in the welding
direction.
