11.7. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION OF VALIDATION CASE
11.7
Discussion and Conclusion of Validation Case
In this section have there been conducted weld analysis with three diﬀerent ap-
proached: 3D with prescribed temperatures (3D AWI ), 3D with distributed heat
source (3D Dﬂux) and 2D plain strain with distributed heat source (2D Dﬂux).
The transient temperature and the fusion boundary was relativity similar for all the
models, with no major deviation between the models.
The obtained longitudinal stresses are illustrated in Figure 11.39. It is observed
that the two 3D models predicts similar stress levels and slopes, whereas the 2D
plain strain model has signiﬁcantly lower stress levels. The 3D models correlate
with the experimental results, whereas 2D model underpredicts the stresses. The
small diﬀerence between 3D AWI and 3D Dﬂux can be a consequence of the slightly
diﬀerent heat input on the second pass.
From the observation can it be concluded that prediction of residual stresses through
CWM is feasible in general. By assuming that the neutron diﬀraction measurements
are representative, can it also bee concluded, that the 3D models perform consid-
erably better compared to 2D plain strain. However the 2D models will give some
indication on the general stress level and distribution in the out of plane direction.
Furthermore can it be said that the prescribed temperature approach and the dis-
tributed heat source approach, yield similar result, which conﬁrms the accuracy
classiﬁcation of heat source models, given in Section 5.5.1.

Regarding the predictions of the transverse and through-thickness residual stresses
(only considering 3D models), the predictions is not that accurate compared to the
welding experiment.
Whether it is due to inaccuracy in the calculation method
142
Jessen
==PAGE:154==
11.7. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION OF VALIDATION CASE
or erroneous predictions is more unclear. However it is clear that the longitudinal
stresses is dominant and therefore only residual stresses in the longitudinal direction
will be investigated in the coming analysis, even through the veriﬁcation case showed
better predictions for the 3D model. The intention with the analysis is to investigate
the diﬀerence between the generation of residual stresses for the SAW process and
the LH welding process. Here it is assumed that a 2D plain strain model will give
reasonable indications.

Chapter 12
Welding test simulation - SAW
and LH welding
In this chapter the two conducted welding test SAW and LH welding, will be ana-
lyzed numerically. The welding test are described in Section 10.5 and Section 10.5,
for SAW and LH welding, respectively.
The purpose modeling these two welding experiments is to investigate the residual
stress development for each welding process. The result should be used to evaluate
whether LH welding could be applied in order to reduce the development of residual
stresses.
Further more should these analyses be used to evaluated the development of resid-
ual stresses dependent on material model, namely isotropic and kinematic material
model, which has been developed during this theses.
In both cases has the 2D plain strain approach been used.
12.1
Case 2:
Two Pass One Sided SAW Butt
Weld of S355ML
In this section will the SAW experiment conducted at Force Technology be analyzed
numerically with respect to obtaining predictions of residual stresses.
The welding experiment is described in Section 10.5.

12.1.2
Results
Thermal Analysis
The result of the thermal analysis, in terms of transient temperature history and the
fusion boundary can be seen in Figure 12.3 and Figure 12.4, respectively. The Peak
temperatures of TC 1 at 9mm, match perfect with the experimental measurements.
The cooling slope for pass 1 correlates minor, whereas the cooling slope for pass 2
has a better match. The reason for this, is likely, that the welding specimen was
equipped with a backing plate with a considerable thickness. The plate is not mod-
eled in the FE-model, because the stiﬀness of the backing plate is not wanted in the
mechanical analysis1. This could, however, be modeled by applying poor strength
properties to the backing plate. It has been tried to modify the thermal boundary
conditions in order to promote a slower cooling rate.
Furthermore, it is observed that the thermocouple at 72mm shows considerably dif-
ference from the predicted. As it will be demonstrated in the next section, is it also
the case for the LH weld. This can indicate that the thermocouple at 72mm (TC16)
had a defect.
The thermal analysis is nonetheless considered representative, because the predic-
tions of the fusion boundary have a relatively good match.
1The backing plate was mounted by spot welded and therefor is it assumed, it had no eﬀect on
the stiﬀness of the plate.

During the mechanical analysis was it experienced that the kinematic material model

A successful analysis, using the kinematic plasticity model, was obtained with a
mesh density related to an element length of 2mm, within the HAZ. Instead of
using 4 back-stresses, as indicated in Section 10.4, was only 2 back-stresses applied.
The eﬀect of this will not be considered in the present work, however, it is usually
considered that Chaboche kinematic model with two back-stresses are suﬃcient [33].
The Von Mises stress is plotted in Figure 12.7 and in Figure 12.8 is the residual
stresses plotted, together with and isotropic analysis with identical mesh density.
For comparison is the original mesh density also plotted on the same ﬁgure. By
observing the ﬁgure, it is seen that the kinematic model predicts considerably lower
residual stress values compared to the isotropic. This eﬀect was expected and is well
described in various literature [47, 46, 44, 61]. This is allegedly due to early yielding
when the stress reverses.
By observing the Von Mises stress for the kinematic model, it is seen that the stress
distribution is quite uniform compared to the isotropic model. This is probably also
148
Jessen
==PAGE:160==
12.1. CASE 2: TWO PASS ONE SIDED SAW BUTT WELD OF S355ML
due to the eﬀect of stress reversal, where plastic deformations will initiate at lower
stress because the yield surface has been translated.
The displacements for all analysis can be found in Appendix N.4.
Surprisingly,
showed isotropic and kinematic diﬀerent deformation mode. The isotropic model
predicted downward angular distortion, while the kinematic model predicted upward
and in a considerably greater magnitude. By observing the eﬀect of the diﬀerent
passes during heating and cooling is it observed that the kinematic model has greater
contractions during cooling compared to the isotropic model. This is probably a con-
sequence to the lower yield point due stress reversal (Baushinger’s eﬀect). It can
be argued that the Baushinger’s eﬀect would cancel out in the steel which has been
annealed due to the welding process.
It must be noted that deformations of the 2D model do not simulate the real be-
havior of a weld, because the model is not constrained by the cooled weld in the
out-of-plane direction, as it would in reality. But the deformations illustrate the
relative amount of deformation in the weld during the heating and cooling process.
A 2D axisymmetric model would probably yield more precise predictions of the
deformation behavior.

12.3
Discussion and Conclusion of SAW and LH
Welding Simulations
2D plain strain numerical analysis of both SAW and LH welding experiment has
been conducted. The analysis has both been performed with the isotropic and the
kinematic material model, which were developed during the present work. The ob-
tained longitudinal residual stresses for both welds are shown in Figure 12.19. It
was predicted high stresses levels for both the SAW and the LH simulation at the
order of 500MPa using the isotropic plasticity model, where the LH simulation had
a slightly higher stress level. Based on the observed can it be said, that the con-
siderably diﬀerences in heat input between the two welding processes, where the
LH process generates less, has minor impact on the level of the generated residual
stresses. An possibly explanation can be, that the deformations are generated over
diﬀerent length scales. For the SAW weld, the deformations is greater, but the area
which is heated by the welding process is correspondingly greater. This aﬀect that
the area which should absorb the deformations during cooling is larger and hence
the strain levels will be a result thereof. For the LH welding process, less heat is
generated, but the area heat is correspondingly smaller and thus the strain levels
will, in the same manner, be a result thereof.
The predicted stress distribution over the cross-section for the two cases where sig-
niﬁcantly diﬀerent. For the SAW simulation was it observed peak stress levels over
the whole cross-section, where the peak stresses was concentrated in the center of
155
Jessen
==PAGE:167==
12.3. DISCUSSION AND CONCLUSION OF SAW AND LH WELDING
SIMULATIONS
the weld for the LH simulation.
For simulations using the kinematic plasticity model was it observed, in both cases,
that the predicted stresses were lower compared to the isotropic model. It is the
belief, that this is due to the kinematic plasticity model’s capability of modeling the
Baushinger’s eﬀect, namely the lower yield point at stress-reversal, i.e translation of
yield surface due to cyclic thermal loading during multi-pass.
Seen in the light of the accuracy of the 2D plain strain in the validation case, should
the trends seen in this investigation, be taken as an estimate of the expected stress
levels in SAW and LH welding, performed by similar procedure. Presumably will a
3D analysis give more accurate predictions.
In order to evaluate the accuracy of the simulations, a comparison should be con-
ducted with experimental measurement of the residual stresses obtained from the
welding specimens.

Chapter 13
Case 4 - Monopile
In the aforementioned thesis made by Christian Ammitzbøl Madsen and Daniel
Sommerlund Perdersen [42] has an analysis of a circumferential weld of can sections
been conducted.
Two wall thicknesses was analyzed, 30mm and 60mm1.
In this section will the isotropic material model, developed in this work, be applied
to the models developed in [42]. This done in order to investigate the expected
residual stress distribution in monopiles with the currently fabrication process. Un-
fortunately was analysis with the kinematic material model not able to succeed for
this model.
The models have kindly been supplied by Christian Ammitzbøl and many of the
pictures seen in the following section is taken directly from their work.
There will not be elaborated on the thermal model, because the exact same thermal
model is used. For information on the thermal analysis is it referred to [42].
Furthermore, will the applied WPS and mechanical model be described brieﬂy. For
a complete description it is also referred to [42].
13.1
Application
The model was based on a WPS, which are currently used in the fabrication of
monopiles at Bladt industries A/S. The analyzed cross-sections can be seen in Fig-
ure 13.1. As seen in the ﬁgure, consists the 30mm weld of 5 passes and the 60mm
weld of 16 passes. The can sections are initially joined by a single V-weld on the
inner side of the monopile (denoted as pass no. 1 in the WPS). This weld pass is
not modeled in the analysis.
The welding process used is SAW, where two welding applications is used in tandem,
on single wire and one double wire.
1A weld in 90mm wall thickness was also tried but not succeeded

WPS is s355/s420.

could be that, according to Von Mises yield theory, it is possible to have biaxial
principal stresses greater than uniaxial yield without yielding, due to the . Here the
uniaxial yield corresponds to the given yield stress (stress-equivalent plastic strain
relation).

Chapter 14
Conclusion and Further Work
The overall objective in the conducted work was been to gain knowledge of the
residual stresses in supersized monopiles by use of numerical simulations. The goal,
on a long term basis, is to obtaining possible design optimizations in the future.
During the work of this theses has there been developed heat elevated material
properties of steel S355ML which is the preferred steel for oﬀshore structures. Both
isotropic and kinematic material models has been developed, based on monotonic
and cyclic testing. The monotonic test has been ﬁtted with the Ramberg-Osgood
power ﬁt, which is proved to provide a quite accurate ﬁt and did also fulﬁll the
requirement for isotropic input data in a numerical model. The cyclic test has been
ﬁtted with the Chaboche kinematic model through the a ﬁtting function available in
Abaqus.
The models has been veriﬁed and calibrated numerically with respect to the experi-
mental tests. The veriﬁcation showed good correlation between the material models
and the experimental test. However, for the cyclic test some uncertainty excites
for the experimental data in the high temperature range (600 −800◦C). This un-
certainty can mainly be correlated with the experimental testing equipment, which
proved to be unreliable for high temperature cyclic testing.
As an initial study on the feasibility of numerical modeling of residual stresses in
weldings, was a validation case simulated. The simulated weld was a 10mm double-
pass one-sided butt-weld, where experimental data was available, in terms of tem-
perature measurements, cross-sectional data and residual stresses measurements.
Th welding process used was submerged arc welding (SAW).
A sequential coupled thermo-mechanical approach was applied, which has proven to
be suﬃcient for determining residual stresses on a macroscopic scale.
Two kind of heat source models was applied, prescribed temperature and distributed
heat source. The prescribed temperature heat source was applied in a 3D model by
use af Abaqus welding interface and the distributed heat source was applied by use
of Dﬂux subroutine on a 3D model and 2D plain strain model. Both heat source
models was proved to provide reasonable temperature predictions. The outcome of
165
==PAGE:177==
the mechanical analysis showed that both 3D models (prescribed temperature and
distributed heat source) showed in general good correlation with the experimental
stress strain measurements in the longitudinal direction of the weld. However, in the
center of the weld greater uncertainty exist, due inconsistency of the experimental
results. The 2D plain strain model under predicted the residual stresses. All three
models had in common that the level of residual stresses was in the yield stress
regime in the weld.
The predictions of the secondary directions, transverse to the weld and through-
thickness showed minor correlation with experimental result. This is however, as-
sumed to be of minor importance due to their little impact on the general level of
residual stresses for that weld geometry.
The modeling experience gained from the validation case was utilized in simulations
of two welding test, which also has been conducted in the present work. The weld-
ing tests was performed with two kinds of welding processes: SAW and laser hybrid
(LH) welding. The outcome of the test was in terms of temperature measurement
and cross-sectional information of the fusion boundary and heat aﬀected zone.
The choice of modeling these two welding processes, is based on the feasibility for
use in the fabrication process of welds in monopiles. Currently is SAW the applied
welding process of monopile can sections, whereas LH welding is assumed to be a
welding process which can be applied in the future. By comparing theses two weld-
ing process, with respect to residual stress generation, can it be evaluated whether
optimization can be made by choosing diﬀerent welding process.
Even though it was proven in the validation case, that the 2D plain strain model
provided conservative results, was it chosen to use this modeling approach for the
welding tests simulations. This choice is made due to the limited time frame. This
yield that the obtained residual stresses are not representative for the real condi-
tions, but will show general residual stress level trends between the two welding
processes.
Both material models, isotropic and kinematic has been applied. The thermal mod-
eling showed good correlation with the experimental measurements, both for the
fusion boundary and the transient temperature history.
The mechanical simulations using the isotropic plasticity model predicted high stress
levels, in the order of 500MPa, in the weld for both the submerged arc weld and
the LH weld. This is considerably above the stress level for the onset of yielding
at 20◦C. The stress distribution was diﬀerent for the two welds, where peak stress
levels was observed over the whole cross-section for the submerged arc weld, whereas
peak stress levels was more concentrated in the center for the LH weld.
The simulation with the kinematic plasticity model showed considerably lower stress
levels compared to the isotropic model for both cases. This corresponds to the ex-
pected eﬀect of using a kinematic material model. It is the belief, that this is due
166
Jessen
==PAGE:178==
to the kinematic plasticity model’s capability of modeling the Baushinger’s eﬀect,
namely the lowered yield point at stress-reversal, i.e translation of yield surface due
to cyclic thermal loading during multi-pass.
The developed isotropic plasticity model for S355ML was also applied in simulations
of welds in monopiles, namely in a weld of 30mm wall thickness and in a weld of
60mm wall thickness fabricated by use of SAW. The simulations has been developed
based on welding procedure speciﬁcations used in the industry for fabrications of
welds in monopiles. The analysis showed residual stress at the level of 400MPa for
both the 30mm weld and the 60mm weld, which corresponds to yield stress at 20◦C.
The assumption made by design guides and codes of practices, assuming residual
stress at the level of yield stress within welds has been conﬁrmed in the analysis.
The validity of the results should be conﬁrmed by comparison with measurement of
residual stresses in corresponding weld.
With regards to future work, following the work done in the present thesis, it is, as
mentioned, advisable to conduct measurement of residual stresses in welds similar
to the analyzed for comparison of the obtained results. Furthermore, by having
the results of the validation case in mind, could it be interesting to compare the
obtained 2D plain strain results with 3D simulations.
Similarly could it be interesting to perform simulations of monopile welds with use
of LH welding and compared with the obtained results for the same welds using SAW.
By having the uncertainties, regarding the obtained results of the cyclic tests, in
mind, can it also be recommended to conduct additional cyclic testing of S355ML.
Especially in the high temperature range and also with use of improved equipment
which allows use of strain-controlled test procedure. A possible solution of such
high-Temperature Materials Testing equipment is oﬀed by MTS Systems, consisting
of complete testing solutions for heat elevated material testing for temperatures up
tp 1000◦C.
167
Jessen

Appendix A
S355ML Steel Test Specimens -
Manufacturer Information and
Cutting Procedure
The steel tested in the present work is steel grade S355 with Quality ML (S355ML).
This steel is the most frequently used construction steel within the oﬀshore sector
and therefor also the treated steel in the present work. It is estimated that 90% of
the structures is constructed in this material1.
S355ML is a thermomechanical (TM) rolled low-carbon ﬁne-grain steel after Euro-
pean standard DS/EN 10025-4.
+ EN 10025-1. The chemical composition (product analysis) of the steel plate is
reproduced from the inspection certiﬁed listed in Table A.1.
A steel plate of quality S355ML of the dimensions 3000mm x 1500mm was ordered
at Danish steel supplier Lemvigm¨uller. The manufacturer of the steel is KROEGER
STAAL B.V. and inspection certiﬁed by Voestalpine Grobblech gmbh. The certiﬁ-
cate can be found in the end of the current appendix.

Appendix B
Heat Elevated Monotonic Tensile
Test of S355ML
In order to reproduce numerical simulations that comply with reality, descent in-
put parameter is needed. In computational welding mechanics (CWM) especially
the material properties are of great importance. Such parameters can be obtained
through experimental testing. In connection with this project heat elevated mono-
tonic tensile test of S355ML has been performed.
The test is performed in compliance with DS-EN ISO 6892-2-2013 - Metallic material
- Tensile testing Part2: Method of test at elevated temperature [13] and DS-EN ISO
6892-1-2013 - Metallic material - Tensile testing Part1: Method of test at room
temperature [11].
A total of 25 test has been performed ranging with temperatures ranging from 20
◦C to 1000 ◦C.
In excess of the author, Stian Saupstad participated in the planning and execution
of the tests.
B.1
Test Specimen Geometry
The tested steel where of quality S355ML which is the preferred steel in welded
oﬀshore structures. The steel specimens origins from the steel plate described in
appendix A. The test specimen is water cut in order to avoid additional residual
stress. There must be assumed some residual stress due to the rolling process, but
the extent of these is unknown. These will also be present in a welded oﬀshore
structure such as monopiles made of hot rolled steel1.
1In case of neutron diﬀraction measurements of residual stresses, would these residual stresses
also be neglected due to the process of reference point determination.
186
==PAGE:198==
B.2. TEST CONDITIONS
The geometry of the test specimen complies with [13]. The geometry is illustrated in
Figure B.1 where the thickness is 10mm. The notation of the dimensions, analogous
to the Eurocode notation can be seen in Figure B.2. The test piece is made with
18mm holes in order to allow a bolted connection. This is preferred due to the fact
that a regular grip connection would suﬀer under the expansive heating process of
the test specimen. In addition, would a griped connection be impossible with the
test setup oﬀered on DTU, due to damage on equipment.
Figure B.1: Geometry of monotonic test specimen.
Figure B.2: Eurocode notation of temperature elevated tensile test specimen, Figure
from [13].
B.2
Test Conditions
The test procedure is carried out according to [13].
The zero force point was set after the testing equipment was assembled and before
the test piece was retained in the gripping jaws.
187
Jessen
==PAGE:199==
B.2. TEST CONDITIONS
The test piece was retained as recommend in [13], with a bolted connection.
The establishment of the extensometer gauge length is performed according to
Method 1 after [13]. Where the gauge length is established at room temperature
with nominal gauge length. The extension is measured at test temperature and the
percentage extension is calculated with gauge length at room temperature. This
method is chosen among many methods, due to the fact that the testing equipment
does not allow any other method to be used.
The nominal gauge length L0 is chosen to 50mm.
The heating procedure of [13] was only partly followed.
The test specimen was
heated to the speciﬁed temperature and held for at least 10min before loading (soak-
ing time). Unfortunately could the permissible temperature variation along the test
piece not by complied due to the insulation eﬃciency of the heating furnace, on
average, there where 8 ◦C between the top thermocouple and the lower thermocou-
ple. The permissible temperature variation along the test piece is listed in Figure
B.3.
Figure B.3: Permitted deviation between Ti and T and maximum temperature vari-
ation along the test piece, Table from [13].
The test is displacement controlled. The recommended strain rate for measuring
diﬀerent steel properties is illustrated in Figure B.4. Where ReH is the upper yield
strength, ReL is the lower yield strength, Rp is the proof strength, Rm is the tensile
strength and A is the elongation after fracture. The ranges of deﬁned as:
Rang 1 ˙ε= 0.00007 s−1
Rang 2 ˙ε= 0.00025 s−1
Rang 3 ˙ε= 0.0014 s−1
Rang 4 ˙ε= 0.0067 s−1
All with a relative tolerance of ±20%.
The chosen strain rate is range 1 for the ﬁrst part of the test and range 2 for the
second part (Rm, A and Z). The reason for not choosing range 3 in the second
part is that, at some point for elevated temperatures, the stress-strain curve gives
no clear indication of entering the plastic range as it does for t = 20 −200◦C, which
188
Jessen
==PAGE:200==
B.3. TEST SETUP
is illustrated in the results (B.19). This questions the ability to determine the point
where the strain rate must be changed.
By choosing range 2 instead of range 3, the con-
sequence of not choosing the exact right spot
is not severe, because range 2 is valid within
the whole test (if test range 1 was chosen for
the whole test, the test would take very long
time). However, Figure B.4 is reproduced di-
rectly from [11], which raise questions, if any
deeper consideration about the strain rate at
elevated temperatures has been made.
Originally it was intended to control test ma-
chines displacement of range 1 by the strain of
the extensometer (strain rate controlled) and
range 2 by the displacement rate of the piston
(displacement controlled), thus is the following
displacement rate of the piston applied:
˙d = ˙εLc = 0.00025s−185mm = 0.02125mm/s
(B.1)
However, after the ﬁrst tests, became it clear
that the test machine had some diﬃculties with
strain rate controlled approach. The test ma-
chine had a tendency to either overcompensate
or undercompensate in order to follow the pre-
scribed strain rate.
In order to prevent this
unwanted eﬀect, range 1 was changed to dis-
placement control.
This was done by analy-
sis of the displacement rate within the elastic
range of the already performed tests. A clear
trend was found and the displacement rate of
0.0059mm/s of range 2 was adapted.
Figure B.4: Allowable strain
rate, according to [13] for
method A if ReH, ReL, Rp,
Rm, A and Z are to be de-
termined, hatched area is rec-
ommended, Figure from [13].

Test Setup
In this section, the test setup is described. A photograph of the test setup can be
seen in Figure B.5 and Figure B.6. Schematic drawings of the setup are illustrated
in Figure B.13. The setup consists of the following equipment:

The grips, which consists of an end piece and a middle piece, which can be seen
in Figure B.7. These are specially produced for this project, in order to meet the
requirements of the connections after [13] and to insure a minimum heat transfer to
the hydraulic grip of the MTS universal test station, which has a maximum working
condition of 40◦C. At the top end piece, two aluminum cooling blocks is mounted
with cooling paste, in order to prevent overheating of the top hydraulic grip.
190
Jessen
==PAGE:202==
B.3. TEST SETUP
Figure B.7: Grips.
The displacement measurement mechanism that allows measurement of displace-
ment of the specimen within the furnace can be seen in Figure B.8 and Figure
B.9. The mechanism consists of two aluminum plates, which is weight reduced by
drilling and a pair of attachment grips. One grip is attached to one aluminum plate
independent of each other, which allow independent movement between the plates.
On the plates, displacement sensors is mounted (two in order to take into account
for possibly skewness) and on the side an instron extensometer with a maximum
travel of ±5mm as seen in Figure B.11. The grips are mounted to the test piece
with two cone socket screws (one on each side for each grip) in order to insure
knowledge about the attachment point of the grip. The upper and lower grip is
mounted 50mm apart on the test piece corresponding to the nominal gauge length
L0. The mounting mechanism seen in Figure Figure B.10 consists of a connection
made of disc’s and ceramic isolation material with a resilient eﬀect. This connection
remedies the compressional eﬀect of the cone screw so biaxial stress conditions is
reduced and counteracts transverse contractions under the test, due to Poisson’s
ratio, so the displacement measurement mechanism do not fall oﬀimmediately after
test start.

The heating device or the furnace is the brand Scandia Ovens A/S. It has a heating
limit of 1100◦C. The furnace is made of ceramic blocks capsuled by a stainless steel
cover. It is equipped with a temperature sensor which measure the temperature right
next to the heating element. It is programmable, in terms of target temperature,
heating slope and holding time.
Calibration
The extensometer, displacement sensors and force cell were calibrated before any
testing. The extensometer was calibrated with respect to the nominal gauge length
L0 = 50mm thus strain was monitored directly by it. The calibration device can
be seen in Figure B.15. The displacement sensors were calibrated with respect to
relative displacement. The sensor has a unique electrical resistance coeﬃcient, which
can be determined by linear regression between electrical output for diﬀerent known
displacements. The calibration process can be seen in Figure B.14. The force cell

B.4
Test Procedure
The test procedure is illustrated by a process map (BPMN-map) in Figure B.16.
The following explanation is associated with it:
• Test start: Datalogger is started
• MTS test machine is started by executing the test sequence program
• when the plastic range is reached, an execution bottom is triggered manually
and test range 2 speed is enabled
• when approximately 6% strain is reached the extensometer is dismounted from
the displacement measurement mechanism in order not to damage it
• when rupture is observed, the test machine is stopped and the datalogger is
stopped and data output is stored.
The data acquired during the test are listed Table B.2.

B.5.1
Comments to the Tests Execution
During the test it experienced that the displacement measurement mechanism fell
oﬀbefore reaching the speciﬁed strain of 6%, presumably due to contractions of
the test specimen in the transverse direction. This had consequences in test of test
piece 10 and 20 where the data was unable to be used. Despite this problem of
197
Jessen
==PAGE:209==
B.5. RESULTS
”detachment” the data was usable in most cases due to the detachment happened
when the plastic range, and the piston displacement measurements can be used by
superposition.
During tests at 800◦C and 1000◦C, the thermocouples were destroyed. After this
point temperature measurement was performed by laser thermometer. The laser
thermometer is of high quality and the focus point is 13mm in diameter. The mea-
surements were taken directly at the center of the test piece by opening the furnace
brieﬂy. After obtaining a satisfactorily temperature measurement, the furnace was
held, at least for 10 min in order to insure constant thermal conditions within the
furnace.
B.5.2
Processing of Results
According to DS-EN 1993-1-3 [18] it is recommended to perform at least 5 tests of
the same condition in order to obtain characteristic values. This recommendation
has not been obeyed in this investigation, for a number of reasons:
1. The time consumption would take an overwhelming dimension seen in the light
of the scope of this work. Each test is rather time consuming due to the heating
process (approximately 600 man-hours has been used in this investigation,
without counting time used on data processing)
2. It is assumed that the tests will ”support” each other, meaning that the trend
experienced in a diﬀerent test conditions, i.e. temperatures, will give an indi-
cation on if the obtained result is reasonable, or more test has to be performed.
3. The result is not intended to be used as characteristic values
The test results were processed in order to obtain the desired stress-strain curves.
The Stress σ was calculated by use of (B.2), where F is the force and A0 is the
measured cross-sectional area, listed in Table A.3.
σ = F/A0
,
A0 = b0a0
(B.2)
The strain ε, which is the relative change in length was calculated from (B.3) where
ΔL is the change in length and L0 is the gauge length.
ε = ΔL/L0
(B.3)
The measurement of the two displacement sensors was averaged in order to com-
pensate for any skewness in the measurement device and 5 mm was used for L0,
whereas the measurement of the extensometer was calibrated to output strain mea-
surements directly to the system manager. In order to obtain strain from the piston
displacement measurement a length of 80 mm was used for L0, corresponding to the
parallel length Lc in Figure B.2.
On Figure B.17 (left) the raw data for specimen 21 is plotted. By observing the
ﬁgure it is seen, that the extensometer and displacement sensor follow the same
198
Jessen
==PAGE:210==
B.5. RESULTS
path, which was expected. The best results were obtained from the displacement
sensors, whereas the extensometer had some skewness inherited from the placement
on the measurement device, therefor the data from the displacement sensors is used
preferable to the extensometer measurements.
For the stress-strain relation based on piston displacement measurements it is seen
that the slope in the elastic regime is much more moderate. This is an expression
for displacement within the testing machine and the specially made grips.
It is also observed that when the curve enters the plastic regime, the slope of the
displacement sensors/extensometer and strain based on piston displacement follow
the same slope, which is an indication on, that when plastic deformations initiates
most of the displacement is occurring within the parallel length Lc. This informa-
tion is extremely useful information for obtaining complete and correct stress-strain
curves, especially when the measurement device, supplying displacement sensor and
extensometer data only operates within ±10%. In addition, it was experienced that
the measurement device simply fell oﬀfor greater strain values due to material con-
tractions, which follows from Poisson’s relation2.
By having this information in mind the stress-strain curve based on the displacement
sensors and the stress-strain curve based on the piston displacement be matched by
moving the latter to an appropriate strain value. This has been achieved for all 25
trails. In addition has the stress-strain curve has been smoothed in order to discard
unwanted small ﬂuctuations3. The matched/merged curve and the ﬁnal ”smoothed”
curve for specimen 21 can be seen in Figure B.18.
This process has been performed on all tests and the ﬁnal stress-strain curves for
all tests can be seen in Figure B.19.

B.5. RESULTS
Figure B.19 illustrates the obtained stress-strain curves using engineering strain,
meaning the expression (B.3) is used directly. This expression is valid for only small
values of strain (up to approximately 5% [16]), for greater values the true/logarith-
mic strain should be applied, which is valid within the whole spectra. True strain
takes the reduction of cross sectional area into consideration, which inevitably will
happen as the specimen is elongated. The engineering strain and corresponding
stress are transfered to true using the following:
εt = ln(1 + ε)
,
σt = σ(1 + ε)
(B.4)
The true stress-strain curves is illustrated in Figure B.20.
By observing Figure B.19 it is seen that the plastic range initiates at decreasing
levels of stress, for increasing temperatures without exception. This trend is not
completely followed when looking at the maximum reached stress or tensile strength
Rm. It is observed that Rm generally decrease with increasing temperature. How-
ever, for tests performed at 200◦C and 300◦C the value of Rm increases. The highest
values was observed for 300◦C which obtains values greater than observed at room
temperature (20◦C).
On the two curves for 600◦C it is observed that the curves have a ”hump” after
entering the plastic range. This eﬀect is not due to material properties, but due to
late shift from test range 1 speed to test range 2 speed. This ”hump” is coped by
applying the well known power ﬁt - Ramberg–Osgood Fit.

Modulus of Elasticity
The process of obtaining values of E-modulus is illustrated in Figure B.17 (right),
which is a zoom on the elastic range of the data for specimen 21. The E-modulus is
obtained by making a linear regression ﬁt4 within an, manually selected, appropriate
range.
The obtained values of E-modules, including mean and standard deviation is plot-
ted as a function of temperature in Figure B.21 together with recommended values
according to DS-EN 1993-1-2 [19]. The ﬁgure on the left contains all the obtained
values, whereas the ﬁgure on the left contains the ﬁnal values where obvious wrongly
values is omitted, namely specimen 7, which is assumed incorrect due to well estab-
lished information on E-modulus for steel at 20◦C.
By observing the ﬁgure it is seen that the obtained E-modulus have rather good
correlation with the trend of the Eurocode recommendations. The biggest deviation
is present at 400◦C and it is also noted that there is a rather large spread of the
results for 500◦C. The ﬁnal mean values of E-modulus together with the standard
deviation is listed in Table B.5.

Figure B.21: E-modulus as a function of temperature, unsorted (left) and sorted
(right), the mean value is marked by a green circle.
Yield Strength
The yield strength is the stress at which the material begins to behave plastic. The
task of establishing this point can be quite comprehensive, because plastic strains is
accumulated within the range deﬁned as elastic (and vise versa). This phenomenon
is illustrated in Figure B.22 where point 1, denotes the true elastic limit where no
plastic strain is accumulated. Due to this material behavior the yield limit is often
established relative to a certain strain level, where it is assumed that the majority
4The standard function ”regression” is used within MATLAB
202
Jessen
==PAGE:214==
B.5. RESULTS
of strain after this point will be plastic. The by far most used form procedure to
establish the yield strength is the 0.2% proof stress or oﬀset yield point (Rp0.2),
which also is the formulation used in Eurocode [18]. The 0.2% oﬀset yield point is
illustrated in Figure B.22 (point 4). The yield strength is established by ﬁnding the
intersection between the stress strain curve and the 0.2% oﬀset of the slope of the
established E-modulus.
This approach has been used to establish the yield strength. The process is illus-
trated in Figure B.23 and the established values of Rp0.2 is illustrated in Figure B.24
together with the yield strength calculated by using the approach recommended in
[19]. The mean values of Rp0.2 together with the corresponding standard deviation
is listed in Table B.5.
Figure B.22: Stress-strain curve with 0.2% oﬀset yield strength/proof stress: 1: True
elastic limit, 2: Proportionality limit, 3: Elastic limit, 4: Oﬀset yield strength, from
[68].

Tensile Strength
The tensile strength Rmor the neckling point is the maximum stress experienced
during the test. This value is simply established by locating the maximum stress
during the test.

Appendix C
Heat Elevated Cyclic Test of
S355ML
In order to reproduce numerical simulations that comply with reality, descent in-
put parameter is needed. In computational welding mechanics (CWM) especially
the material properties are of great importance. Such parameters can be obtained
through experimental testing. In connection with this project heat elevated cyclic
test of S355ML has been performed.
The general test conditions are determined using ”A Code of Practice for the deter-
mination of cyclic stress-strain data” [29].
Some practices recommended in DS-EN ISO 6892-2-2013 - Metallic material - Ten-
sile testing Part2: Method of test at elevated temperature [13] was applied e.g.
heating procedure and establishment of gauge length, because no equivalent recom-
mendation is available for heat elevated cyclic tests.
A total of 7 test has been performed with temperatures ranging from 20 ◦C to 850
◦C.
C.1
Test Specimen Geometry
The tested steel where of quality S355ML which is the preferred steel in welded
oﬀshore structures. The steel specimens origins from the steel plate described in
appendix A. The test specimen is water cut in order to avoid additional residual
stress. There must be assumed some residual stress due to the rolling process but
the extent of these is unknown. These will also be present in an welded oﬀshore
structure such as monopiles made of hot rolled steel1.
1In case of neutron diﬀraction measurements of residual stresses, would these residual stresses
also be neglected due to the process of reference point determination.
207
==PAGE:219==
C.2. TEST CONDITIONS
The geometry is in accordance with ASTM E606 [4]. The geometry is illustrated in
Figure C.1 where the thickness is 10mm. The notation of the dimensions, analogous
to the Eurocode notation can be seen in Figure C.2. The test piece is made with
18mm holes in order to allow a bolted connection. This is preferred due to the fact
that a regular grip connection would suﬀer under the expansive heating process of
the test specimen. In addition would a griped connection be impossible with the
test setup oﬀered on DTU, due to damage on equipment.
Figure C.1: Geometry of cyclic test specimen.
Figure C.2: Eurocode notation of temperature elevated tensile test specimen, Figure
from [13].
C.2
Test Conditions
The zero force point was set after the testing equipment was assembled and before
the test piece was retained in the gripping jaws.
208
Jessen
==PAGE:220==
C.2. TEST CONDITIONS
The test piece was retained with a bolted friction connection, consisting of two cus-
tom made grips.
The establishment of the extensometer gauge length is performed according to
Method 1 after [13]. Where the gauge length is established at room temperature
with nominal gauge length. The extension is measured at test temperature and the
percentage extension is calculated with gauge length at room temperature. This
method is chosen among many methods, due to the fact that the testing equipment
do not allow any other method to be used.
The nominal gauge length L0 is chosen to 25mm.
The heating procedure of [13] was only partly followed.
The test specimen was
heated to the speciﬁed temperature and held for prescribed 10 min before loading
(soaking time). Unfortunately could the permissible temperature variation along
the test piece not by complied due to an excessive heating loss though out the grips,
in average there where 30 ◦C between the measurements from the top thermocouple
and the lower thermocouple. The permissible temperature variation along the test
piece is listed in Figure C.3.
Figure C.3: Permitted deviation between Ti and T and maximum temperature
variation along the test piece, Table from [13].
The general test conditions in terms of total strain range Δεt and loading frequency
are determined using ”A Code of Practice for the determination of cyclic stress-
strain data” [29].
The practice recommended in the code of practice relies greatly on the use of the
experimental data i.e.
the test should be performed under similar conditions in
which the data should be used in order to simulate realistic material response. [29]
supply Table C.1 for guidance on test choice.
209
Jessen
==PAGE:221==
C.3. TEST SETUP
Table C.1: Guidance on which tests should be performed to elucidate particular
aspects of the response, from [29].
The aim of the experiment is to determine material characteristic for use in low
cycle fatigue, namely multi-pass welding simulations which involves reversed strain
range (R = −1) and relatively large deformations O(εmax = 0.12).
The applied test parameters is chosen based partly on values used in similar exper-
iments [29, 6] and estimates on what capabilities the experimental setup was cable
of.
The total strain range Δεt is chosen to 1.4% yielding stress range of ±0.7%, which
allows to capture a relatively large portion of the plastic strain range. The strain
rate is chosen to 0.0125%/s which gives a total time of 14s for each load cycle or a
frequency of 0.07143s−1. This stress range and rate is in compliance with low cycle
fatigue (LCF) testing regime (failure within 102 −104 cycles) [50].
Strain controlled test is recommended, in order to obtain stabilized hysteresis loops
which the material parameters should be based on [29]. However this option is not
possible due to limitations of the preciseness of measurement equipment, which is
outlined in Appendix B, section B.2. In stead displacement control is used. The
displacement range which is used for the setup is derived from preliminary strain con-
trolled tests. It is found that a displacement of ±0.62273mm equals strain of ±0.7%.
The testing time is chosen to 20min which corresponds to 85 load cycles.
C.3
Test Setup
In this section, the test setup is described.
A photograph of the test setup can be seen in Figure C.4 and Figure C.5. Schematic
drawings of the setup is illustrated in Figure C.12. The setup consists of the following
equipment:
210
Jessen
==PAGE:222==
C.3. TEST SETUP
• MTS312.31 universal test station
• Heating furnace
• Datalogger
• MTS system manager
• Enstron extensometer ±5mm
• Displacement sensors x 2
• Temperature measurement devises x 2
• grip, x 2
• Cooling fan x 2
• Ceramic insulation material
• High temperature gloves
• Aluminum cooling block x 3
• Displacement measurement mechanism
Figure C.4: Test setup: test machine
MTS312.31,
heating
furnace,
dis-
placement measurement mechanism,
temperature monitors, cooling fans.
Figure C.5: View from inside of heat-
ing furnace:
displacement measure-
ment mechanism, grips, placement of
test specimen.
The grips, which have a cross-section of 40mm x 40mm, can be seen in Figure C.6.
These are specially produced for this project, in order to make cyclic heat elevated
tests possible with the provided testing equipment. The purpose with these grips,
is to keep the temperature of the jaws below 40◦C which is the maximum working
condition. At the top and bottom grip, respectively two and one aluminum cooling
block is mounted with cooling paste, in order to prevent overheating of the top
hydraulic jaw.
The specimen is ﬁxed in the grips with a friction connection, which was produced
according to EC 3[20]. The quality of the used bolts was 12.9 which is within the
requirements and yields a pretension force of 117 kN corresponding to a tightening
moment of 355 Nm. The ﬁxation of the test specimen was made on a straight steel
table in order to minimize misalignments.
211
Jessen
==PAGE:223==
C.3. TEST SETUP
Figure C.6: Grips.
The displacement measurement mechanism that allows measurement of displace-
ment of the specimen within the furnace can be seen in Figure C.7 and Figure
C.8. The mechanism consists of two aluminum palates, which is weight reduced by
drilling and a pair of attachment grips. One grip is attached to one aluminum plate
independent of each other, which allow independent movement between the plates.
On the plates displacement sensors is mounted (two in order to take into account for
possibly skewness) and on the side an instron extensometer with a maximum travel
of ±5mm as seen in Figure C.10. The grips is mounted to the test piece by two cone
socket screws (one on each side for each grip) in order to insure knowledge about the
attachment point of the grip. The upper and lower grip is mounted 25mm apart on
the test piece corresponding to the nominal gauge length L0. The mounting mecha-
nism seen in Figure C.9 consists of a connection made of disc’s and ceramic isolation
material with an resilient eﬀect. This connection remedies the compressional eﬀect
of the cone screw so biaxial stress conditions is reduced and counter acts transverse
contractions under the test, due to Poisson’s ratio so the displacement measurement
mechanism do not fall of after test start.
Figure C.7: Displacement measurement
mechanism (width).

mechanism (length).

The heating device or the furnace is the brand Scandia Oven A/S. It has a heating
limit of 1100◦C. The furnace is made of ceramic blacks capsuled by a stainless steel
cover. It is equipped with a temperature sensor which measure the temperature right
next to the heating element. It is programmable, in terms of target temperature,
heating slope and holding time.
Calibration
The extensometer, displacement sensors and force cell was calibrated before any
testing. The extensometer was calibrated with respect to the nominal gauge length
L0 = 25mm thus strain was monitored directly by it. The calibration device can be
seen in Figure C.14. The displacement sensors was calibrated with respect to relative
displacement. The sensor has an unique electrical resistance coeﬃcient which can
be determined by linear regression between electrical output for diﬀerent known
displacements. The calibration process can be seen in Figure C.13. The force cell

As seen in the table, the planned test listed in Table C.4 was not performed. The
reason for this was a combination of time consumption and furnace performance.
For temperatures above 300◦C, did the friction connection loss the pretension force,
hence the connection was unusable. In order to accommodate this, the specimens
for temperatures above 300◦C was welded directly to the grips as seen in Figure
C.16. Although this operation induces a highe amount of heat into the specimen, is
it assumed that it will not aﬄict the mechanical properties of the parallel length of
the test specimen. As seen in Table C.5 was the highest successful performed test
at 850◦C. In order to obtain this temperature, the furnace temperature was set to
maximum (1100◦C), hence test temperature above 850◦C was not capable with the
test setup. It is also noted that the temperature diﬀerence between the upper and
lower measurement is above the allowable diﬀerence, however this is ignored and the
original target temperatures are related to the respective tests. The results, in terms
of cyclic stress-strain hysteresis curves is illustrated on the following page.

C.5. RESULTS
The strain measurements is based on the displacement measurements from the dis-

The test at 20◦C was performed with a diﬀerent procedure compared to the rest.
The test was carried out by attachment directly into the jaws of the test machine
and the extensometer was attached directly onto the specimen. The test was still
performed displacement controlled in order to be comparable with the remaining
tests. The displacement length was, derived by a preliminary test to be correspond-
ing to the strain range ±0.7%. In addition, was this test cycled to rupture, thus
only hysteresis loops corresponding to 20min are shown in the previous ﬁgure.
The complete test consisting of 704 cycles can be seen in Figure C.17. The onset
of crack propagation is approximated, from the stress-time curve, to occur at 530
cycles. The test specimen after complete test can be seen in Figure C.18 showing
the crack at end stage.

By observing the results on page 218, it is clear that the result of test 600◦C and
800◦C is relatively diﬀerent from the remaining tests. It was also experienced that
after ended test, had the displacement measurement devise detached itself from the
219
Jessen
==PAGE:231==
C.5. RESULTS
specimen2. This means that the strain measurements of these test is questionable,
and it can be discussed if the results is usable for further investigation. However
these results is used, due to lack of better result.
It is also observed that the most smooth curves is observed for the test at 20◦C
which where the test performed with extensometer attached directly onto the test
specimen. However the tests at 100 −400◦C the hysteresis loop has comparable
shape and the obtained maximum strains is in the same order of magnitude.
On Figure C.19, Figure C.20, Figure C.21, Figure C.22 and Figure C.23 is the
strain and stress plotted as a function of time, for test 20◦C, 100◦C, 200◦C, 300◦C
and 400◦C, respectively. Test 600◦C and 800◦C are omitted due to the uncertainties
present in these tests. By observing the stress as a function of time (upper and lower
peaks) it is seen that a small amount cyclic softening is present (with the exception
of test at 400◦C) in the order 2.5% from maximum stress to stabilized cycle. For
the test at 400◦C some cyclic hardening is present also in the order of 2.5%3.
By observing the strains as a function of the time it is seen that the strains are
shifting to one ore the other side (positive or negative) as the specimen is cycled.
This indicates that the test specimen has ratcheting nature. Normally this phe-
nomenon is only said to occur at force-controlled test, referring to that there exists
strain-controlled and force-controlled test, however in displacement controlled test
the strains is not restricted to a speciﬁed interval, thus ratcheting is possible 4.

C.5.1
Stabilized Hysteresis Cycle
The stabilized hysteresis cycle is established when the stable conditions is observed
i.e. no signiﬁcant change in shape of the loop. The determination of the stabilized
hysteresis cycle is marked with red on the results on page 218. However for the test
at 600◦C and 850◦C no stabilized cycle was observed, probably due to the issues
regarding the displacement measurement device, as described in previous section.
Consequently, a cycle located early in the test is chosen, as it assumed this is the
most representative for the real behavior.
The stabilized cycles is illustrated in
Figure C.24 (left). By observing the ﬁgure it is seen that the strains for test at
20◦C −400◦C lies within the same range, however test at 100◦C and ◦C has slightly
higher strain range Δεt = 1.6% relative to the target range Δεt = 1.4%. For test
at 600◦C and 850◦C it is seen that strain range is considerable higher, this could be
an indication of erroneous strain measurements, however when the strength of the
material is decreased, it is assumed that the strain will increase in some degree.
The determination of the module of elasticity is based on the established stable
cycles. The E-modulus is calculated from both the compressing and tensile elastic
range, as recommended in [29]. The process of locating the linear elastic range is
based on visual considerations. The data point which are used for the linear re-
gression is illustrated in Figure C.24 (right) and the derived values of E-modulus
is illustrated in Figure C.25 and listed in Table C.6. By observing Figure C.25 is
is observed that the derived E-modulus do not perfectly correlated with the recom-
mendations from Eurocode [19] and the results observed at monotonic tension test
of same material (see Appendix B), however the decreasing trend with increasing
temperature is observed.
The yield strength, established after the 0.2% oﬀset procedure is listed in Table
222

conservative yield stress 0.55 · Rp0.2 which is derived by AREVA GmbH Erlangen in
connecting with calibration of Chaboche material models [33] is listed there as well
together with the corresponding yield strain.

Appendix D
Laser Hybrid Welding
In connection with a visit at LORC, a test facility for large oﬀshore structures,
an oﬀer of a laser-hybrid (LH) test welding was generously oﬀered by Steen Erik
Nielsen, head af welding innovation at Force Technology.
The test was performed at Lindoe Wielding Technology (LWT) where a newly es-
tablished setup of LH equipment is present. LWT is a joint venture between LORC
and Force Technology with the main purpose of disseminate and test LH technology.
In excess of the author, Kim Kristiansen participated in the welding experiment.
D.1
Test Setup
The LH welding equipment consists of one setup of a welding robot connected with
one MAG welding equipment and two 16 kW lasers which can combined to one laser,
which is approximately equivalent to a 32 kW laser (see Figure D.1).
Figure D.1: LH welding setup on robot (left), 2 x 16 kW laser generators (right).
225
==PAGE:237==
D.1. TEST SETUP
The LH conﬁguration can be seen in Figure D.2, where MAG is trailing the laser
beam.
aseream
M
ticot

Figure D.2: LH welding conﬁguration.
There test weldings was decided to be performed. The test specimens consist of two
10mm steel plate of quality S355ML with a 480 mm welding path joined by two
end plates (80mm x 40mm). The geometry is illustrated in Figure D.3 and Figure
D.4.
The steel plate which the test specimens origins is described in Appendix
A. The welding direction is parallel to the steel plate rolling direction, which also
coincide with the longitudinal direction of the circumferential welding of monopiles
foundations.
The test specimens is mainly water cut, except for the 15o angle chamfer, which is
cut by a milling machine.

==PAGE:238==
D.1. TEST SETUP
Figure D.5: Placement of holes for thermocouples on left and right side of the weld,
N.B. welding direction is from bottom of Figure to top fo Figure.
Each welding is monitored by 16 thermocouples mounted after the pattern illus-
trated in Figure D.5 and the distance to the center of the weld is listed in Table D.1.
Holes of 1.5mm is drilled, so the tip of the thermocouples is placed in the middle of
the plate, at a depth of 5mm. The hole oﬀers a tight ﬁt of the thermocouples and
no additional fastening was needed. The thermocouples was of type ”K” and was
connected to a datalogger for data collection.
The preparation of the welding cross-section was proposed by Steen Erik Nilsen on
the basis of the only available welding procedure speciﬁcation for that particular LH
equipment, which is developed at LWT for 25 mm steel plate thickness.
The procedure speciﬁes that a MAG welding is produced before joining the plate
with the LH welding from the opposite side. The purpose of making a MAG welding
prior to LH welding is mainly of safety reasons. If no MAG welding was present,
there is a risk of the laser beam could penetrate all the way through the plate, and
cause collateral damage.

D.3
Test Procedure
Three test welding specimens was produced, of which each test specimen was welded
in two turns - one MAG welding and one LH welding. Yielding a total of 6 welds.
In order to insure cooling to ambient temperature between the MAG and LH weld-
ing, all MAG weldings was performed ﬁrst followed by all LH weldings. In Table
D.4 the sequence and time of measurement is listed.

The welding procedure was performed by a skilled welder with more than 10 year
experience with laser welding and robotics. For each welding, the welding path was
programmed by deﬁning an start point and and end point in three dimensions. This
also insured that the focus point and hight of the MAG was at the right position at
all time. On Figure D.6 is the schematic welding procedure illustrated. On Figure

the placement of the thermocouples.

Appendix E
Submerged Arc Welding
In order to model the heat source for a particular submerge arc welding (SAW)
wheat input data was needed. Such data, in terms of temperature measurements
and cross section macrograph is produced through test welding.
Three 2 pass SAW test welds of a 10mm plate was produced at Force Technol-
ogy.
Test participants: Stian Saupstad and Kim Kristiansen.
E.1
Test Setup
The test setup consists of a automatic SAW machine illustrated in Figure E.2 and
a close up on the contact tube which leads the feeding wire to the melting pool is
illustrated in Figure E.3. An Schematic illustration of a SAW is illustrated in Figure
E.1.
Figure E.1: Schematic illustration of SAW, Figure from [5]

==PAGE:246==
E.1. TEST SETUP
Figure E.2: SAW equipment setup.
Figure E.3: closeup on contact tube.
The geometry ot the test specimens consist of two 10mm steel plate of quality
S355ML with a 480 mm welding path joined by two end plates (80mm x 40mm).
The geometry is illustrated in Figure E.4 and Figure E.5 The distance between the
two plates which are joined is 3mm at the bottom. The steel plate which the test
specimens origins is described in Appendix A. The welding direction is parallel to
the steel plate rolling direction, which also coincide with the longitudinal direction
of the circumferential welding of monopiles foundations.
The test specimens is mainly water cut, except for the 35o angle chamfer, which is
cut by a milling machine.

The 4mm feeding wire is of type ESAB OK Autrod 12.22. The ﬂux powder is of type

E.3. TEST PROCEDURE
E.3
Test Procedure
For each test specimen the following procedure is followed
1. Pass 1, datalogger is started at onset of weld.
2. Pass 2 started after 17min of test duration, yielding a 940s inter pass time
3. data logging is stopped after approximately 50 min
The actual welding process for test 1 can be seen in Figure E.9 and the ﬁnished
pass 1 weld can be seen in Figure E.10 where the weld slag haven’t been removed
yet.
Figure E.9: Back plate welding.
Figure E.10: Test setup.
E.4
Results
Three SAW tests has been performed and time series of temperature measurements
of the heat dissemination has been logged.
Unfortunately, thermocouple 1 was placed to close to the weld and became inoper-
ative during test 1 and the data from thermocouple 1 is therefore not present in the
results. Due to operator error, must it be noted that the inter pass time for test 3 do
not follow the prescribed procedure. The onset of pass 2 was considerably delayed
which also is displayed by the results.
The time series containing the temperature measurements of test 1, 2 and 3 is il-
lustrated in Figure E.11, Figure E.11 and Figure E.11, respectively. The time of all
the curves has been manipulated so the presented curves represent placement of all
the thermocouples in one straight line perpendicular to the welding in the centerline
(240mm). This have been achieved be knowledge of the placement of the thermo-
couples and the travel speed. The maximum temperature for each pass for each
test is listed in Table E.4. From one of the pre-tests a macrography of the welded
cross-section has been produced, illustrated in Figure E.14. In order to make the
heat aﬀected zone to stand out 2% sulfuric acid (H2SO4) has been applied to the
weld area.

Appendix F
FEAT-WMT
FEAT is a semi-commercial FEM software developed by FeatPlus Limited on the
behalf of British Energy and Magnox Limited, who run all the UK’s commercial
nuclear power stations. FEAT covers analysis in Computational Fluid Dynamics,
Thermal Analysis and Stress Analysis and is only available in UNIX/LINUX plat-
forms.
FEAT-WMT is a weld modeling tool based on FEAT macros which sole purpose is
to investigate and calibrate heat sources for use in FE weld modeling. The motiva-
tion to use this is that FEAT-WMT models the thermal ﬁeld as a 3D steady-state
problem, i.e. the material moving a constant speed past a stationary source. This
analysis runs much faster than a 3D transient analysis, which is oﬀered by normal
commercial FEM software such as Abaqus. This also means that the tool only ap-
plicable for welds with a suﬃcient length so starting and stopping eﬀects can be
neglected, which is the case for welds covered in this thesis.
The software allows calibration of the heat source based on experimental data: mea-
sured temperature history of thermocouples and fusion boundary trace. Several heat
source models are available in: ellipsoid heat source, Goldak double ellipsoid, cone
source. Also the eﬀects of heat weaving and multi-wire can be implemented into the
analysis.
After calibration, the heat source parameters can be used in an external FE code,
such as Abaqus. FEAT-WMT also oﬀer facilities to export the heat source as time
slice output ﬁles which can be implemented to Abaqus through Fortran subroutines.
In addition FEAT-WMT can convert the 3D moving-torch source for simpliﬁed mod-
els such as 2D or antisymmetric idealization, or a 3D lumped bead model [62].
The normal procedure for performing a heat source analysis in FEAT-WMT is
to implement a suitable 2D or 3D mesh of the weld, containing material element
information which can distinguish parent and the diﬀerent beads. In case of im-
plementation of 2D mesh FEAT-WMT will automatically grow a 3D mesh in the
longitudinal direction. An input macro is manually constructed whit all the needed
input information. Key information are:
• Thermal material properties
242
==PAGE:254==
F.1. PROCEDURE OF HEAT SOURCE PARAMETER ESTIMATION WITH
FEAT-WMT
• Heat source parameters: torch speed, current, potential diﬀerence, heat source
model and source parameters
• Placement of temperature measurements analogous to the placement of the
thermocouples in the experimental setup
• Experimental calibration data
When the FE model is constructed, a 3D steady analysis and a 2D transient analysis
can be performed and the temperature history and fusion boundary trace can be
compared whit experimental results.
When suﬃciently accurate results are obtained, the heat source can be imple-
mented to external FE-code for further investigation, in this case structural thermo-
mechanical analysis for predicting residual stresses.
F.1
Procedure of Heat Source Parameter Estima-
tion with FEAT-WMT
For illustrative purpose of showing the capabilities of FEAT-WMT, is the following
case has been produced:
In an earlier study [30] was a on-pass butt-weld performed on a 10mm thick steel
plate. The welding parameters used in the weld are listed in Table F.1.
Table F.1: SAW parameters example: one pass, one sided butt-weld.
Transient temperature history measurement was measured at the center of the weld,
and cross sectional macrography was produced.
On the basis of the geometry of the weld a appropriate mesh was produced and a
3D steady and 2D transient analysis was carried out using a Goldak double ellipsoid
heat source.
By warring the heat source parameters, acceptable results in terms of temperature
measurements and fusion boundary trajectory. The obtained heat source parame-
ters, temperature history (TC 1) and fusion boundary trajectory are given in Table
F.2, Figure F.1 and Figure F.2, respectively.

Appendix G
Estimating Heat Source
Parameters of Validation Case
The distributed heat source of the validation case has been modeled by use of FEAT-
WMT which main application is outlined in Appendix F. Two heat source models
has been investigated, the ellipsoid heat source model and Goldak heat source model.
The applied mesh of the 3D steady-state FE problem can be seen on Figure G.1.
The software generates a 3D mesh by grooving the illustrated mesh geometry in the
out of plane direction. An enlarged illustration of the more dense mesh close to the
weld bead and the placement of the thermocouple measurements can be seen on
Figure G.2.
Figure G.1: Mesh geometry.
Figure G.2: Placement of thermocouples.
The determination of appropriate heat source parameters is performed by manual
iteration by varying the geometry of the heat source and eﬃciency. This done by
calling an input macro containing the run information i.e. mesh geometry, heat
245
==PAGE:257==
source parameters (geometry, eﬃciency, current, voltage, torch speed), symmetry
plane, thermocouples placement, boundary conditions (ﬁlm and radiation), liqui-
dation temperature, thermal properties (conductivity, speciﬁc heat, density, latent
heat), experimental thermocouples measurements data, fusion body trajectory (if
any).
The input macro scripts for running the heat model analysis for ellipsoid and Goldak
heat source can be seen in appendix I.1 and I.2, respectively.
The thermal properties applied is corresponding to the properties described in sec-
tion 6.4.1. However, the software does not oﬀer capabilities of applying diﬀerent
thermal boundary conditions on diﬀerent parts of the model, thus an overall emis-
sivity of 0.7 and an overall ﬁlm coeﬃcient of 5.77 has been adapted.
G.0.1

comparison with the experimental temperature ﬁeld and assumed fusion boundary
trajectory (no cross-section of the weld was present in [30]). The initial values af the
shape parameters is based on [62] and [24] for ellipsoid and Goldak double ellipsoid,
respectively.

a more precise representation of SAW [3] is it chosen to use the Goldak heat source
model in the simulation of case 1 where Dﬂux implementation is used.

Appendix H
Dﬂux User Subroutine -
Implementation Guide and
Scripts
H.1
Dﬂux Subroutine - Modeling Nonuniform Dis-
tributed Flux in Abaqus
Dﬂux is used to deﬁne a nonuniform distributed ﬂux as a function of position, time,
temperature, element number, integration point number, etc. in a heat transfer or
mass diﬀusion analysis [2]. This is particularly useful in CWM where the shape of
the temperature ﬁeld is nonuniform.
The subroutines in Abaqus is all written within Fortran language, this implies that
a Fortran compiler, such as the Intel R
⃝compiler 16.0 with Fortran is implemented
into Abaqus so it is called within Abaqus. Secondly the compiler should be set
up to run with a writing environment such as Microsoft Visual Studio (2012). An
implementation guide of Dﬂux and the scripts which is used to generate heat sources
in the FE simulations is found in Appendix H.
H.2
Dﬂux Subroutine Implementation Guide
The subroutine is written within Fortran language, this implies that an Fortran
compiler, such as the Intel R
⃝compiler 16.0 with Fortran must be implemented into
Abaqus, so it is called within Abaqus. Secondly the compiler should be setup to
run with an writing environment e.g. Microsoft Visual Studio (2012). Guides for
the procedure of implementing these prerequisites can be found on the Internet.
The Dﬂux is enabled in Abaqus by applying a user deﬁned ”Body heat ﬂux” load
to the model. By doing this, Abaqus will inform that a Dﬂux subroutine needs to
249
==PAGE:261==
H.2. DFLUX SUBROUTINE IMPLEMENTATION GUIDE
be attached to the job.
The attachment is made by opening the thermal job and toggling to the ”General”
tab. Within this tab, the navigation path of the Dﬂux subroutine script can be
deﬁned.
The Dﬂux subroutine script has the following initial design:
SUBROUTINE DFLUX(FLUX,SOL,KSTEP,KINC,TIME,NOEL,NPT,COORDS,
1 JLTYP,TEMP,PRESS,SNAME)
C
INCLUDE ’ABA_PARAM.INC’
C
DIMENSION FLUX(2), TIME(2), COORDS(3)
CHARACTER*80 SNAME
user coding to define FLUX(1) and FLUX(2)
RETURN
END
On which [2] gives the following description:
Variables to be deﬁned:
FLUX(1)
Magnitude of ﬂux ﬂowing into the model at this point. In heat transfer cases the
units are JT –1L–2 for surface ﬂuxes and JT –1L–3 for body ﬂux. In transient heat
transfer cases where a non-default amplitude is used to vary the applied ﬂuxes, the
time average ﬂux over the time increment must be deﬁned rather than the value
at the end of the time increment. In mass diﬀusion cases the units are PLT –1 for
surface ﬂuxes and PT –1 for body ﬂux.
FLUX(1) will be passed into the routine as the magnitude of the ﬂux speciﬁed as
part of the element-based or surface-based ﬂux deﬁnition. If the magnitude is not
deﬁned, FLUX(1) will be passed in as zero.
This ﬂux is not available for output purposes.
FLUX(2)
250
Jessen
==PAGE:262==
H.2. DFLUX SUBROUTINE IMPLEMENTATION GUIDE
In heat transfer cases: dq/dθ , the rate of change of the ﬂux with respect to the tem-
perature at this point. The units are JT –1L–2θ−1 for surface ﬂuxes and JT –1L–3θ−1
for body ﬂux.
In mass diﬀusion cases: dq/dc , the rate of change of the ﬂux with respect to the
mass concentration at this point. The units are LT −1 for surface ﬂuxes and T −1 for
body ﬂux.
The convergence rate during the solution of the nonlinear equations in an increment
is improved by deﬁning this value, especially when the ﬂux is a strong function of
temperature in heat transfer analysis or concentration in mass diﬀusion analysis.
Variables passed in for information:
SOL
Estimated value of the solution variable (temperature in a heat transfer analysis or
concentration in a mass diﬀusion analysis) at this time at this point.
KSTEP
Step number.
KINC
Increment number.
TIME(1)
Current value of step time (deﬁned only in transient analysis).
TIME(2)
Current value of total time (deﬁned only in transient analysis).
NOEL
251
Jessen
==PAGE:263==
H.2. DFLUX SUBROUTINE IMPLEMENTATION GUIDE
Element number.
NPT
Integration point number in the element or on the element’s surface. The integration
scheme depends on whether this is a surface or a body ﬂux.
COORDS
An array containing the coordinates of this point. These are the current coordi-
nates if geometric nonlinearity is accounted for during the step (see “Deﬁning an
analysis,” Section 6.1.2 of the Abaqus Analysis User’s Guide); otherwise, the array
contains the original coordinates of the point.
JLTYP
Identiﬁes the ﬂux type for which this call to DFLUX is being made. The ﬂux type
may be a body ﬂux, a surface-based ﬂux, or an element-based surface ﬂux. For
element-based surface ﬂuxes, this variable identiﬁes the element face for which this
call to DFLUX is being made. This information is useful when several diﬀerent
nonuniform distributed ﬂuxes are being imposed on an element at the same time.
See Part VI, “Elements,” of the Abaqus Analysis User’s Guide for element face iden-
tiﬁcation. The key is as follows:
JLTYP
Flux type
0 Surface-based ﬂux
1 BFNU
11 S1NU (SNEGNU for heat transfer shells)
12 S2NU (SPOSNU for heat transfer shells)
13 S3NU
14 S4NU
15 S5NU
16 S6NU
TEMP
Current value of temperature at this integration point (deﬁned only for a mass diﬀu-
sion analysis). Temperature for a heat transfer analysis is passed in as variable SOL.
252
Jessen
==PAGE:264==
H.3. DFLUX SUBROUTINE SCRIPT FOR CASE 1: 3D MODEL
PRESS
Current value of the equivalent pressure stress at this integration point (deﬁned only
for a mass diﬀusion analysis).
SNAME
Surface name for a surface-based ﬂux deﬁnition (JLTYP=0). For a body ﬂux or an
element-based surface ﬂux the surface name is passed in as blank.
H.3
Dﬂux Subroutine Script for Case 1: 3D Model
SUBROUTINE DFLUX(FLUX,SOL,KSTEP,JINC,TIME,NOEL,NPT,COORDS,JLTYP,
1
TEMP,PRESS,SNAME)
INCLUDE ’ABA_PARAM.INC’
C
PARAMETER(PI=3.14159)
DIMENSION COORDS(3),FLUX(2),TIME(2)
CHARACTER*80 SNAME
C
v1=0.005949
C
v1= travel speed
C q1 = heat caused by burner
C rad= radious of the gauss heat distribution
af=0.003
ar=0.012
b=0.003
c=0.002
a=530.0
w=27.3
eff=0.325
Q=a*w*eff
C
x=COORDS(1)
y=COORDS(2)
z=COORDS(3)
C coordinate of the origin of the gaussian heat input
x1=0.0
253
Jessen
==PAGE:265==
H.4. DFLUX SUBROUTINE SCRIPT FOR CASE 1: 2D MODEL
y1=-0.00560686
z1=0.0
d1=v1*TIME(2)
IF(KSTEP.GT.4) THEN
y1=-0.000606553!+0.001
d1=v1*(TIME(2)-377.68)
eff=0.38
v1=0.00592
b=0.004
END IF
C
ff=2*af/(af+ar)
fr=2*ar/(af+ar)
C
C d1= relative burner position at each increment time
JLTYP=1
IF(z-d1.LE.0) THEN
heat_r=6*(3)**(1/2)*Q*fr/(ar*b*c*PI*(PI)**(1/2))
shape_r=exp(-3*(x-x1)**2/c**2-3*(y-y1)**2/b**2
1-3*(z-z1-d1)**2/ar**2)
FLUX(1)=heat_r*shape_r
END IF
IF(z-d1.GT.0) THEN
heat_f=6*(3)**(1/2)*Q*ff/(af*b*c*PI*(PI)**(1/2))
shape_f=exp(-3*(x-x1)**2/c**2-3*(y-y1)**2/b**2
1-3*(z-z1-d1)**2/af**2)
FLUX(1)=heat_f*shape_f
END IF
C
IF(KSTEP.EQ.5 .OR. KSTEP.EQ.9) THEN
FLUX(1)=0
END IF
RETURN
END SUBROUTINE DFLUX

y=COORDS(2)
! y coordinat of considered node
z=COORDS(3)
! z coordinat of considered node
C coordinate of the origin of the heat input [m]
x1=0.0
y1=-0.00833!+0.0004
z1=0.0
C d1= relative burner position at each increment time
d1=v1*(TIME(2)-1)
IF(KSTEP.LE.5) THEN
! 1. pass - only MAG
Q=a*w*eff
! Power input [watt]
C
Ellipsoid heat model
heat=6*(3)**(1/2)*Q/(rl*ra*rv*PI*(PI)**(1/2))
shape=exp(-3*(x-x1)**2/ra**2-3*(y-y1)**2/rv**2
1-3*(z-z1-d1)**2/rl**2)
JLTYP=1
FLUX(1)=heat*shape
! Heat input flux to ABAQUS
ELSE
! 2. pass - LH
v1=1.0/60
! Torch speed
258
Jessen
==PAGE:270==
H.6. DFLUX SUBROUTINE SCRIPT FOR CASE 3
y1=0.0
! vertical starting posistion of MAG
d1=v1*((TIME(2)-0.01)-10002)
! relative burner position at each increment
eff1=0.4
! MAG efficiency
eff2=2.8
! Laser efficiency
Q1=a*w*eff1
! MAG power input [watt]
Q2=8000*eff2
! Laser power input [watt]
C
Goldak heat model
heat1=6*(3)**(1/2)*Q1/(rl*ra*rv*PI*(PI)**(1/2))
shape1=exp(-3*(x-x1)**2/ra**2-3*(y-y1)**2/rv**2
1-3*(z-z1-d1+0.01)**2/rl**2)
y1=0.0
! vertical starting posistion of CIN
C
CIN shape parameters
r0=0.001
s=0.005
r=sqrt((x-x1)**2+(z-z1-d1)**2)
Kz=1.3/s
k=3.0/r0**2
C
Heaviside function
IF((y-y1)-s .GT. 0.0) THEN
u=1.0
ELSE
u=0.0
END IF
C
CIN heat model
heat2=-k*Kz*Q2/(PI*(1-exp(Kz*s)))
shape2=exp(-(k*r**2+Kz*(y1-y)))*(1.0-u)
JLTYP=1
FLUX(1)=heat1*shape1+heat2*shape2
! Heat input flux to ABAQUS
END IF
RETURN
END SUBROUTINE DFLUX
259
Jessen

==PAGE:280==
Appendix J
Use of High Performance
Computing (HPC) at DTU
Compute
In this thesis, DTU Compute’s HPC has been used for Abaqus calculations. The
input ﬁle (.inp) and a job submission ﬁle (.sh) is submitted to a DTU compute server
via WinSCP. The job submission is made through xterm at the remote desktop
application ThinLinc.
The script used to submit an Abaqus job is as follows:
#!/bin/bash
#PBS -N [ANALYSIS NAME]
#PBS -l nodes=1:ppn=[NUMBER OF PROCESSORS]
#PBS -l walltime=[WALLTIME]
#PBS -j oe
#PBS -M [E-MAIL]
#PBS -m abe
#PBS -l feature=XeonE5-2680
cd $PBS_O_WORKDIR
ABAQ="/appl/Abaqus/Commands/abq6131"
module load intel
$ABAQ job=[HEAT JOB] user=[FORTRAN SCRIPT].for cpus=$PBS_NUM_PPN interactive &&
$ABAQ job=[STRESS JOB] cpus=$PBS_NUM_PPN interactive
The user command containing calling the Fortran script can be omitted in case of
no user subroutine.
Useful commands used within the xterm environment:
cd - change directory
ls - list directory
269
==PAGE:281==
qsub jobfile.sh - submit job
qstat - status of job
showq - show job que
qdel job_id - delete job
270
Jessen

Appendix L
Model Veriﬁcation
The model case 1: ”2D Model Using Dﬂux Subroutine”, located in section 11.6 has
been convergence tested with respect to mesh geometry.
Four mesh geometry has been investigated with average mesh size of 2mm, 1mm,
0.5mm and 0.25mm in the weld area, the mesh geometries is illustrated on Figure
L.1, Figure L.2, Figure L.3 and Figure L.4, respectively.
The stress analysis has been performed with both quadratic and linear element,
namely:
Quadratic: CPE8 (quad), CPE6 (tri)
Linear: CPE4 (quad), CPE3 (tri)
Whereas the thermal analysis only have been performed with quadratic element, of
type DC2D8.
The modeling approach i.e. boundary conditions, loads (Dﬂux), steps is made after
the approach outlined in section 11.6 in the main report.
Figure L.1: Mesh geometry with average element size of 2mm in weld area.
287
==PAGE:299==
L.1. RESULTS
Figure L.2: Mesh geometry with average element size of 1mm in weld area.
Figure L.3: Mesh geometry with average element size of 0.5mm in weld area.
Figure L.4: Mesh geometry with average element size of 0.25mm in weld area.
L.1
Results
The transient temperature result is compared with the temperature measurements
from the experiment performed by JLH on Figure L.5 (only peak temperature lo-
cated 14mm from the weld center). It is seen that, the model with mesh density of
2mm give substantial diﬀerent results from the remaining. The reason for this, is
that very few nodes is located within the applied heat source shape, hence the sum
of the applied power is only applied to very few nodes and some heat ﬂux can be
lost at the outer edges of the hear shape, especially when the heat source is moving
over a 2D shell. The remaining mesh densities show good correlation.
The resulting stresses in terms of longitudinal (σ33), transverse (σ11) and through-
thickness (σ22) has been calculated. The values has been extracted from the top

surface, center of specimen and bottom surface as illustrated on Figure L.6, showing
the path deﬁnition for, in this case the 0.25mm model.

L.1.1
Quadratic vs. Linear Elements
A comparison between the performance of the quadratic and linear element has
been made on Figure L.7, Figure L.7, Figure L.7 and Figure L.7 for average element
length of 2mm, 1mm, 0.5mm and 0.25mm, respectively. All the ﬁgure shows the
longitudinal stresses measured along the center path.
By observing the ﬁgures it is seen that the quadratic element and linear element
have the overall same stress distribution. However when observing the near weld
region, where the highest values of stress is present, and hence occurrence of plastic
deformations, a diﬀerent tendency is observed. The quadratic calculations shows
tendency of questionable ﬂuctuations whereas this tendency is not present for the
results of the linear elements. The results of 0.25mm is an exception (Figure L.10).
Here better correlation is observed between the quadratic and linear results.
The reason for this irregularities, for the quadratic elements, is probably that the
quadratic elements, as a consequence of the higher order, are capable of being both
elastic and plastic at the same time. This feature has, in case of large stress vari-
ations within a single element, the tendency of providing enormous predictions as
seen in the present results.
The bottom line of these observations is, that the linear element probably will serve
as a better choice of element for this kind of analysis.
However if it is chosen
to use quadratic elements, the element length in the weld region should be small
290
Jessen

L.1.2
Convergence Analysis - Linear Elements
The longitudinal stresses has been plotted for the center, top surface and bottom
surface is illustrated on Figure L.11, Figure L.12 and Figure L.13, respectively.
The heat input of the ”2mm model” has been adjusted to match the measured
transient temperature history, in order to make the performance of the model com-
parable.
By observing the results from the longetudial stress from the center measurement
on Figure L.11 , is it seen that the models have the same overall shape and general
good correlation. However very close to the center og the weld, the ”0.25mm model”
292
Jessen
==PAGE:304==
L.1. RESULTS
have a bit larger stress compared to the remaining models. The same apply to the
top and bottom measurement of the longitudinal stress, illustrated on Figure L.12
and Figure L.13, respectively. However the ”2mm model” has in general a bit lower
stresses compared to the rest.
In the transverse and through-thickness direction on Figure L.14 and Figure L.15,
respectively, the same general trend is observed.
Based on the observed results, it is concluded that an overall tendency of convergence
is present for the ”1mm model”, ”0.5mm model” and ”0.25mm model”. However
within the near weld region it is noted that the mesh density corresponding to an
average element length of 0.25 mm within the near weld region gives slightly more
detailed predictions. Based on the above and having the linear element properties,
in terms of plastic predictions in mind, it is concluded that linear elements with
mesh density related to an element length of 1mm, 0.5mm and 0.25mm within the
weld region, is suﬃcient for obtaining representative predictions. However the mesh
density related to 2mm, would still yield reasonable predictions.
